Teeth: the map declares its water-facing flanks in `meta.waterward` (compass letters in frame axes - Enokida and Kuwabata both declare `["W", "S"]`, east being the landward shore the village sits on and north being the header-pond corner, deliberately undeclared since its eastern half is landward-ish), and `polder_waterward_flanks_wet` samples ~20 points just outside the dike band's extreme on each declared flank and requires >=70% to land in recorded wet cover (a waterside/toe marsh poly or the header pond); the pre-fix Enokida manifest with the declaration injected is frozen as `pool/regressions/polder_waterward_flanks_dry_on_prefix_enokida.json`. The landward flank is NOT asserted dry - a corner-wrapping fringe legitimately spills toward an undeclared flank, so the declaration only promises wetness where water is claimed. (2) *Dike-top housing, a KNOB for future variety - deliberately unused by every existing map* (GM: "I don't want to modify any of our existing settlements to do it, but I would like this to be a type of settlement that can exist"). `s.dike_top_houses(count, seed, span=...)` seats farmhouses in SINGLE FILE on the perimeter dike crest - the settlement form for an ISLET polder with no landward shore, the deep-water case the Q&A identified (linear dike/canal-bank settlement "taking advantage of the elevated typology," attested from the 8th century; the margin-polder pool maps rightly keep their shore villages instead). `perimeter_dike` now records its `crest` centerline (inner/outer midpoints) for the helper to walk; each house stands on a PLATFORM (a locally widened crest pad, drawn in the dike's own earthwork treatment - piled spoil per the same 挖塘培基 cycle; pad dimensions are a reasoned reconstruction) and is tagged `on_dike` in `M['houses']`; sites within `gap_clear` of a sluice gap are skipped (nobody builds over the notch) and a crowded call self-spaces to single file. No homestead bundle on the crest - the crest is the dooryard/lane, gardens live down on the parcels - and `farmsteads()` now preserves tagged houses through its survivor rebuild. `settlement_form` gains the `dike_top` value (typing rule: needs `terrain="low"` - the form stands on a polder dike), giving the twin-detector a genuinely distinct structural read for free. *Checks:* `structures_clear_of_dike` exempts tagged houses (its real-map and synthetic fixtures still fire on UNtagged ones), and the tag is not a free pass - new check `dike_top_houses_on_the_dike` requires every tagged house's center within 14 px of the recorded band (the platform slack) and fires on a tagged house with no dike at all (fixture `dike_top_houses_on_the_dike_*.json` via make_regressions). Unit coverage: `test_settlement.py::test_dike_top_houses_seats_a_single_file_on_the_crest` (crest record, seating, gap skips, spacing, determinism, rng-stream neutrality, form declaration), `::test_farmsteads_keep_dike_top_houses`, `::test_marsh_waterside_role`.

**The twin-detector** (`check_village.py`: `twin_axes`, `twin_diff_count`, `twin_report`) is a POOL-level tool run alongside - not inside - the per-map gate. For every pair of villages that SHARE a `down_deg`, it counts how many of 8 coarse structural axes fall in different buckets - `cluster_region`, `cluster_shape`, `headman_side`, `lane_skeleton`, `water_source`, `focal_set`, `grain_orient`, `settlement_form` (nucleated / linear / dispersed / water-town - the biggest structural read, defaulting to `nucleated`) - and flags the pair `TWINNED` when fewer than **4** differ (else `PASS`). *Why these choices (research.md D6):* **same-`down_deg` scoping** - villages that already differ by water direction are trivially distinguishable, so comparing them would dilute the signal. **Coarse buckets** (which side / which type / which octant, via `_dir8`), never pixel positions - the axes must answer "a different KIND of place?", not "moved a few px?", so genuine near-variants are not falsely flagged. A `None` axis is "no evidence", never counted as a difference, so a data gap can't manufacture distinctiveness. The **4-of-8 threshold** is the tuning target: high enough that a pair sharing most axes really does read as a copy, low enough that two honestly-different villages clear it. *Worked calibration:* the re-varied **Kikuta** (a LINEAR ribbon along the field margin, drifted field grain) vs **Hoshigaoka** (a NUCLEATED blob with a fengshui crescent pond) differ on `settlement_form`, `cluster_shape`, `headman_side`, `focal_set`, and `grain_orient` = 5 axes → PASS; before the re-vary they differed on 1 → TWINNED. The GM's read confirmed the calibration: the earlier 4-axis PASS (a cross vs a spine skeleton) still "looked similar" because it varied only INTERNAL detail on a shared archetype - the fix was the structural `settlement_form` knob (ribbon vs blob), which is why that axis was added and weighted as the biggest structural read.

### Workflow (spec → validator → persona)

1. Pre-design conversation with the GM (scale, fields, water source, civic features, the residing NPC), grounded in the setting numbers below. **For a town, also settle:** the **holding clan** (sets the monastery dedications - ASK if not given), **walled or unwalled** (border/contested towns are walled), the **elevation slope** if there's a clear gradient (a hill), and any exceptions to the defaults (single monastery, no theater stage, a town that changed hands).
2. Write a thin spec by copying the closest existing one: a **village** from `kikuta.gen.py` (pond, one comb-field, communal windbreak) or `hikari-no-sato.gen.py` (stream, split two-block, per-house groves); a **hamlet** from `moritono.gen.py`; an **unwalled town** from `hoshizora.gen.py`; a **walled town** from `hirameki.gen.py`. Running it writes the `.svg`, the `.json`, **and the `.png`** next to itself - `s.finish()` rasterizes the PNG automatically (via `resvg` at 2600px), so the `.png` can never drift from the `.svg`. Only touch `settlement.py` if you need a genuinely new shared capability.
3. Run `python3 check_village.py <manifest>.json` - the machine gate. It must report ALL CHECKS PASSED before going further. (Running the test suite, `python3 -m pytest`, re-runs every gen and so refreshes every pool `.json` and `.svg` - but the suite sets `DIAGRAM_SKIP_RENDER=1`, so `.png` files only refresh when you run a gen directly. If you ever hand-edit an SVG, re-run the gen rather than calling `resvg` yourself. Renders are gitignored and reach main via the stop-work ritual's render-sync step in CLAUDE.md - the GM browses the complete render set in main's pool dirs.)
4. Read the PNG yourself - the persona pass the gate cannot do (coherent village? legible? balanced?). Per Constitution Principle I, get an independent review if you both built and reviewed.
5. Show the GM. Save the `.svg`, `.png`, `.gen.py`, and `.json` in `pool/`.

### Scale and density (ground in the setting numbers)

Pull from `/gm-assistant/setting/median-domain.md`, `demographics.md`, `village-headsmen.md`:

- Average village: 200-500 people = 40-100 households (median household ~5 over 2-3 generations). Declare `meta(households=N)`; the gate's **`households_consistent`** check requires the OCCUPIED farmhouse count to be **~0.7 per household** (extended families share a roof - the GM's rule: "~70 households ... at least 50 houses"). So ~70 households → ~50 occupied houses, a few larger (the ~5% wealthy-landowner farmers) plus the headman's (largest). **Abandoned houses do not count toward households** (they're former homes).
- **Population is DWELLINGS x ~5, never total buildings** (`population_consistent_with_housing`, town + city). A town/city's **businesses** (`shop`, and the dual-use `merchant` house+shop) and its **civic/government** buildings (ministries, the governor's yamen, flophouses, kura, gate furniture) house no one, so counting them inflates the count. Declare `meta(population=N)`; the gate counts only DWELLINGS times 5, within a tight tolerance of N (default 7%, override with `meta(population_tol=...)`) - so you declare the figure you actually want and the gate verifies the map delivers it, rather than passing a loose band. **What counts as a dwelling differs by scale:** a TOWN counts farmhouses + the residential kinds `laborer`/`servant`/`burakumin`/`samurai`/`merchant` (its depicted farmhouses ARE its partial county farmer cohort). A CITY counts the **urban residential kinds ONLY** - **no farmhouses at all**: a city's declared ~3,000 is its urban castes (budgets.md lists ZERO farmers in the city tables), and the farmers who feed it - whether in the surrounding county villages OR the unusual in-wall agricultural district - are simply not part of that figure. So a 3,000-resident city places ~600 dwellings (Tango does), and its TOTAL building count is *higher* (the shops/civic are extra). City maps depict the full city; town maps depict a representative slice of the ~1,200 county, so their declared `population` is the depicted figure, not the documented 1,200. **Close the count with a deterministic END-OF-GEN TOP-UP, not by re-tuning packs**: every RNG-consuming edit re-rolls all later seeded packs (+/-15 dwellings of jitter), so chasing the floor through pack requests is whack-a-mole. Instead, after `farmsteads()` and the fire towers, sweep a fixed grid over each quarter and `try_building()` into whatever gaps the FINAL layout left, until the caste floors and the dwelling total are met (see `top_up()` in `tango.gen.py`) - it consumes no RNG, sees every obstacle, and adapts when the layout churns (fixed-coordinate hand-adds go stale on every re-roll). Guard it: skip spots near ministries/the yamen (the 14px stand-clear), inside recorded label boxes, and within ~85px of a caravan stables (its open ground).
- **A city's CASTE MIX must match budgets.md, not just the total** (`city_caste_counts_in_band`). The total-population check alone lets the mix silently DRIFT: the classic failure is one quarter's pack (the laborers) absorbing everyone else's slots while **servants get starved to near-zero because they were appended to the END of a pack list** (`["laborer"] * 445 + ["servant"] * 50` places 445 laborers and then has no room left for the servants). A provincial city is roughly **~40% laborer / 20% servant / 25% merchant / 10% samurai / 5% burakumin** of its ~600 households, and each caste is held within **+/-30%** of that target. Two rules for hitting it: (1) **INTERLEAVE servants** into the laborer and merchant packs (`(["laborer"] * 3 + ["servant"]) * N`), since they live among the households they serve - never tack them on the end. (2) The samurai and merchant **walled ESTATES are households too** - the check counts `M["manors"]` (extramural samurai estates, who commute in) toward samurai and `M["merchant_estates"]` toward merchants, so the in-wall *houses* need only make up the remainder (which is why `city_samurai_housing_sufficient` wants ~39 in-wall samurai houses, not the full ~60). Space-bound quarters (the SE samurai quarter is crowded by the government compounds) lean on their estates to reach band.
- **Walled merchant compounds: 1-3 per provincial city, ROLLED 30/40/30, because the gate is a GRANTED privilege (GM 2026-07-23).** A gated compound is not something wealth can buy - it is a right the daimyo explicitly grants to a merchant family, mirroring the Edo-period system of individually granted merchant privileges (an audience with the daimyo at the New Year's celebration, permission to carry a persistent surname across generations despite not being samurai, and the like). So of a provincial city's ~12 very-rich merchant families (budgets.md, 2% of ~3,000), most can AFFORD the wall but lack the legal standing to build it - only 1-3 hold the grant. `s.merchant_estates(seats)` rolls the count per city (`MERCHANT_ESTATE_WEIGHTS`, seeded on the map seed like the torii re-roll; `count=` pins it), places the first n of a hand-vetted seat list (provide >= 3 so any roll lands), records the target in `meta['merchant_estate_roll']`, and `merchant_estates_match_roll` gates drawn == rolled - the pre-roll state (both cities hand-coding exactly 1, a copied pattern with no recorded reasoning) can never silently return. Seat-vetting lessons from wiring Tango (its gen comments carry the details): scan for court-sized pockets against the PRE-estate fixed features only (streets/wall/moat/water, early fixed wells with the |dx|>=39-or-|dy|>=31 wellhead clearance, frontage shops, civic + label ground - pack housing places after the estates and flows around; late well passes yield to the court blocks), keep seats off samurai/burakumin pack ground (caste), and pin-vet every seat with `count=3` against the full gate even when the current seed rolls fewer - a reseed may seat any of them. The engine registers each court in `self.placed` (SAT-distance) as well as `block_polys` (center-test), so pack houses keep real clearance from the compound wall. Towns hand-place (no roll column yet - Hoshizora/Hirameki carry none today); the capital column gets added when capital maps exist.
- To seat ~50 houses you usually need them **two-deep** around the fields, not a single necklace - call `ring()` twice per field, an inner pass (small gap) and an outer pass (larger gap, e.g. ~55). A single ring saturates a field's perimeter around ~40 houses.
- Hamlet (`meta(scale="hamlet")`): 50-100 people = 10-20 households. A hamlet has **no headman of its own** (it falls under the village district's headman - so omit `s.headman(...)`), **no village shrine**, and **no tax-free plot**. The gate enforces this asymmetry: `hamlet_has_no_headman` for hamlets vs `village_has_headman` for villages; tax-free and the orientation checks simply don't apply at hamlet scale. A hamlet typically has just 1-3 small fields.
- Villages and hamlets are peasant-only - no resident samurai (samurai live in the county town).
- **Non-standard size:** for a larger-than-usual village (say 500 people / ~80 houses), declare `meta(target_houses=N)` and the gate's count band follows it (~N ±15%). House count is bounded by field perimeter (houses ring fields at ~56px spacing), so to actually *seat* that many: enlarge the canvas (`Settlement(W=..., H=...)`) and give bigger/more fields, then raise the `ring()` counts. A second concentric house ring is a small library addition if one ring around the fields can't hold them.
- **Town (`meta(scale="town")`):** a county seat, ~1,200 people / ~238 households (per [`budgets.md`](https://github.com/EliAndrewC/l7r/blob/main/setting/budgets.md)). A town is **not** peasant-only: it has ALL castes, and the map must place the **actual documented number of each non-farmer household** (the Town caste table's Families column - one building each), not a token sample. Per `budgets.md`: **~24 merchant houses, ~29 laborer dwellings, ~13 servant households, ~12 burakumin, ~4 samurai families**, plus ~156 farmer households. There is **no peasant headman** at town scale; the state is the magistrate, in a **walled manor** (`s.manor(...)`, walls only - its interior is a separate Mode A diagram). Three modeling conventions adjust what is actually DRAWN (and the gate's target bands): **servants** mostly live inside the samurai/merchant compounds they serve, so only the ~5 "miscellaneous" households stand alone (the rest aren't drawn); **shops** are additional business premises counted separately from the ~24 merchant dwellings (not household-gated); **samurai** show as 5-10 houses with the ~15-strong working platoon barracked inside the manor (not drawn). The gate enforces these counts per caste (`town_caste_count[...]` - which sums the size variants below), farmer plurality (`town_farmers_plurality`), and the magistrate's manor (`town_has_magistrate_manor`). **Merchant and laborer housing varies in SIZE by wealth, exactly like a provincial city's** (`town_merchant_housing_varied`, `town_laborer_housing_varied`): per the `budgets.md` Town wealth tiers a MINORITY of merchants are very-rich/rich and live in large homes (kind `merchant_large`, ~4-5 of ~24) and a few laborers are 'master/rich' (kind `laborer_large`, ~2-3 of ~29), the rest in small/standard dwellings - the town is never one uniform house size. The `_large` footprints are too big for the tight frontage/pack grids, so place them **hand-set in cleared gaps AFTER all the seeded packs** (`s.building(x, y, *s._dims("merchant_large"), "merchant_large")`), which also perturbs no RNG. Mind `town_farmers_plurality`: the farmhouses must stay the largest single group, so the laborer total (small + large) cannot exceed them - Hoshizora caps its `laborer_large` at 2 for this reason. **The residential housing is also BANDED by distance from the road** (`merchant_residences_behind_businesses`, road-fronted towns only - those with a trunk `M["road"]`, e.g. unwalled Hoshizora; a walled town's interior grid is laid around cross-streets, not one radial axis, so the single-axis test is skipped there): a merchant family lives over or directly behind its own shop, so the **merchant residences** (`merchant_house`/`merchant_large`) sit in a tight band **directly behind the storefronts** (no merchant home may fall within the shops' own road-distance band), and the **laborer warren** sits **further back still, with a clear radial gap** between the two bands (the nearest laborer must be >= 35px deeper than the deepest merchant residence). The read is **shops front the road -> merchant homes -> gap -> laborers**, never the two house-types intermixed. Place the hand-set `merchant_large` in cleared gaps just behind the frontage (Hoshizora's sit at road-distance ~106-116px, the laborers begin at ~156px) rather than scattered through the laborer pack. **A home tucked DIRECTLY BEHIND a storefront must also share its ORIENTATION** (`housing_aligned_behind_storefronts`, same road-fronted-town scoping): a merchant family lives over/behind its own shop, so the dwelling lies PARALLEL to the shopfront, never askew - the pair reads as one aligned unit. "Directly behind" is judged in road coordinates (project onto the road): a home at nearly the same position ALONG the road as its nearest shop (in its radial shadow) and one building DEEPER (~15-74px). When the road runs at an angle the storefronts tilt to it, so the homes behind them must tilt to the SAME angle - pass `rot=` to `s.building(...)` set to the local road tangent (Hoshizora's diagonal Imperial Road gives `rot` ~-27deg; the merchant homes match, vs the default `rot=0` which left them visibly askew). Angles are compared mod 180 (a 180deg-flipped footprint is still parallel), and only the home-over-its-shop case is implicated - pack-edge laborers/burakumin that merely pass near a shop (deeper than ~74px, or offset along the road) are not.
- **Provincial city (`meta(scale="city")`):** **~2,000-4,000 inside the walls, average ~3,000 / 600 households** (per `budgets.md`): ~120 servant, ~240 laborer, ~150 merchant, ~30 burakumin, ~60 samurai households - and, structurally, **ZERO in-city farmers** (the farmers who work the city's land live in the surrounding villages/hamlets, counted in other totals). The samurai run ~10% (the governor R8, six provincial ministers R7 + their R6 deputies, R3-5 clerks, bushi) plus a small **foreign** Imperial yoriki sub-station auditing gate tariffs. A city map **places these households densely - near the full ~600** (Tango runs ~600 buildings) - on a crossing street grid, with a smaller building grain (`s.bscale` ~0.5) so the big merchant/samurai footprints fit. The gate checks the required **structures + neighborhood presence + the street network**, not an exact per-caste headcount, but the neighborhoods are sized to the real numbers (~240-310 laborer, ~120-150 merchant, ~50-60 samurai, ~30-60 burakumin). Domain capitals (~12,000) have their own numbers and are a **separate future mode**.
- **Farmland is sampled, non-farmers are not.** We deliberately draw only some of a town's ~156 farmer households (the rest implied by the off-edge field, below), but we draw **all** the non-farmer households at their documented counts - so the map answers "where does each caste live?" faithfully. Farmers must still be the largest single group on the map (`town_farmers_plurality`), so ring the fields deep enough that farmhouses out-number the laborers (the largest non-farmer group, ~29).

### Fields

- Outlines are terrain-following: organic lobes (outgrowths) and bays (indentations) off a semi-rectangular core. Never clean rectangles.
- Interior paddies are a mosaic of bunded basins of **non-uniform size** (vary the cell widths - break the grid), with wavy bunds, not straight. Each cell reads as its crop: flooded rice basins show transplant rows, soybeans grow as rows on the raised bunds (nitrogen-fixing), and ~1 in 6 plots is a dry/hedge crop (furrowed) guarding against blight.
- Crop rows are **regular within a plot but angled differently between plots** - each basin has its own row orientation, never one global east-west direction.
- Field arrangement is village-specific - it might be one contiguous comb-fan field (Hoshigaoka, Kikuta) or a genuinely SPLIT multi-block field (Hikari, two blocks either side of a spur). The number of blocks is TERRAIN-driven. The universal rule: when there ARE two large common fields, do not make them similar size and orientation side by side (vary and stagger); and spread any smaller fields around the settlement.
- Every field is ringed by farmhouses, all field-adjacent. Whether blight leaves **abandoned houses** is village-specific and declared via `meta.fallow_implies_abandoned` (no current pool map carries a blight; the knob remains for a map that wants fallow fields ringed by abandoned houses).
- No paddy on hills (upland rice exists but is not chosen when flat land is available). Hills carry grass, woods, and shrines only.
- Strip-allocation (usufruct, per `village-headsmen.md`): the headman doles non-contiguous strips across the common fields; show via subtle subdivision and the religious figure's tax-free allocation highlighted as non-contiguous **plots** (a vermillion tint + outline - high-contrast against the greens, and thematically the shrine's sacred color; a thin gold outline blends into the tan bunds and was hard to spot). That allocation is law-bounded to ~2 households worth (**2-3 plots**, scattered across the common fields) - gated by `check_village.py`.

### Houses

- Stylized pitched-roof glyphs: two shaded roof planes (darker north slope, lighter sunlit south slope) with a ridge highlight along the long axis, entry on the south face. NOT flat envelope/box icons.
- South-facing convention: ridge runs E-W, facade faces south (historical minka sun orientation).
- **A fireproof *kura* grain storehouse is a WEALTH MARKER on ~30% of plain farms - an ATTACHED annex, never a free-floating shed** (`farm_sheds_attached`; recorded `M['farm_sheds']`, a `_CROP_HARD` feature). It abuts the house wall and is drawn WITH the house in `farmsteads()` so a move-procedure can never strand it (the regression that prompted the check: the kura got detached and drifted into the courtyard). The decision is position-seeded (`_hjit(x,y,3.0) < 0.30`, no RNG ripple) so it is stable across regenerations, and the kura's footprint is RESERVED in the homestead bundle bbox so a neighbor never lands on it. **Which wall:** a DISPERSED farm puts it on the WEST; a NUCLEATED-cluster house puts it on the shaded NORTH (back) wall - the cluster hugs the field to the east so the sunny walls are the garden's, and the north is the one side the garden never takes, so a north kura stays clear of it (`gardens_clear_of_sheds` reads the recorded footprint and guards this). Distinct glyph from the shared draft-animal byre below. The headman's house is the largest and carries no kura.
- Houses never overlap and never sit on a road/lane or have water running through them.

### Water, religion, state markers

- Villages **must** have a water source, but its KIND is village-specific (regional). Historically not every paddy village had a pond: a reservoir **pond** (*tameike*, never "tank") suits a dry/rain-shadow locale (Kikuta); a **stream diversion** from off-map feeding the main field, which then feeds the smaller fields field-to-field, suits a river valley (Hikari). A pond, if used, must be larger than a house, on flat ground, set back from steep hills (erosion). Channels route AROUND buildings (never through the headman's house), take a near-direct path, **wind gently** (not dead straight), and must be **anchored at both ends** - each end starts/ends inside its source (pond / off-map edge / another field) and well inside its target field, so the field paints over the end (a visible connection, not a line stopping in the gap). Anchors are declared per channel as `frm`/`to` in the manifest. Channels are **no-build corridors**: the generator reserves ~33px around each, which is enough to keep a farmhouse's whole **footprint** off it (its half-diagonal is ~26px), not merely its center - and `no_structure_on_channel` gates the rendered map with the *same rotated-rect footprint test the streams use* (a corner clipping a channel fails it, not just a center on it). The earlier center-only guard (`houses_off_corridors`, still kept for the lane) let corners slip through - that is how Hikari once shipped farmhouses sitting on its field-to-field channels.
- **A source brook is DIVERTED into the head-race, it does not run over the field (Ikegami).** A river-valley settlement whose water comes from an off-map **brook** (rather than a pond) taps it at the **field head**: the brook flows in from the map edge, reaches the sluice, and there **BECOMES the irrigation channel** - it hands off to the comb and stops. It must NOT continue on over the paddies, and it must NOT run parallel to the head-race (two overlapping water lines reads as a mistake - the GM's exact catch on the first Ikegami draft). So the brook is recorded as a stream `frm={"kind":"offmap"}` with **no `to`**, ending AT the sluice; the field's water is then the head-race ditch below it, plus an **undrawn hairline anchor channel** `frm={"kind":"stream"}, to={"kind":"field"}` from the sluice winding into the paddy interior (the same undrawn-topology trick as Hoshigaoka's `frm:pond` feed anchor at line ~300 of its gen). That anchor gives `fields_show_water_source` its channel and `field_ditches_reach_source_and_sink` its external source, while the visible water stays the brook + head-race with no overlap. The `stream_runs_off_edge` check accepts this via the **`at_ditch` allowance**: a brook may END within ~22px of an irrigation ditch/channel (diverted into it), not only at the map edge / pond / moat / drain-outfall / field head. Teeth: `test_checks.py::test_stream_diverted_into_a_channel_passes_and_open_ended_brook_fires` (a diverted brook passes; the same brook ending in open ground fires).
- **A DRAINAGE pond sits BELOW the field, the mirror of a source pond (Ikegami, `meta(pond_role="drainage")`).** The usual *tameike* is a SOURCE at the valley head, above the paddies (default `pond_role="source"`). A drainage pond is the opposite: a reservoir at the field's low foot that CATCHES the runoff, so the field's **drain** must actually reach it (`pond_connected_to_field`: a source pond is fed by a channel that touches it; a drainage pond is reached by the drain - this caught the disconnected-drain bug where the drain stopped short of the pond). Either way the pond sits **clear of the field envelope** (`pond_clear_of_field`: no field vertex inside the pond, no pond-rim point inside a field - a pond is a distinct water body beside/below the crop, joined by a channel, never laid over it). The role flips grounding direction in `field_ditches_reach_source_and_sink`: for a drainage pond the pond is a SINK, not a source.
- **Water flows downhill.** Streams and channels follow the land's slope. If a map declares its slope with **`meta(downhill=<dir>)`** (a cardinal name like `"south"`, or a `[dx,dy]` vector in map coords where `+y` is south), every channel must run *with* it - its source (the tap on the stream/pond, `poly[0]`) sits **uphill** of where it feeds the field (`poly[-1]`), gated by `channels_flow_downhill`. So when a north-flowing stream feeds a field to its east, tap the stream **upstream/north** of the field and angle the channel down to it; a channel run flat or angled back uphill would carry the water *away* from the field. Hirameki's hill is in the north, so it sets `downhill="south"`. Maps without the tag are exempt (slope unknown) - tag a map when its elevation gradient is clear (a hill, a falling valley).
- **The religious building scales with the settlement** (gated by `religious_matches_scale`): a **hamlet** has NONE; a **village** has a Shinto **shrine** ("Shrine to <Fortune>" / "village shrine"); a **town** has **monasteries** (each "Monastery of <Fortune>", with a torii in front); a **city** would have full **temples**. Never label a village site a "temple" - temples belong at town scale and up (see the `/temple` skill). If the GM says "temple" for a village, silently correct it to "shrine."
- **A town has TWO monasteries by default**, one for each of the **2 patron fortunes of the clan** whose holdings include it. Declare the clan with `meta(clan="Lion")`; the gate then expects exactly those two dedications (`town_monastery_count`, `town_monasteries_dedicated`). Patron pairs: **Crab** Bishamon+Ebisu, **Crane** Benten+Daikoku, **Dragon** Hotei+Ebisu, **Lion** Bishamon+Daikoku, **Phoenix** Fukurokujin+Hotei, **Scorpion** Benten+Jurojin, **Unicorn** Fukurokujin+Jurojin. **When generating a town, if the GM has not named the clan, ASK** - the dedications can't be chosen without it. Override the default with `meta(monastery_fortunes=[...])` (an explicit list, any length) for special cases: a town that **changed hands** (Hirameki is Lion-held but keeps a small older Benten monastery from Crane rule, so `monastery_fortunes=["Bishamon","Benten"]`), or a quiet town with just **one** monastery (Hoshizora, `monastery_fortunes=["Bishamon"]`). `town_declares_monasteries` requires every town to set `clan` or `monastery_fortunes` - this is also how the data persists across regenerations (it lives in the `.gen.py` source, re-run each pass). The same rule extends to **city temples** at provincial-city / capital scale (see the city-temples bullet below). Mark the principal monastery `primary=True`.
- **City temples: a deliberate L7R liberty on size, monks INSIDE the precinct, and a LAY temple neighborhood (GM, 2026-07-20; the *why* is mandatory, so it is recorded here in full).**
  - **Count - two major complexes, plus a handful of small shrines.** A city defaults to **two major temple complexes**, one per patron fortune of the holding clan (the town-monastery rule ported up; declare via `meta(clan=...)` or `meta(monastery_fortunes=[...])`), dotted with a smattering of small wayside shrines (`s.small_shrine`, gated by `city_temple_neighborhood_has_shrines` >= 3 near the clustered temples). **More than two major temples is the marked exception, with three recognized justifications:** an **especially large** city; an **especially pious** city that is a pilgrimage destination; or a city that **changed hands** between clans and kept the old ruler's temple (the town-tier Hirameki case ported up). Nagahara is the plain two-temple case (Bishamon + Ebisu, the Crab pair). **Tango is the changed-hands case:** it passed between Lion and Crane, so it keeps 3 - the union of the Lion pair Bishamon+Daikoku and the Crane pair Benten+Daikoku (Daikoku shared). Its Lion-legacy **Temple of Bishamon is a converted samurai estate**, which is why it stands among the SE samurai rather than in the SW temple neighborhood - and being a **lone** temple among houses (not a >=2 cluster) it needs no wayside shrines of its own. (The union rule is tidy: a changed-hands city gets 3 temples if the two clans' patron pairs overlap by one fortune, 4 if they share none.)
  - **Size - the deliberate liberty.** Historically the median temple was **tiny**: Edo Japan ran 90,000+ parish temples at 1-2 priests each, and small hereditary Chinese temples held a handful of monks; only the famous pilgrimage complexes ran large (the great Song public monasteries several hundred, Daxiangguo and Shaolin 1,000+). **L7R deliberately over-sizes - every city temple is a major complex** in a way history would usually lack. A **provincial city** (~3,000 inhabitants) has **~15-30 monks per complex** (a serious Ming urban monastery's scale); a **domain capital** (~12,000) has **~50+ monks per complex**. That puts clergy at ~1-2% of city population against the historical ~0.2-0.4% (Song China) - a deliberate **2-5x density**, softened in-world by the temples serving the whole province's worship, not just the walled city.
  - **Initiates - mostly living out.** Each complex carries **~2x its monk count in initiates** ("many are called, few are chosen"), but only a small fraction reside inside (~5-10 per complex at provincial-city scale, ~10-20 at capital scale). The rest are laborers, merchants, and servants engaged in religious study while working ordinary jobs (the "altar boy" tier); on the map they are **ordinary caste-pack houses**, never separate buildings.
  - **Where the clergy live - a BLANK-COURT compound; celibate monks inside, married adepts OUT in the neighborhood (GM 2026-07-24, superseding the earlier draw-no-monk-houses rule).** A city temple draws exactly like the governor's yamen: a walled compound with a deliberately blank court - the interior is the subject of its own Mode A diagram when the PCs visit, so on the city map the big rectangle IS the temple. Celibate monks, resident initiates, and a few lay servants (~25-45 per provincial-city complex) live in dormitories and cloisters **implied within that compound**, **implied population on top of the dwellings-x5 count** - do not house them in the packs. But adepts may marry and raise children, so a share of each complex's 15-30 monks are married adepts whose families keep **2-3 ordinary homes in the temple's neighborhood**: kind `"monk_house"`, drawn DELIBERATELY identical to a laborer house (no label, no glyph of its own - the manifest kind exists so the checks, the budget, and the population math can see them, not the eye). They count as real dwellings (population = dwellings x 5) but ride OUTSIDE the lay caste table's 600 families - clergy are not a lay caste, so the caste bands ignore the kind, and a small budget line (5 x C_PACKED) covers their ground. Gated by `city_temples_have_monk_housing` (>= 2 within ~170px of each major temple) and `city_monk_houses_by_their_temple` (none stranded away from every temple). Beyond those, at most **0-2 exception features** stand apart: a retired abbot's hermitage, or a small daughter-chapel with 2-4 monks of its own - drawn precisely because they are exceptions.
  - **The temple NEIGHBORHOOD is LAY households, and the temple sells its own sacred goods directly.** The crucial correction (GM, 2026-07-20): amulets, charms, talismans, incense, and lamp-oil were **temple-direct sales - a major revenue stream** (Chinese temple accounts book "annual oil-lamp and incense income"; Japanese omamori/ofuda are *bestowed* by the temple for an *offering*, never wholesaled). So there is **NO third-party "religious-goods shop"** competing at the temple's own gate, and none should be drawn. The independent commerce that actually clustered at the gate is:
    - **Food and drink** - teahouses, eateries, sweet-sellers. This is the main draw (worshippers and pilgrims need feeding and resting; the temple does not do that itself) and the bulk of the neighborhood's commerce.
    - **Souvenirs and regional-specialty goods** (*meibutsu*) - local crafts, sweets, keepsakes: **secular** goods branded by the place, distinct from the sacred objects the temple keeps to itself.
    - **Funeral trades**, clustered by the **graveyard** (not the hall) - coffin-makers, grave-marker stonecutters, and (the China-first detail) joss-paper / spirit-money and paper-effigy makers, serving the parish death economy the temple presides over but does not manufacture.
    - **Service trades** - fortune-tellers and diviners at the gate, and the **retained temple-artisan families** (carpenters, gilders, painters) who work *for* the temple on its endless repair cycle and live in the district as workshops-cum-dwellings, not storefronts.
    **At this map scale none of these has a distinct glyph** - a teahouse and an eatery are both the generic `shop` glyph, so **the story is in placement (a shop beside the temple reads as a teahouse), never in the icon**; do not try to draw an "amulet shop" or "religious-goods store". Everything sacred is inside the compound.
  - **Neighborhood SIZE - anchor on ~40-80 lay houses (~1-2 per resident monk).** For a provincial city's two temples (~30-60 monks total), size the lay service band at **~40-80 houses around the temples**, which works out to roughly **1-2 lay service households per resident monk** for a city whose worship traffic is *regional* (not a pilgrimage flood). This is ordinary zoning within the existing caste bands, **not extra population**. A **pilgrimage destination inverts the ratio** to 10:1+ lay-to-monk - the *monzen-machi* / gate-town pattern - which is what the "especially pious city" exception looks like on the ground. (Tango already reads correctly for the opposite reason: its temples sit *in* the merchant district, so the food/drink/souvenir commerce is already all around them. Nagahara does not - its commerce all went to the river/wharf, leaving the temple quarter as bare laborer/servant housing.)
  - **Small shrines can sit at SPECIFIC meaningful locations, not only clustered by the temples.** The `city_temple_neighborhood_has_shrines` floor (>= 3 near the >=2-temple cluster) is a minimum; **additional wayside shrines placed at meaningful spots elsewhere are good flavor** - a **wharf shrine** (fittingly to **Ebisu**, fortune of fishermen and honest commerce) at the docks, a bridge shrine, a gate shrine, a well shrine. Historically wayside shrines stood exactly at such thresholds (a landing, a crossing, a gate), so a shrine placed where the city's life turns reads truer than one more shrine crowded by the halls.
  - **Festival rhythm (China-first, not a drawn feature).** On fair days the great temple's forecourt hosts the periodic market (the *miaohui* / Daxiangguo pattern), the temple renting stall space - a good gameable beat (the temple district *becomes* the market on festival days), though nothing on the static map shows it.
- **(RETIRED 2026-07-24) A town monastery's torii avenue is no longer sized to its approach space** (`monastery_torii_scale_with_space`, removed). The space-driven rule - "roomy approach owes the seven, cramped corner keeps 1-2" - predated the 1/3/7 `TORII_WEIGHTS` table (it still banned a count of 3, which the town column rolls at 60%) and is superseded by the per-temple seeded ROLL: `shrine_hall` rolls every hall's count on its tier's column (pinnable `torii_count=`), records the target on the religious rec, and `torii_match_roll` + `torii_count_canonical` gate it. The COUNT is patronage (rolled); the gen authors the avenue GEOMETRY to fit its ground - see the 'Torii - research + the numerology rule' bullet below. In Hirameki today: Bishamon rolled 3, Benten rolled the full 7 (the old Crane-relic monastery turns out to be the heavily-patronized one - a story the map wears well).
- The main shrine's placement is village-specific: it may stand **on flat ground** reached by a torii approach (Hikari's Benten sits at the southern entry path with a 2-torii gateway; Kikuta's Benten sits in a back-slope sacred precinct reached by a **7-torii approach avenue** - the flat-ground equivalent of a hill ascent) OR sit on a **hill** reached by a torii ascent (if a map uses one). A flat-ground avenue is a straight/gently-curving *sando*: the torii march in a line up to the hall. If on a hill the hall sits ON the summit, never overhanging the edge, with the torii ascent winding across the open face. Torii **counts follow the numerology canon** (`torii_full_avenue_is_seven`, GM 2026-07-21): an approach is either a **modest entrance of 1-2 arches** or a **full avenue of EXACTLY 7** - 7 is the numerologically significant count, and 3-6 reads as an unfinished avenue (Hirameki's Bishamon shipped at 4 and was completed to 7, the motivating example; its pre-fix manifest is the negative fixture). **Monasteries and temples take the full seven whenever there is room** (room is a design-time judgment: Hoshizora's monastery keeps a 1-arch entrance because the theater ground and the Imperial road own its approach; Tango's Daikoku keeps 2 in the dense city fabric). **Village shrines usually keep 1-2** (Hikari 2, Ueda 1); Kikuta's formal 7-torii Benten avenue is the sanctioned showcase, and Hoshigaoka's shrine carries a full 7 because the country monk posted there insists on the potent number (a per-map flavor choice, not the default). Whatever the count, the arches stay clear of the shrine hall and spread out. **The sando packs the arches DENSELY** (~15px / ~30ft pitch at village scale, roughly one arch-width apart): the true-scale torii glyph is small (~8px/16ft, GM 2026-07-21), so a widely-spaced avenue reads as isolated gates rather than a corridor - `torii_spread_out` floors the pitch at one arch-span (16ft) so a *senbon*-style avenue is legal but arches never overlap into a blob. **The INNERMOST arch of a sando sits at the hall's THRESHOLD** (`shrine_avenue_fronts_the_hall`, GM 2026-07-22, village shrines only): the nearest arch stands directly in front of the modest hall, not set out with a gap (Kikuta d=34 from the hall center; Hoshigaoka ~10px below its S edge). It exempts the modest 1-2 arch entrance (not a processional avenue) and the **gateway-BESIDE-the-hall** pattern - the hall aside the entrance track while the arches straddle it (Hikari's Benten, well OFF the avenue axis, so "in front" does not apply) - and it is village-scoped, since a large temple/monastery sando may legitimately keep a courtyard between its outer arch and the main hall. **Torii and religious footprints also keep clear of the wall's works and the patrol ring** (`torii_clear_of_halls_towers_ring`, `religious_clear_of_ring_and_towers`; GM placement rules 2026-07-21, caught on Tango where a wayside shrine seated against the SW wall tower): an arch or a hall overlapping a guard tower, a gate structure, or the ring-road corridor reads as impossible construction. An ordinary street running THROUGH a torii stays legitimate - a monzen sando arch spans its road - so only the ring road is treated as a barred corridor. A recent addition belongs at the village edge/gateway where there is room, not displacing an established structure. Each village district has one tax-free religious figure whose dwelling may double as the shrine.
- The shrine HALL stands BESIDE a lane/road, never ON it (`shrine_halls_clear_of_lanes`, GM 2026-07): a building sits off the way. The **TORII is the deliberate exception** - a gateway arch straddles the approach and the road runs UNDER it (a real, common feature), so torii are exempt from the check. Hikari's Benten shows the pattern: the hall sits just WEST of the entrance track while its two torii straddle the track as the village's southern gateway (the road runs up through the arches). The check uses a **segment-to-rectangle** distance, because a thin lane can thread THROUGH a wide hall between its corners, so corner-sampling (as `houses_clear_of_lanes` uses for small houses) would miss it.
- Fallow field: dry stipple, dashed organic outline (post-blight). Abandoned house: grayed and dashed with a collapse mark, clustered around the afflicted fields. Lanes and irrigation channels are no-build corridors.
- **No legend/key box, no descriptive subtitle, and do not label what is visually evident.** A settlement map should be self-evident: just the place name as a title (no "an average farming village..." subtitle/summary line), and inline labels only for the few non-obvious elements (fallow fields, the tax-free plots, named buildings). Do NOT state counts the reader can see (e.g. "2 torii" / "7 torii") - the torii are drawn. **No two body labels may overlap** (gated by `no_label_overlaps`). **No label may run off the edge of the image** (`labels_within_image`): plenty of things rightly run off the frame - farm fields, roads, samurai country estates, farmhouses, the countryside continuing beyond - but a label that spills past the edge is clipped and unreadable, so every label's bounding box must sit inside the frame. The frame is the cropped **view** (a village crops to its content, a city crops tight to the walls, so the `viewBox` bounds, not the full canvas) or, uncropped, the canvas; the title is placed directly (not in `M["labels"]`) and sits inside by construction. **The title is auto-placed over BLANK space** (`title_clear_of_features`): `s.title(name)` scans the framed window top-first for a text box that clears every feature (buildings, fields, water, groves, the pond) and drops the name there - so the place name never sits on the map content (it records its box in `M["title"]` and the check verifies it). Call it **AFTER `crop_to_content`** so the search runs over the final window. **Every settlement map shows a SCALE BAR** (GM 2026-07-20, matching the Mode A compound sheets): `s.title()` draws it directly under the place name - a 100 map-px bar with Mode A's end-tick/mid-tick furniture, the real distance beneath it, and a fine-print `(1 px = N ft)` line. 100 map-px is a round real distance at every rung of the GM's scale ladder (100 ft at hamlet/town 1 ft/px, 200 ft at village 2 ft/px, 300 ft at provincial city 3 ft/px), so the bar needs no per-scale tuning. The blank-space search and the recorded `M["title"]` bbox cover title + bar together (so `title_clear_of_features` gates the bar's placement too), and `scalebar_matches_declared_scale` gates that the bar exists and its distance agrees with `meta["ftpx"]` - a manifest with a title but no scalebar predates the bar and needs regeneration. **There is NO compass** - the maps are always north-up (drawn convention), so a non-diegetic N/S rose added clutter for no information; it was removed project-wide. The fix for an off-subject label is to move the `s.label(...)` to where the thing it names is actually visible - e.g. Tango's "farmland" label moved from a spot west of the crop onto the visible moat-irrigated south field that runs off the bottom edge. **Label text renders ON TOP of everything** (`labels_render_on_top`): no part of a label may be covered by anything. `s.label(...)` / `s.title(...)` draw into a dedicated **topmost label layer** (`s.add_label`, above even the TOP layer of gate furniture / kido / torii), so the architecture guarantees it; the check guards the invariant by flagging any label whose bounding box is overlapped by a structure drawn over it (a higher draw-z) - the structures it knows the footprint of are the gate guard-houses/towers (`M["gate_structs"]`), the kido ward gates (which now record a `z` + `bbox`), and the torii. (This caught the kido ward gates painting over the "Ministry of Retainers" / "Ministry of Revenue" labels.) **A label must also not sit on a feature it does NOT name** (`labels_clear_of_other_buildings`, **town + city scale**): a label may cover the feature it labels - its own building/compound, or (for a zone/neighborhood label) any building of that cluster - and may clip a street-fronting shop or an interleaved servant house (those line every quarter, so they are never treated as a victim), but where it spills onto a DIFFERENT-identity building it tells the reader that building is something it is not. Each labeled structure (buildings by kind, flophouses, temples, ministries, the governor's yamen, gate furniture, merchant estates, manors) carries a GROUP; the label text declares which group(s) it may overlap (`"flophouse"`->flophouses, `"guard house"`/`"inspection"`->gate furniture, `"Ministry of..."`->ministries, `"Temple of..."`/`"shrine"`->temples, `"samurai..."`->samurai houses + estates, `"graveyard"`/`"cremation"`/`"mausoleum"`/`"ossuary"`->the FUNERARY group (the funerary structures cluster, so a funerary label may cover any of them, but NOT a temple), a `"...street"`/`"...road"` label->the merchant frontage it runs along, etc.; a farmland/market/title label names no building, so it must clip none). Funerary structures (cemeteries, mausoleums, cremation grounds, ossuaries) are themselves victims now, so a temple/monastery label may not sit on a graveyard and vice-versa - the Hoshizora "Monastery of Bishamon" went `label_below=True` and its graveyard `label_above=True` so neither sits on the other (`s.shrine_hall(..., label_below=True)`). Anything else its bbox overlaps fires. The fix is to nudge the label onto its own subject and off the neighbor - move the `s.label(...)`, or pass `label_below=True` to `s.flophouse(...)` so a flophouse jammed against the gate labels south into its cluster instead of up over the guard house. (This caught a "flophouse" label over a merchant estate, a "gate guard house + inspection" label over a flophouse, and the "Temple of Benten" label over a merchant house.) **A finer companion catches the SAME-GROUP case** (`city_civic_label_on_its_own_building`): because `labels_clear_of_other_buildings` lumps every ministry into one `ministry` group, it permits a ministry label to sit on a SIBLING ministry - so a NAMED civic building's label (a ministry by name, the governor's yamen, a named temple) must additionally not overlap any OTHER named civic building. (This caught the "Ministry of Justice" label sitting on the "Ministry of Works" office.) Fix by separating the two offices (which the abut rule below already wants) or moving the label onto its own building. **One caption per named district** (GM 2026-07-21, caught on Nagahara): a neighborhood that already carries its label ("temple neighborhood") never gets a second caption for a sub-pocket of itself - the monzen-machi commerce strip is PART of the temple neighborhood, so a separate "monzen" caption was redundant clutter. Sub-pocket features that need naming are named by their own glyph labels (a specific temple, a graveyard), not by district-tier captions. Rule-of-thumb + review-pass check, not automated Python (same family as don't-label-the-obvious). **Naming convention (not gated, but apply it): reserve "quarter" for a genuine ~1/4-of-the-settlement division** - a CITY has quarters - and call a small TOWN sub-area a **"neighborhood"** (or "enclave"/"row"), never a "quarter": a town's outcaste cluster is a "burakumin neighborhood", its warrior cluster a "samurai neighborhood". (The city, Tango, already uses "neighborhood" throughout; the towns were the offenders.)
- **A building's subtitle must add information, never restate the label or the obvious.** Drop subtitles that repeat the label - a "Monastery of Bishamon" needs no "(town monastery)" note (`religious_subtitle_not_redundant` gates this for religious halls: the subtitle may not contain "shrine"/"monastery"/"temple") - and drop subtitles for the visually evident, like "atop the hill" under a manor that is plainly drawn on a hill. KEEP subtitles that genuinely add context: "(still tended)" on a shrine, "hunting lodge by the Shirin Forest" (what the compound actually is). Do NOT label a manor with the settlement tier ("county seat", "provincial capital") - that names the town, not the building, and is redundant on a town map.

### Towns and walled towns

**The unifying principle for towns: Rokugan's geography is modeled on China, its administrative culture on Japanese samurai society.** That is the *why* behind almost every town decision below - the Chinese **walled county-seat** layout (the wall defines the town; the magistrate's *yamen* on the gate-to-citadel axis; the whole urban population inside; farmland outside), zoned and defended *jokamachi*-style (samurai by the castle, merchants on the streets, outcasts at the edge). When a town choice is unobvious, reach for "what would a Chinese county seat administered by samurai do?" A town spec adds urban vocabulary on top of the field/house machinery:

- **Urban core - businesses front the streets, housing sits back.** The `shop` and `merchant` glyphs are **businesses** (drawn with a striped awning + hanging sign so commerce reads as visually distinct; the awning is TRUE SCALE - a real machiya storefront awning/deep eave is ~3-6 ft, so the strip is 5 ft scaled with the glyph grain and floored at 2.4px for visibility, while the sign is a location MARKER like the well - a real ~2-4 ft kanban would vanish, so it floors at a legible 6x5px and straddles the frontage line instead of jutting into the street; GM 2026-07-23, replacing fixed-pixel decor that read as a ~20 ft awning + 33 ft sign at city grain); `laborer`/`servant` are **housing**. Three gated rules tie them to the street network: businesses must **front a street** (`businesses_front_streets`), every street-fronting building must **face the street it lines** (`buildings_face_street`), and **housing stays off the main street's frontage** (`housing_off_main_street`, set back behind the shops). Place businesses with **`s.frontage(street, items, rows=2)`** - it lines a street with shophouses, each rotated to face it (pass `skip=<full road poly>` when fronting a sub-stretch of a longer road). Fill block interiors with **`s.pack(bbox, items, face_streets=True)`** - each building turns to face its nearest street, and spots that wouldn't front any street are skipped. Place housing with plain `s.pack(...)` in set-back bboxes (>92px from the main street). The **burakumin quarter** is its own pack, segregated at an edge (exempt - not part of the commercial frontage).
- **Streets and town layout (history).** Chinese towns - Rokugan's geographic model - were *planned*, not organic: a main avenue ran from the principal (usually south) gate straight to the **government office (yamen)**, which sat on that axis facing south, with the grid divided into blocks by cross streets. Early (Tang) cities walled those blocks into curfew *wards*; by the Song the wards opened into **shop-lined streets** (merchant shophouses fronting the roadbed), and commerce spilled **outside the gates** along the approach road (the *guan-xiang* suburb). Japan copied the Tang grid for its imperial capitals (Nara/Kyoto) but laid out castle towns (*jokamachi*) as zoned districts - samurai by the castle, merchants in trade-blocks, temples at the edge - with deliberately kinked, defensive streets. **So a town should read as blocks fronting streets, not a scatter of houses.** An unwalled town gets this for free if a road runs through it (Hoshizora's Imperial Road is its spine). For a walled town, draw the streets explicitly (below).
- **Road** - `s.road(pts, label=...)`, an Imperial or trunk road as a spine; a no-build corridor (wide clearance). Like streams, **a road runs OFF both map edges** (`edge_features_run_off_map`).
- **Where a road (or town street) CROSSES water, a BRIDGE carries it over** (`roads_bridge_water`, universal): a road does not simply run through open water. After laying ALL roads/streets and ALL water (streams, irrigation channels, the city moat), call **`s.bridges()`** once - it scans for every place a carried path intersects a watercourse and drops an `s.bridge(...)` (a planked timber deck with side rails, drawn on the TOP layer so it sits above the water and the roadbed) at each crossing, oriented along the road. The classic case is a **walled city's approach road crossing the moat at each gate** (Tango gets two, N and S); a country road crossing a stream gets a timber bridge the same way. The check re-derives the crossings from the polylines and fires if any lacks a bridge within ~40px - so a watercourse added *after* the `s.bridges()` call (leaving a fresh unbridged crossing) is caught; put the call last. A road merely running *alongside* water (never intersecting) needs no bridge and is left alone.
- **Where WATERCOURSES meet, they MERGE like a confluence** (`waterways_merge_at_crossings`, universal) - they must not stack opacity into a dark seam where they cross. Water is semi-transparent, so two overlapping strokes drawn independently double up to a darker patch at the junction (the old bug). The fix mirrors the road crossroads: `s.stream` / `s.channel` / `s.moat` / **`s.pond`** / **`s.field_channel`** (the comb-net irrigation channels) all defer through a shared **`_water`** block that renders, bottom-to-top, **all the pond RIMS (edges - the only water feature with a border), then all the beds (the blue water bodies, one color) inside a single opacity group, then all the lighter sheens in another group above** - so overlapping beds composite as one continuous body of water (an X-crossing or a Y feeder-into-moat reads as a real confluence), and the check enforces it by recording each course's bed/sheen draw position (`bedz` / `sheenz`) and firing if any course's opaque bed is drawn *over* another's sheen at a crossing or feeder junction. Just route every watercourse through those methods (don't hand-draw a water `<path>` with `s.add`) and it holds automatically.
- **Lanes stay OFF the dry crop plots** (`lanes_clear_of_dry_plots`, GM 2026-07-21, caught on Hikari no Sato's west field-spur): a trodden path never crosses row crops - historically it runs on the baulk BETWEEN plots or along the hem's edge. A lane may TOUCH a plot's boundary (paths hug field margins by design; only points >3px inside a plot fire). When a dry-plot staircase blocks the way to the paddy and no clear baulk exists, the correct answer is usually to STOP the field track at the hem's edge (past it, farmers walk the plot baulks) rather than force a route around - Hikari's detour around the staircase foot fought the homestead packing on every seed, while the hem-edge stop kept the known-clean packing untouched.
- **A pond JOINS its feeders at the rim, the same way** (the "`_| |_` not `_|_|_`" rule the streets already follow). The pond is not a standalone ellipse painted over its inflows; it goes through the SAME `_water` block, with its **rim in the bottom EDGE layer (below every bed)** and its **fill in the shared bed group as the TOPMOST bed** (`pond_fill=True`). So a feeder brook's or irrigation channel's bed, sitting in that bed group above the rim, **covers the rim where they meet** - leaving a clean gap in the shore exactly at the mouth, so the water reads as continuous (channel water flowing into pond water) instead of the rim drawing a hard border across the junction; and because the fill is drawn LAST among the beds, it paints over any feeder's small **inside-the-rim overshoot** - an irrigation channel's round end-cap bulging a few px past the rim, which was showing as a darker nub on the open water when the channel was drawn *after* the pond (a feeder drawn *before* it is hidden by the fill anyway). So the open water stays clean whatever the draw order. The other half of the trick is that a feeder must **stop AT the rim, not draw across the open pond**: `_clip_to_pond` snaps a pond-anchored course's endpoint onto the rim - trimming a run that lies *inside* the pond (a channel whose sluice sits within the water) or extending one that sits *just outside* (the sluice foot), for both the bed and the sheen. This is **deferred to flush time** (a course carries a `clip` spec through `_water`), so it works even when the feeder is drawn BEFORE the pond in the gen (the pond geometry, `M["pond"]`, is not yet known at call time). A course is treated as a feeder when its `frm`/`to` anchor has `kind=="pond"` (`_pond_anchored`) - so declaring the anchors (which the connectivity checks already require) also drives the clean join, no extra call. The visible result: on Ueda / Hoshigaoka / Kikuta / Moritono the inflow brook merges seamlessly into the pond and the supply canals branch out of its downhill foot, none of them laying a colored stripe across the open water.  **The LATE-block wrinkle** (GM 2026-07-23, Tango's in-wall tank; gated by `pond_fill_covers_channel_mouths`): the comb ditch nets render in the LATE water block, which draws after the whole shared block - so when a `late=True` channel joins the pond, the shared block's fill-topmost trick cannot cover its mouth, and the head-race's round end-cap rode ON TOP of the open water (its `#6C9CBE` supply color visibly mismatched against the `#9CB4C8` fill), reading as the channel INTERSECTING the pond.  The engine now RELOCATES the pond's fill + sheen into the late block (re-emitted last among the late beds) whenever a late stroke's clipped endpoint lands in the rim zone; the rim EDGE stays early, below every bed, so the mouth still covers it - the covering order of the shared block, reproduced one block later.  The check reads two new manifest records: `M["drawn_channels"]` (every `field_channel` stroke's post-clip geometry + `bedz` + `late` flag) and `M["pond_layer"]` (the fill's `bedz` + which block it landed in).  `bedz` values are offsets within their OWN splice block, NOT globally comparable (a late bed's raw z can be numerically smaller than an early fill's while drawing after it - the broken-engine shape the fixture pins), so draw order across blocks is the `(late, bedz)` pair compared lexicographically.  A `channels` record with no `bedz` is a gen-side topology entry (its visible stroke lives in `drawn_channels`) and is exempt, as are `drawn: false` underground conduits; a joining stroke that runs THROUGH the open water mid-run (interior vertex deep inside the rim) fires the same check - mouths, not crossings, the pond sibling of `channels_join_water_not_cross`.
- **We do not draw all the FAR farmland, but the NEAR ring reads packed.** A town's *paddy fans* are a representative sample - the far countryside is implied off-map, so **at least one field must run OFF the map edge** (`town_has_field_off_edge`) - a partial field at the margin signals "more farmland beyond." Off-edge fields are exempt from the ringing and irrigation checks (their houses/water are off-map too). But the *near ring* - the flat, waterable ground the frame actually shows - is not sampled-away to bare scrub: it is filled with cultivation (see "Near-ring farmland density" below). The old reading (a few paddy clusters in a sea of scrub right up to the gate) inverted the real land-use gradient.
- **NEAR-RING PADDY IS COMB FIELDS ONLY (GM 2026-07-23, superseding features 013 and 014's fillers).** The farmland around a town or city is drawn exclusively with the SAME real-irrigation machinery as every village: `build_comb` fans - a sluice tapped on a real source, a head-race forking into supply canals, flooded plots cascading downhill, a dry hem quilt on the upslope margin, a drain, and farmhouses ringed alongside. The near ring is the settlement embedded in these fans; open ground between them reads as pasture/commons and the head of paddy country continuing beyond the frame (coverage need NOT be total - "the presumption is that there's more we don't see").  Two fillers are **REJECTED and recorded (Constitution XII, so they are never reinvented)**:
  1. **The 013 dry/garden tile quilt (`near_ring_cropland`)** - packed the ring with channel-free dry-crop rectangles because dry cropland is exempt from `fields_show_water_source` (the cheap fill). GM verdict: "tons and tons of dry crop fields everywhere" on what should be wet-rice country. Historically backwards: the town sits in the basin BECAUSE of the rice.
  2. **The 014 gridded paddy basins (`near_ring_paddy`)** - discrete square basins with per-basin squiggle intakes off the moat. Plumbed, but visually nothing like a real paddy: "irregular channels feeding ~16 rectangular-ish plots... don't look like any rice paddy in any village or hamlet." A rice paddy is an irrigation SYSTEM (source -> canals -> plots -> drain), not a colored rectangle with a hose to it.
  Both generator methods remain in `settlement.py` for the historical record but no pool map calls them.
- **The frame is a CONTENT crop (`s.crop_city(margin, west=..., ...)`, GM 2026-07-23 second pass), sized to show the fans without wasted space.** History: the pre-rollout crop (MARGIN ~96px, "a city map is about the city") cut every comb fan to a ~46px sliver - the fans were always there, just invisible, which is why the ring read as bare/dry through two features of filler work. An interim hand-widened frame (~250-260px past the moat) proved the fans; the settled form is the content crop: the frame hugs the moat ring + every KEPT satellite (gate markets, flophouses, funerary grounds, wharf stalls, placed labels) + `margin` - **and the AGGRESSIVE margin (35px, the `crop_city` default) is the standing default for ALL cities** (GM 2026-07-23: "the aggressive crop [is] the default for all cities unless I state otherwise"); a new city gen calls `s.crop_city()` bare plus one farm-band side override, while the fans, hems, farmsteads, and estates do NOT set the frame - they CLIP at the edge ("it's okay if farms get cut off... the point is to show they're there, not to show the entire field"). Per-side margin overrides keep a representative farm band on a flank with no satellite anchor. Consequences to mind when editing a city gen: features parked "at the frame edge" under a hand frame (funerary grounds, drain tails) become FRAME DRIVERS or in-frame danglers under the content crop - site funerary just past the fields, and run drains off well beyond any plausible frame; the title placard searches the framed window and treats placed LABELS **and the wall/moat/ring-road/through-road** as obstacles (title_clear_of_features gates the label half; with no blank corner left after an aggressive crop, the placard had landed straddling the rampart). And **a label never pins the frame** (GM 2026-07-23): if a feature label is the only thing holding a side open, RELOCATE the label inward (Tango's "samurai estates" label moved in among its manors) rather than padding the crop around it. Placement lesson for new fans: a comb's fall must run ALONG the visible band or off-frame from a near-rim sluice - plots start ~120px downhill of the tap, so an outward fall from a tight frame vanishes entirely; and `build_comb`'s chirality (canal B swings 58 deg CCW of the fall, canal A 42 deg CW) rules out some falls near a curved moat rim - both hands were tested and rejected for a south fall on Tango's east rim before settling on the east fall.
- **Water sources for city fans - three honest patterns.** (1) **Moat-fed** (`{"kind": "moat"}` channel anchor): the sluice a stride outside the rim, tap picked UPSTREAM of the sluice when the moat has a current (`moat_channels_flow_with_current`). (2) **Off-map source, moat as storm drain** (`{"kind": "offmap"}` source + `{"kind": "drain"}->{"kind": "moat"}`): a north-band fan falling toward the city whose collector EMPTIES INTO the moat (Tango fn1/fn2, Nagahara fnn2). (3) **Tail-water reuse** (`{"kind": "stream"}` or `{"kind": "river"}` on the OUTFALL): fans below the city tap the moat's outfall stream - a city's drainage watered the fields downstream, the historically standard arrangement (Tango fse1/fs3); Nagahara's far-bank fan taps the Hayakawa directly (the `"river"` channel-anchor kind, added for it). Per-comb `dry_keepout` circles hold a fan's hem quilt off estates and other structures placed before it. **Mouths are CONFLUENCES, never crossings** (GM 2026-07-23: "they just run into the moat... and aren't intersecting at the edge"): a drawn tap/culvert ends at the receiving water's BANK (the engine's `_clip_to_moat`/`_clip_to_river` trim to the bed edge MINUS the stroke's cap radius - a round linecap inks past the endpoint, which is how a 7px tap once plunged to mid-moat), and it wears the receiving water's OWN color (`#9CB4C8` - the tap carries moat/river water; the color change to the comb's canal palette happens at the sluice, where a transition reads naturally). The recorded topology still ends ON the centerline for the anchor checks; `channels_join_water_not_cross` is the ratchet - no channel/ditch segment may strictly cross a moat/river centerline mid-run. **And every water-to-water HANDOFF shows its control gate** (`s.sluice_gate(x, y, rot=heading+90)`, gated by `channel_gates_at_water_junctions`): where the water-colored tap hands off to the comb's own canal (the sluice - the palette seam sits exactly there) and where a drawn drain hands off to its outfall culvert, a small timber board-gate glyph marks the junction - the control structure the "sluice-fed head-race" doctrine always implied but no map drew; it is what makes the color/direction change read as engineered plumbing rather than two strokes crossing (GM 2026-07-23, the junction-seams pass). An UNDROWN drain record (`drawn: false`) is an implied underground conduit (Tango's in-wall nw1 drain beneath the rampart) - no visible seam at the MOAT side, no gate there.  **An IN-WALL drain cuts off at a gate before the ring road** (`s.inwall_drain_outfall(drain_pts, moat_bias)`, gated by `inwall_drains_gated_at_cutoff`; GM 2026-07-23, generalizing the nw1 handoff into a rule for ANY drainage ditch inside a city wall): the visible runoff ditch never reaches road, rampart or moat - it is TRIMMED back to half the ring-road width + 10px clear of the ring-road centerline and drops through a **sluice-gate glyph at the cut end** into the underground stone culvert (the undrawn conduit records the drain->moat topology; the culvert's connection to the moat is real but invisible, and the CONTROL GATE at the drop is the one visible piece of the plumbing - without it the trimmed ditch reads as unfinished linework, which is exactly what the pre-fix Tango showed: the ditch lapped 3px past the patrol road's centerline and simply stopped, bare).  The helper trims the drain polyline BEFORE it is drawn or recorded (assign the return value back onto the net's drain channel - `comb_field(..., inwall_drain_bias=...)` in the Tango gen, `draw_comb_field(..., inwall_drain_moat_bias=...)` on the shared path), so the drawn ditch, the `field_ditches` record, and the conduit all share the same cut geometry - placement and check reading the same source, per the standing engine rule.  `moat_bias` aims the conduit's far end: the moat vertex nearest cut+bias (nw1 uses (-40, 30), W-SW, so the implied culvert runs downhill with the moat's current).  The check: every undrawn drain->moat conduit starting inside the wall needs a drawn stroke ending at its start (within 8px), the cut clear of the ring-road centerline by half-width + 4px with no touching stroke crossing it, and a sluice_gate within 16px (fixture `inwall_drains_gated_at_cutoff_fires_on_tangos_bare_outfall`).
- **PADDY-FIRST samurai estates.** The fans claim the near ring BEFORE any country estate is placed; the gentry keep only the leftover pockets - most estates sit farther out in the rural district, so a city map showing as few as ONE estate (even a fraction running off the frame edge, its land continuing beyond) is the historically accurate signal (`city_estates_multiple_shown` floor is now >= 1, was >= 2). Estates still cluster on the capital-facing side (`city_estates_toward_capital`, BOTH half-planes of a diagonal), stay dispersed (>= 200px), and vary in size - Tango seats 3 in its SE leftovers (one cut by the frame edge), Nagahara 3 on the far-bank strip between the river fan and the wharf road. **Estate DRIVEWAYS are not drawn** (GM: the long worn lanes read as fat roads across the countryside; the check doctrine already held that approach lanes are "not drawn at this scale") - the manors stand as clean walled compounds, approaches implied off-map.
- **Check calibrations that encode the doctrine** (all in `check_village.py` with dated WHY comments): `near_ring_cultivated_fraction` samples a walled city's ring within ~800 real ft of the rampart **in CANVAS space** (fully frame-independent both ways: an uncapped sampler re-defines "near ring" as "all visible countryside" when the frame widens, and a view-clipped sampler shifts the fraction when the frame TIGHTENS - both caught 2026-07-23); its floors are re-anchored to combs-only references (city dense 0.12 vs bare pre-013 Tango ~7%; town dense 0.28 vs five-fan Hirameki ~31% - the old 0.45 town floor was calibrated WITH the rejected quilt and would force it back). `near_ring_paddy_dominant` keeps the paddy teeth, with a comb's dry hem excluded via the field bbox EXPANDED by the dry_band (~135px - the hem quilt rings the envelope OUTSIDE its bbox at the head/flanks). The near-ring research figures that informed the look (a dense wet-rice near-city plain is ~70-75% paddy with the rest bunds/ditches/lanes/farmsteads; ~1.5-2 farming households per hectare of paddy; a mid-season district shows a MAJORITY of plots holding water) are recorded with the 2026-07-23 session notes for future calibration.
- **Every town has a THEATER STAGE** (`s.theater_stage(cx, cy, w, h, label=...)`; `town_has_theater_stage`) unless `meta(theater_stage=False)` - a roofed performance stage facing an open viewing ground (the East Asian troupe/festival venue: a temple OPERA STAGE / shrine NOH-kagura stage, NOT a Greco-Roman seating bowl). It **belongs to a temple/monastery precinct**: it must sit ADJACENT to a religious hall (`theater_stage_by_temple`, center within ~260px of the nearest one) AND its viewing ground must **OPEN TOWARD that hall, not away** (`theater_stage_faces_temple`) - the stage faces the hall, the audience gathered between. Orient it with `rot`: the glyph's open side is local +y, so `rot=0` opens the ground **south**, `180` **north**, `90` **west**, `270`/`-90` **east** - pick the one that points the ground at the temple (Hoshizora's monastery is north of its stage, so `rot=180`; Hirameki's stage sits north of Benten facing south, `rot=0`; Tango's likewise). For a **walled** town it sits INSIDE the walls (`theater_stage_inside_wall`) unless `meta(theater_stage="outside")`.
- **The town granary is NOT a separate building by default - it lives inside the magistrate's compound (the yamen).** A county seat collects and forwards tax rice, so it has substantial grain storage, but at this scale that store sits within the administrative compound (both the Chinese ever-normal granary and the Japanese domain rice storehouse were kept at the seat of government), which on the map IS the magistrate's manor. So treat the granary as **implied by the manor** and do not draw a standalone one. The exception is a **rice-TRANSIT hub** - a town at a road/river junction where grain from many counties is gathered and forwarded up the kick-up chain - which historically grew a distinct fortified storehouse district. For that case only, set `meta(granary=True)` and draw a row of fireproof *kura* with `s.granary(cx, cy, n=...)` near the gate or transport route; `town_has_granary` then requires the granary to be present. The flag is **opt-in** (the inverse of the gate market / theater stage / monastery defaults): absent it, no granary is expected or drawn.
- **Several merchant houses keep an attached storehouse (*kura*).** Because most Rokugani farmers are **tenants**, their (often absentee) landlords' rent-rice and bulk goods are stored in town - over and above the ordinary inventory storeroom a shop already has. So a noticeable **minority** of businesses run the classic narrow-front / deep-lot merchant compound with a fireproof *kura* behind the shopfront. Call `s.merchant_storehouses(count=6)` AFTER placing the businesses: it tucks a small kura behind several merchants (the **back**, opposite the street-facing awning), drawn as an annex like the farmhouse `shed`, so it needs no open ground in the maximally-packed quarter (there is none). `town_has_merchant_storehouses` requires **>= 3** - more than a token one or two, but still a minority.
- **Draft animals live in the farmhouse, not a barn; rural settlements have no hay barns.** East Asian wet-rice farming is not herd-based, so there are no European-style barns. The peasant farmhouse - specifically its *doma* (earthen-floored work bay) - stalls the draft ox or horse **under the same roof**, including for winter, so the farmhouse glyph (with its optional attached `shed` storeroom) already implies both the storeroom and the animal housing. Do **NOT** add barns to a village or hamlet. Barns + grazing pasture (`s.pasture(...)`, `s.building(..., "barn")`) appear ONLY for a specific reason: a **road / post town's horse-relay infrastructure** (Hoshizora, on the Imperial Road - remounts + hayfields to feed them) or a **horse-breeding domain** (Unicorn / Moto). Town **stables** are likewise not drawn separately - warhorses are inside the magistrate's manor, travelers' mounts at the (unlabelled) inns.
- **A town has a market-day flophouse** (`s.flophouse(cx, cy)`; `town_has_flophouse`, default-on). A county seat exists partly *as* the periodic-market center for its hinterland: most peasants come from within a day's round-trip, but those at the far edge of the catchment stay over on market eve in cheap communal lodging - a *kichin-yado* ("firewood-fee inn"), where you sleep on straw under a roof for a sen a night. It is a deliberately **large, plain, barn-like dormitory** (no awning, a long row of plain doorways), distinct from a shophouse, because its whole value is volume at rock-bottom price - it beds dozens on market eve. Put it where travelers ARRIVE: the **gate market** of a walled town (outside the wall - the gate shuts at dusk, so a peasant arriving late literally cannot get in and sleeps outside until dawn), or **along the road** of an unwalled one. Default is one; `meta(flophouses=N)` requires more for a busy hub, `meta(flophouses=0)` opts out.
- **A town has ONE caravan inn + stables** (`s.inn(cx, cy)` + `s.stables(cx, cy)`; `town_has_caravan_inn`, always-on). A county seat sits on the trade route, so - exactly like a provincial city's gate caravan facilities, but a single one instead of several - it needs a prominent two-story **inn** (lodging the wagon-train merchants, drivers and guards) with a large **stables** next to it (within ~150px) and **OPEN GROUND beside the stables** - a pasture for the oxen and horses (the check fails if >4 dwellings crowd within ~75px of the stables). The inn must also sit **ALONG the road - FRONTING the Imperial road (unwalled) or a town street (walled), with nothing between it and the roadbed** - so the caravans pull up to it, not hunt for it behind the shop rows (the `_fronts_route` test: within ~115px of a route and no building blocking the line to it). An **unwalled** town sites it along the road where caravans pass (Hoshizora uses the quiet SW approach); a **walled** town keeps the inn + stables INSIDE the rampart, fronting the main street (caravans enter through the gate). The inn must also **FACE the road and lie PARALLEL to it** (`inn_faces_the_road`): pass `s.inn(x, y, rot=...)` so the noren front faces the nearest roadbed point and the long edge runs along the road - a diagonal road needs a tilted inn (Hoshizora's is `rot=150`, parallel to the -30deg SW road; Hirameki's is `rot=90` to face the vertical main street). `s.stables(...)` takes a `rot` too if you want it to match, but only the inn's facing is gated. Place it (fixed coords, no RNG) after the seeded packs so it perturbs nothing.
