Unlike the house/yard - drawn oversized against the fields for legibility - a dooryard garden at **1 px = 2 ft is near its TRUE size**, so its area is a real quantity we can pin to history: the *saien* is the small INTENSIVE daily-greens bed by the kitchen (*daikon*, onions, greens, herbs); the household's bulk vegetable and dry-crop growing happened out in the **hatake dry fields**, not here. Historically that dooryard bed ran **a few *tsubo* up to ~1.4 *se*, i.e. ~10-140 m²** (1 *tsubo* = 3.31 m²; 1 *se* = 30 *tsubo* ≈ 99 m²). The check sums ALL of a household's beds (a fragmented plot is still one household's garden) and requires the TOTAL in that band - bigger reads as a field, tinier as no garden at all. (China-first per the core principle: rice-south Chinese farmhouse dooryard vegetable plots were the same small intensive kitchen beds; the bulk growing was likewise out in the household's fields.) The generator lands ~35-115 m² per household, centered ~55 m² (~1/2 *se*), comfortably inside.
  - *Shape - an irregular hand-worked quad, not a surveyed rectangle (`garden_plots_are_quads`).* Real dooryard beds were bent to paths, walls, and awkward soil, so the garden is drawn as a slightly-irregular **4-sided polygon** - each corner pulled INWARD from the reserved rect by a small position-seeded fraction, losing the perfect 90° corners while staying **entirely inside** its reserved w×h (so the shape can never create an overlap the rect-based placement/checks already cleared). The manifest records the `poly`; the check validates it is a non-degenerate inscribed 4-gon. (A swept threshing yard gets the SAME treatment but LESS jitter - a work surface stays near-square.)
  - *Occasionally FRAGMENTED into two beds, in THREE arrangements (`_garden_beds`, nucleated only).* Sometimes the land just works out that way - you garden the good topsoil where it lies rather than dig up a rocky patch when there is better ground a few feet over - so ~1 in 4 households (position-seeded), where each bed still reads as a real garden, shows TWO beds. WHERE the two beds sit varies: ~half the splits **FLANK the house on OPPOSITE walls** (a bed on the E and one on the W, or one in each south corner - the workable ground fell on both sides); the rest share ONE side, either **SIDE-BY-SIDE** or - for a south garden, where the upper bed stays sunny - **STACKED above/below**. It is the larger (well-off / headman) plots that fragment (each half must stay ~12 ft wide), which is also where a household works enough ground to bother. **Crucially the beds are decided in `_bundle_geom` and returned in `geom["gardens"]`, so they are RESERVED and collision-checked as part of the whole bundle** - an opposite-side bed grows the bundle bbox and the packer spaces the homestead out for it, so a flanking bed can never overlap a neighbor or a paddy (this is why the split is nucleated-only: a dispersed farm's W wall carries its windbreak grove, and its E-only garden has no clean opposite side). Every bed sits on a sunny band (never the north back), and all beds of one house carry the same parent - so `gardens_present` still counts one garden per house and the AREA band sums them. (Gardens can therefore outnumber houses in the manifest; that is expected.)
- **Storehouses / sheds are a MINORITY (~30%), not universal** (drives the `shed` flag rate, deliberately left unchecked - a visual flourish - and kept a minority).
  - A fireproof **kura** (土蔵) was an expensive **wealth marker** - only well-off farmers and landlords had one. A simpler **naya** (納屋) / **koya** (小屋) outbuilding was more common but still not on every plot: many households stored inside the *doma* (the farmhouse's own earth-floored work room), and the landless *mizunomi* had little to store. So a separate storehouse is a *portion* of farms (~30%), independent of the threshing yard (a farm may have neither, either, or both).
- **Settlement form: NUCLEATED cluster (the DEFAULT) vs DISPERSED ring (drives `s._nucleated`; researched + source-verified 2026-07, this is the record).** WHICH is more common: **nucleated clustered villages predominated in China** (Knapp, *Chinese Landscapes: The Village as Place*) - houses grouped together, "planted like islands among the fields," worked outward. The regional split is SIZE, not form: North China = large compact villages (1,000+ households); **the South-China rice regions** (Middle-Lower Yangzi - our analog, since Rokugani density anchors to Song/Ming rice China) = **small nucleated villages of ~30-60 households** (rice's intensive labor keeps farmers clustered by their paddies; 200-500 villages per 100 km2). **Dispersed** scattered farmsteads appear only in **mountainous/hilly terrain** (Sichuan-type, where fragmented arable forces homesteads apart). The Japan tiebreaker agrees (wet-rice Japan = *shuson*, nucleated). So the **default is NUCLEATED**; dispersed is the hill/upland TERRAIN mode (and dovetails with the multi-block-fields note - broken ground gives both scattered farmsteads AND several field patches). The ~30-60 rice-village band validates Hoshigaoka's ~70 households (a healthy single rice village, just above the band).
  - *The consequence for groves.* A nucleated cluster has **NO per-house windbreak grove** - the houses stand close and shelter each other, and a tight cluster of grove-bundles literally cannot nucleate (each ~6x-house grove-bundle forces the farms to RING the field, i.e. the dispersed pattern). Per the grove bullet below (and Knapp), the nucleated windbreak is instead a **village-EDGE belt** on the windward side (a *fengshui*/shrine grove), placed as one feature - NOT one yashikirin per house. So `_nucleated` swaps the homestead bundle: house + south threshing yard + a dooryard garden on an **adaptive sunny side** (SE/SW tucked beside the yard first - sunny and tight - then the E/W walls), the side chosen by the placer for **fit + no shading** (a garden with a farmhouse close to its SOUTH is shaded, so the placer avoids sandwiching it between houses; this also varies the gardens instead of all-east). The compact grove-less bundle lets the cluster pack into a real nucleus; the placer hugs each homestead to the paddy edge then packs it against its neighbors.
  - *DISPERSED (the shipped ring-village bundle) is unchanged* - house + windward L-grove (*yashikirin*) + yard + east garden, spaced so all fit; it is the terrain mode for upland/fragmented country. The existing ring villages (Hikari, Kikuta) stay dispersed; the water-first base case (Hoshigaoka) sets `s._nucleated=True`. The **windward village-edge belt** for nucleated clusters is a deferred TODO (the cluster currently renders grove-less pending it).
  - *Headman = just a LARGER plain farmhouse (tunable size), NOT its own logic.* Farmhouses are individually sizeable: `try_place(x, y, kind, role, size=(w,h))` overrides the footprint (base px). In nucleated mode `s.headman()` is a thin wrapper - it places a **plain** farmhouse of **~46x28 px** (~92x56 ft at 1px=2ft) through the STANDARD collision-checked bundle path, `role="headman"`. So it gets its (capped) yard + garden like any farm, packs without overlapping a neighbor, and carries no special reservation or drawing. It is the grandest house (clearly larger than a plain 23x14 or well-off ~26x16 farmhouse; the old 108x68 ~216x136 ft was a pre-scale fortress; `headman_is_largest` holds) purely by SIZE. The yard/garden dims are **capped** in `_bundle_geom` (yard ~34x22, garden ~22x17) so they read as an ordinary farm's beside his grand house (span ~the adjacent wall but shallower/narrower); plain houses are well under the cap, so they are unaffected. NOTE the `"big"` kind's glyph draws a small storeroom WING to its north that is NOT in the reserved footprint (it overlapped a neighbor's yard when used for the headman) - so the nucleated headman uses `"plain"`, not `"big"`.
  - *The cluster HUGS the paddy (algorithmic, not per-map seed tuning).* Villages sat as "islands among the fields," abutting their paddies - not floating with a bare buffer. So the nucleated placer, after shoving each homestead toward the nearest paddy edge, packs it against its neighbors with a **field-distance-monotone** constraint (`_slide_nuc(keep_field=True)`): a neighbor-pack move is rejected if it would drift the bundle FURTHER from the paddy than where it started. The cluster therefore glues its field side to the paddy and builds outward in rows (a blob anchored on the field), regardless of where the seed disk is centered - the fix lives in the ALGORITHM, so a map need only say roughly where the village sits, not hand-tune it onto the field edge.
  - *Lanes lace the cluster, and a spur bridges it to the fields.* A nucleated village is threaded with lanes. Lay them with `s.lane(pts)` **BEFORE** the houses - a lane lays a no-build corridor, so the homesteads FRONT it (place lanes after the water/fields, before `try_place`). The pattern: a **main spine** through the cluster (the headman fronts its head) plus a **spur running out to the paddy edge** - the spur is the village-to-field transition the bare buffer should carry (its field end is computed from the field geometry via `_nearest_field_point`, not hand-placed). Remaining house-to-field bareness is later filled by near-homestead hatake (a deferred layer). **Run the spine along the cluster's FIELD-FAR side** (not down the middle) when the field is on one flank: the placer hugs houses TOWARD the field, so a mid-cluster spine catches the houses it pulls across it (`houses_clear_of_lanes`); a spine on the far side lets them pack cleanly between it and the paddy.
  - *Name-informed siting: read the village NAME and let it place the settlement (GM, 2026-07, a GENERAL rule).* A Rokugani place name often encodes the settlement's position relative to its fields, water, or terrain - so before seeding the cluster, ask what the name SAYS and honor it. Worked example: **Ueda (上田, "upper paddy")** - the village's paddies are the UPPER (upslope) ones, so the dwellings sit DOWNSLOPE of them, facing up-valley toward the fields; on Ueda's NE-high slope that puts the cluster on the LOWER (SW) flank with the bulk of the paddy rising above it (NOT the default "village on the high flank"). Other cues to watch for and site to: *shimo-* (下, lower) -> the opposite; *kawa-/-gawa* (river) -> hard by the watercourse; *yama-/-take* (mountain), *saka* (slope), *hara* (plain/moor), *tani/-dani* (valley floor), *sawa* (marshy stream), *hama* (shore), *guchi* (a mouth/entrance/pass). When the name is neutral, fall back to the default (nucleated village on the dry high flank hugging its paddy). The point: the map should be able to be READ back into the name.
- **Village lanes and connecting paths - UNPAVED (or narrow-paved) NARROW single track (researched + source-verified 2026-07; CHINA-first per the core principle, Japan corroborating).** The conclusion is the SAME in both cultures - a village lane is a **NARROW single track, not a wide 2-lane road** - so the "dashed-centerline 2-lane" reading was wrong either way. The reasons, China leading:
  - **CHINA (the guiding star):** the Chinese deliberately built a **network of NARROW PATHS for the WHEELBARROW** (the characteristic Chinese one-wheel vehicle, which carries 3-6x a European barrow and needs only a footpath), plus **carrying-pole porters, packhorses, and litters** - NOT wide cart roads. Two-wheeled **cart-tracks predominated only in the flat NORTHERN/eastern plains**, which is NOT our rice-south analog. So the vehicle to picture is the **wheelbarrow and the shoulder-pole porter**, and the track is one-wheelbarrow / one-packhorse wide. *Paving nuance (a real China/Japan difference):* China had MANY narrow **stone-slab / flagged** paths, especially in the wet rice south (a paved causeway keeps porters and barrows above the mud) - MORE paving than Japan, but still **narrow foot/wheelbarrow paths, never wide cart roads**. A modest village's internal lanes are earth; an important inter-village or pilgrimage path may be a narrow stone causeway (an available upgrade, `worn=True` earth is the humble default).
  - **WATER as the freight highway - true for NATURAL water, but ARTIFICIAL canals are a Rokugan EXCEPTION (GM canon).** In Ming China bulk goods (above all tribute rice) moved by **canal and river boat**, and the man-made Grand Canal + dense artificial canal net were central. **Rokugan deliberately DIVERGES here:** artificial transport canals are NOT Empire-wide - they are a **LION-lands feature** (the Lion deliberately embrace the Chinese precedent), while the other clans rely on NATURAL water or land and simply do not need canals: **Crane** lands are coastal (never far from the ocean), **Dragon** has the Drowned Merchant River, **Unicorn** has the Firefly River valley and is intensely horse-focused, **Scorpion** lands are smaller, etc. So the RULE for a Rokugani village: where there is **navigable NATURAL water** (a river, the coast), that water is the main freight route and the village gets a **boat landing / jetty** as its primary link; but do NOT default to an artificial **transport canal** - add one only for a **Lion-lands** map or when the GM says so. This is a case where explicit GM setting canon **overrides** the China-first historical default. Make the transport canal + boat landing a **tunable, opt-in feature**, not the rule. (Hoshigaoka: irrigation pond + a minor drain, NOT navigable and NOT canal-served, so its land track legitimately IS the main connector - no canal here.)
  - **JAPAN (corroborating):** wheeled carts were near-absent rurally - ~70% mountains, horses too small to pull carts, carts even prohibited in places to prevent rutting and opposed by the packhorse/porter guilds; peasants traveled on foot, goods by packhorse/porter, heavy freight by boat. Paving was highways-only (even the Gokaido was stone-paved only on steep passes). Same bottom line: narrow, unpaved, single-track.
  - *Render:* `s.lane(pts, width=5, clearance=18, worn=True)` = a narrow packed-earth tread + soft worn shoulder, **NO center marking**. Widths at 1px=2ft: a village lane ~5px (~10 ft), the out-track ~6px - clearly narrower than any constructed road. **`clearance` must be wide enough that a house CORNER clears the tread** - a plain house's half-diagonal is ~14px (a well-off one ~15), so `clearance=18` keeps houses fronting the lane rather than sitting on it. `houses_clear_of_lanes` gates this (fires if any house footprint corner falls within a lane's tread half-width): a lane is a no-build corridor, houses FRONT it, so **lay lanes BEFORE the packs** and give the corridor real width (11 was too tight - house corners overlapped the tread).
  - *The connecting path to the wider world IS marked, RUNS OFF THE EDGE, and the main lane does not dead-end.* A village connects to the nearest district / Imperial road (or, in the rice south, a **canal landing**) by a **trodden dirt track** worn by feet, wheelbarrows, packhorses, and porters - NOT a constructed road. Mark it `s.lane(..., connector=True)`: the village's main lane **continues OUT** of the cluster as this track and **runs OFF the map edge** (labeled e.g. "to the road") - it must NOT stop mid-landscape (that reads as a path to nowhere). `connector_lane_runs_off_edge` gates this: a lane flagged `connector` must have an endpoint at/off the map boundary. Internal lanes (the spine, the field spur) are exempt - they legitimately end in the cluster or at the paddy. The field-ward spur (village to paddy) and the road-ward connector are the two ends of the same through-route.
  - *Rokugan nuance (setting, not history).* The Imperial TRUNK network is unusually well-kept - `government.md`: ~50,000 mi of Imperial roads, ~5,000 staffed waystations, maintained by **corvee** (peasants owe ~10-20 days/yr, "most often called for road repair after the spring thaws and autumn storms"), and Hantei X outlawed road tolls (see [[project_rokugan_unusually_well_run]]). But that is the trunk network; a village's own lanes and its local connector are still humble narrow tracks. An actual Imperial road, if shown, may read as a maintained (earth/gravel, wider) road distinct from the village track.
  - *Legacy:* `worn=False` (the old wide dashed lane) is kept ONLY so the not-yet-rebuilt dispersed pool maps (Hikari, Kikuta) render unchanged; new/rebuilt maps use `worn=True`. `M["lanes"]` records each lane (classified LINEAR for overlap - structs avoid it).
- **Homestead groves (*yashikirin*) are a UNIVERSAL-where-possible windbreak L-belt on the WINDWARD side - the largest homestead appurtenance** (drives `groves_on_windward_side`, `groves_clear_of_paddies`, `groves_clear_of_structures`, `groves_are_substantial`, `groves_where_possible`, `gardens_clear_of_groves`, `yards_unshaded_by_groves`, and `no_groves_inside_walls`; the homestead SOLVER spaces each farm so all its features fit, then groves are placed by `farmsteads()` in a second pass).
  - *What it is.* The **yashikirin** (屋敷林) is the cluster of shelter trees the farm homestead planted around its house - regionally *igune* (Tohoku), *kainyo* (the Tonami plain). It is a **windbreak** (*bofurin*) against the cold prevailing winter wind, and it doubled as a **larder of wood**: firewood and fallen-branch fuel, construction timber (prized cedar/zelkova, often on the west), bamboo, fruit/nuts (persimmon, chestnut), leaf-litter for compost, summer shade, and a buffer slowing fire jumping between homesteads. Drawn as a **size-adaptive L-BELT** wrapping the windward N + W walls (one `groves` record per belt ARM), each arm a **dense, overlapping MIXED stand** of three species - tall **evergreen** conifer (dark, dense apex; the windbreak backbone, cedar/pine), **deciduous** broadleaf (mid green; timber/fruit), and a **bamboo** clump (*take*; fine culms with leafy tops) - so it reads as a real grove, not a few scattered trees. Each arm's canopy count scales with its area (grain-invariant, so a grove reads the same at town and city scale), with a few large emergent crowns over many small; the whole belt is sized to be the LARGEST homestead feature (bigger than the house), distinct from the big `s.forest` area feature and the striped kitchen-garden bed.
  - *Historical scale - the real numbers (research grounding, for calibrating the glyph).* A homestead grove is a substantial STAND, not a few trees. The best hard data is a 1987 survey of Kashima in the Tonami plain (the classic *kainyo* dispersed-farmstead country, 46 households): **~33 trees of trunk diameter >= 10 cm per homestead**, of which cedar (*sugi*) was ~48% (**~16 cedars per house**), the rest spread over ~83 other species; **~6 species per homestead** (range 1-14); a large/notable homestead ran **200+ trees across 31 species**. That count is trunks >= 10 cm ONLY - it EXCLUDES the bamboo stand (hundreds of culms), saplings, and the trimmed hedge layer - so the honest figure for a typical grove is **~30-40 mature trees + a bamboo grove + understory**, and a big one **100-200+**. The grove canopy footprint is therefore the LARGEST homestead appurtenance - **bigger than the farmhouse**, and far bigger than the garden or threshing yard - wrapping the N/W as a belt several trees deep. The map need not draw every tree (houses/yards are already oversized symbols), but per Principle "relative sizes roughly honest" the grove glyph must read at the RIGHT relative scale: clearly the dominant homestead feature, a dense stand suggesting dozens of trees - not a garden-sized clump of 5-10. *(Cross-check on the windward rule: Okinawa's homestead groves sit on the E/N sides, because the islands' damaging wind is the typhoon/NE monsoon, not the mainland NW - same logic, different geography, which is exactly why `windward` is a per-map knob.)*
  - *Prevalence - near-universal in dispersed-settlement country.* In a *sankyoson* (dispersed-farmstead) landscape - Tonami, Izumo, the Shonai/Sendai plains - the grove was **effectively universal, one per farmhouse (~100%)**; it IS the defining feature of that landscape (Tonami: ~7,000 grove-ringed homesteads over ~22,000 ha, about one grove per ~3 ha, farmhouses ~150-180 m apart). In **nucleated** villages (the more common East-Asian/China-leaning pattern) the per-house windbreak is less universal and the landscape instead carries **village-edge / shrine *fengshui* groves**. Our farmhouse-ring villages are semi-dispersed, which justifies a HIGH per-house prevalence. So the grove is **universal where physically possible**: `meta(grove_prevalence=N)` defaults to **1.0** (every farm is a candidate; lower it for a sheltered/atypical microclimate). The homestead SOLVER shifts each farm to make windward room, and a **minimal-clump fallback** means any farm with even a sliver of windward room gets *some* grove (small is fine - the spacious farms carry the big stands). The only farms left grove-less are those whose windward side is genuinely blocked - all paddy/edge, the sun-corridor south of a neighbor's yard, or intramural - which **`groves_where_possible`** verifies (it fires if a grove-less farm actually had clear windward room).
  - *Intramural farms (inside city walls) carry NO windbreak groves (default).* The *yashikirin* is the shelter of an ISOLATED farmstead alone in open fields; an in-wall plot is embedded in the urban fabric (surrounding buildings already break the wind) and sits on land too precious for tree belts - so a walled city's interior agricultural district draws NO groves (the grove pass skips any farm inside `M['wall']`; `no_groves_inside_walls` gates it; `meta(inwall_groves=True)` to override). The driver is "the house is not isolated + land is precious", NOT "the wall blocks the wind" (a wall shelters only a narrow downwind band).
  - *Why the WINDWARD side (N/W by default), never the sunny side.* The grove shelters the wall the wind hits and stays OFF the sunny lee, because the house faces the sun and the work yard needs it. The default windward is **N/W**: the **East Asian winter monsoon** (the Siberian high draining cold air southeastward) blows from the **NW across China AND Japan alike** - China is the source region, not an exception - so N+W is windward and the S/E is the sheltered, sunny side. The grove places a deep belt ARM on each windward face (N **and** W for NW), wrapping the corner into an L; a cardinal windward gets one deep band. **Since Rokugan leans continental (China-like), the wind is the same NW but drier and dustier** (continental dust, not maritime snow) - the orientation is unchanged; only the flavor shifts.
  - *Per-map geography overrides the default.* A map keys the grove off its own exposure with **`meta(windward=...)`** (`"N"`/`"NE"`/`"E"`/.../`"NW"`, default `"NW"`) - e.g. farmland downwind of a specific sea, mountain wall, or the western Burning Sands/steppe. The check reads the same `meta.windward`, so the geometry gate follows the declared wind.
  - *Size-adaptive, merging, never drops a house.* Each windward arm is tried at full depth, then shallower, then a shorter partial belt, then a **minimal clump** to slip into any sliver. Arms are kept OUT of `placed`, so a neighbor's grove may **MERGE** with this one - clustered homesteads read as one continuous windbreak (historically each grove was individual, but adjacent ones ran together; we do not model *shared* groves). Groves are placed in a **second pass after every farmhouse + its yard + garden**, so an optional grove can never block a neighbor's mandatory appurtenances and drop that house. The checks gate GEOMETRY per arm (windward / off-paddy / off-buildings), the typical grove's SCALE (`groves_are_substantial`: median grove >= **0.5x** its house - the spacious farms carry real stands, small clumps on cramped farms are fine), and presence (`groves_where_possible`, above).
  - *The homestead SOLVER - one rule that keeps the whole farmstead coherent.* `_solve_homestead` searches a **widening spiral of house positions** (the placed spot first, then shifts) for one where the threshing yard, the dooryard garden, AND room for a windward grove all fit, non-overlapping - keeping the farm **field-adjacent** and dropping it only if even its yard+garden won't fit anywhere (rare). This **spaces a crowded homestead out** instead of stacking features (so the grove no longer needs to overlap the garden - `gardens_clear_of_groves` gates that the two never stack), and it leaves grove-room so the second pass can place a grove on nearly every farm. To help it, the **dooryard garden prefers the LEE sunny side** (east, then the SE/SW corners) and takes the **windward wall LAST**, leaving that side free for the grove.
  - *And a grove never shades a yard.* **A grove never sits in the sun-corridor directly south of a threshing yard** (`yards_unshaded_by_groves`): the yard dries rice in the southern sun, so a *neighbor's* grove immediately to its south would shade it (a grove is N/W of its own house, so this only steers it off a neighbor's yard).
- **Village windbreak - the Chinese FENGSHUI FOREST (风水林), a COMMUNAL grove, is the NUCLEATED village's windbreak (NOT per-house groves)** (drives `village_windbreak_present`, `village_windbreak_on_windward_side`, `village_groves_clear_of_paddies`, `groves_clear_of_lanes`, and the nucleated SKIP of `groves_where_possible`; drawn by `s.village_grove(poly, role=..., dense=...)`, recorded in `M['village_groves']`; researched + source-verified 2026-07, this is the record - do not re-research).
  - *Trees stay OFF the paths (`groves_clear_of_lanes`).* A lane/street/road is bare trodden earth - you do not plant trees on it - so `village_grove` skips any clump within a corridor's keep-out (lanes + town streets + the road, via `_corridor_buffers`), just as it skips houses/yards/gardens/paddy. Each `village_grove` records its **actual drawn clump centers + radius** (`clumps`, `r`), and the check verifies none sits on a corridor (the recorded bounding `poly` covers the whole scatter region, so the check must test the real clumps, not the poly). The per-house *yashikirin* groves (`M['groves']`, a rect each) are checked the same way. This caught the leafy copse scatter landing on the village spine lane.
  - *Trees stay OUT of the SOUTHERN SUN of yards + gardens (`village_trees_unshade_yards_and_gardens`).* The drying yard and the kitchen garden need the sun, which comes from the SOUTH (+y), so no village-grove clump may sit in the strip directly south of either - `village_grove` skips any clump in a yard's/garden's southern sun-corridor (a touch wider than the check, so it stays clear), and the check verifies it against the recorded clumps. (This is the communal-grove companion to `yards_unshaded_by_groves`, which does the same for the per-house groves; the scatter avoids sitting ON a yard/garden, but that is not enough - it must also avoid being just SOUTH of one.)
  - *A clump ABUTS but never OVERLAPS a farmstead (`grove_clumps_clear_of_structures`, GM 2026-07).* Trees stand right up against a house wall - "directly next to" is correct and common - but a clump drawn ON a roof/yard/garden reads wrong. The copse keep-out reserves each house/yard/garden/byre/shed by its circumscribing radius PLUS the clump's FULL drawn radius (clump/2) and a hair - not a fraction of it. The old 0.35xclump was ~0.15xclump short, which let a blob corner clip a small farmhouse (found on Hikari). The check measures the tree blob (a round mass, radius r) against each footprint rect and fires on real penetration; a few px of canopy over the eaves is fine (the GM's "directly next to is OK").
  - *Shape - ORGANIC and EMBRACING, never a ruled wall (a correction the GM caught).* Real groves hug the terrain and WRAP the settlement; a rectangle + a square read as machine-planted. So `village_grove` takes an **irregular polygon footprint** (ragged, terrain-following) and FILLS it with clumps on a jittered grid, SKIPPING any clump that would land on a house / threshing yard / garden / paddy - which lets the belt nestle right up to the village edge without ever drawing trees on a building or in the crops. The 背山面水 ideal ("back to the hill, face the water") drives the whole layout: the DENSE wood sits on the high/windward BACK and wraps the flanks (the 玄武 back-forest + 青龙/白虎 embracing arms), while the FIELD/water side stays OPEN. The back belt is drawn as a CRESCENT embracing the windward edge, deepest in the middle and tapering into horns that wrap the corners.
  - *Why communal, not per-house.* The per-house *yashikirin* (the bullet above) is the DISPERSED-farmstead windbreak; a NUCLEATED cluster (the China-leaning default, per 'Settlement form') shelters itself and cannot fit a ~6x-house grove-bundle on every farm. So the nucleated windbreak is a single **village-scale grove**, exactly the dominant Chinese pattern: the **fengshui forest**, a grove protected by taboo (sacred "dragon" woodland, cutting forbidden - which is why these survived as the most mature forest stands in southern China). This is the CHINA-first answer per the core principle (surveys across Fujian, Jiangxi, Guangdong, Hunan, Anhui - our rice-south analog; Chen & Coggins et al.).
  - *Three parts, sited by role - the DENSE wood is concentrated, but the village is LEAFY, not bare (a second GM correction).* The greenery is STRUCTURED (not a uniform forest), and specifically does NOT line the main road or shade the paddies/drying yards (the field/downslope side stays open - same crop-and-drying logic as the *yashikirin* sunny-lee rule). But a south-China village was NOT bare tan ground with a few tree-blobs: houses nestled in scattered **bamboo and dooryard fruit** (litchi, longan, persimmon, citrus, mulberry - some villages effectively bamboo-encircled). So the three parts are:
    1. **Back-village belt (`role="windbreak"`, 后龙林)** - the big one: a dense grove crescent EMBRACING the high **WINDWARD back edge** (default NW - the winter-monsoon wall), nestled against the cluster and wrapping the NW/SW flanks. Villages backed against the high ground, so where the high side is windward the back grove IS the wind wall (Hoshigaoka: the WEST, away from the E/SE paddy). `village_windbreak_present` requires it; `village_windbreak_on_windward_side` requires its centroid windward of the house-cluster centroid.
    2. **Water-mouth cluster (`role="water_mouth"`, 水口林)** - a smaller irregular cluster of big old trees at the LOW entrance / water-mouth (where the stream/track exits down-valley), "locking in" the water and qi and marking the entrance.
    3. **Leafy scatter (`role="copse"`, `dense=False`)** - bamboo + dooryard fruit clumps filling the OPEN gaps THROUGHOUT the cluster (not a light dusting - the village reads as embedded in green), the conifer-free 'dooryard' species mix. Drawn by passing the whole-cluster region as one sparse-filled polygon; `village_grove` drops a clump into every gap that is clear of a house/yard/garden/paddy, so the houses end up nestled in bamboo and fruit trees while the lanes, yards, and field side stay open.
  - *Scale - the real numbers (research grounding, for calibrating the glyph).* Field surveys of southern-China village fengshui forests: **~2 groves per village** on average; **stem density ~3,400 woody stems/ha**, basal area ~49 m²/ha (genuine closed-canopy forest, not scattered trees); patch AREA is highly variable (famous lineage-village groves exceed 20 ha, e.g. Lingtou, 800 years old), but a TYPICAL village grove is a small forest patch, **~1-2 ha for the back grove** (modest villages <1 ha, big clan villages much larger) and **~0.1-0.5 ha for the water-mouth cluster**. So a ~1-2 ha back belt is *thousands* of woody stems in total, of which roughly **100-300 are mature canopy trees** over a dense bamboo/shrub understory; the water-mouth is **a few dozen big old trees**. Per "relative sizes roughly honest": the back belt reads as a real small FOREST (clearly the largest vegetation feature, a wall of dozens-to-hundreds of crowns - `village_grove` fills its polygon with overlapping dense clumps so a big belt does not read as a handful of lone trees), the water-mouth as a distinct smaller cluster, plus the bamboo/fruit scatter through the village. Hoshigaoka draws a ~1-2 ha embracing windward belt + a ~0.3 ha water-mouth + the leafy scatter.
  - *Why the sizing is honest at this scale.* At 1 px = 2 ft a ~1-2 ha grove is ~27,000-54,000 px² - genuinely large relative to the ~11 ha built cluster (~10-18%), so the belt reads as the dominant feature without being cartoonishly oversized. Sources: Fengshui woodland (Wikipedia); Chen & Coggins et al., "Fengshui forests and village landscapes in China" (57-village survey); Hu et al., "Values of village fengshui forest patches" (Pearl River Delta, 32 patches, the density/basal-area figures); "Village Fengshui Forests as Cultural and Ecological Heritage" (Forests 2020).
  - *The FAR side of the back-grove is NOT more forest - it is degraded FUEL-AND-FODDER COMMONS + the back-slope waste (`s.commons(poly)`, `M['commons']`; drives `commons_clear_of_paddies`, `commons_beyond_the_windbreak`).* The lush grove is a THIN protected band; the taboo is exactly *why* it is green when the surrounding hills are not. South China's hills were progressively stripped for firewood/timber over ~1,000 years, so past the grove the ground is cut-over and open - "a monotonous succession of hills either deforested or carrying open stands" of masson pine / China-fir, with severe erosion. So `commons` draws the far/upslope side deliberately OPEN and SPARSE - coarse grass tufts, low brush, and a FEW scraggly individual pines - VISUALLY DISTINCT from the dense dark closed-canopy grove (it is a *commons*, not a field, and never the flooded paddy). It has **NO solid fill and NO hard outline** (an early filled quad read as a literal rhombus): the scrub is a FEATHERED scatter that thins to nothing at the margin, so the ground has no boundary at all, just scrub petering out onto the open slope. It is drawn as a ragged BAND that PARALLELS the grove's back at a jittered width and is drawn UNDER the forest, so the grove's canopy overlaps its inner edge and the two ABUT seamlessly (no bare gap between wood and scrub). The land TOPOSEQUENCE is **village -> back-grove -> fuel commons -> (off-map domain boundary)**, so the commons sits on the windward/high side and FURTHER out than the windbreak (`commons_beyond_the_windbreak` gates the ordering; `commons_clear_of_paddies` keeps it off the wet crop). Sources: South China degraded lands (millennium of fuel/timber cutting); FAO forest-rehabilitation (deforested hills, open pine, erosion).
  - *`commons(poly, role="grazing")` for the OTHER dry margins.* The same scrub glyph fills any dry, marginal, non-paddy flank of the SATOYAMA toposequence (grass/scrub grazing hill) - the NW pond CATCHMENT, the NORTH up-valley head above the dry-field (*hatake*) margin, the NE flank, the SW/south back-slope behind the village. `role="grazing"` is general marginal hill-grazing, so it is **exempt from `commons_beyond_the_windbreak`** (that windward-back-slope ordering only applies to the fuel/fodder commons proper, `role="commons"`, the default); `commons_clear_of_paddies` still applies to all. **Fill the WHOLE margin as a CONTINUOUS RING, not isolated corner patches** (the GM's rule): every "empty" edge of the map is DRY marginal land in a NW-high/SE-low valley - so it is a continuous band of the toposequence (pond-catchment -> dry fields -> grazing scrub -> grove -> graveyard -> marsh), never open plain or forest-everywhere and never a few little rectangles floating in tan. Broad polygons that span a whole edge (e.g. one north band across the entire top above the dry fields, dropping at the east edge to meet the SE marsh; one south band filling the back-slope below the village) read as real hillsides; small disconnected quads read as patches. **`margins_form_continuous_ring` gates this** (village/hamlet): no more than 12% of the FRAMED view may be bare of every ground feature - calibrated so the ring-conforming maps (0-8.4% bare, the tan showing only as feathered seams between scatters) pass with margin while open plain fires. **Draw the bands for the FRAME, not the canvas**: `crop_to_content` hugs the hard content (houses/fields), and commons are NOT crop content - so a band placed against the canvas edge can fall entirely OUTSIDE the cropped view. This bit Ueda (2026-07-20): its W/SW back-slope band sat at canvas x 120-480, the crop tightened the frame to x>=505, and 28% of the rendered map was bare open tan with the ring off-camera. Check `meta["view"]` after a first render and span the bands across the framed window; generous overshoot is safe (the scatter auto-skips paddy, dry plots, lanes, water, structures, and swept clearings, and `commons_clear_of_paddies` rejects only a patch with no open ground at all). Keep each `grazing` polygon OFF the crops: `commons` auto-skips the paddy envelope, dry-plots (via `block_polys`), and - since the urban-clearance halo (see the scrub-off-buildings bullets under the grove section) - every structure plus its 30 ft halo; but a poly should still not CLAIM built or cropped ground (`scrub_clear_of_urban_fabric` fires on a poly containing any dwelling/well center), so hold the polygon ABOVE the dry-field tops and WEST of the paddy edge (query those extents; don't eyeball). On a fully-filled map the auto-title has no blank margin to land in, so leave it a small tan CLEARING (dip the band's top edge over the title's x-range - a natural col in the ridge) and call `s.title()` AFTER `crop_to_content()` as usual so `title_clear_of_features` still passes.
  - *DRAW ORDER for the margin fill (the GM's rule): place the grazing/scrub AFTER the farmhouses + groves but BEFORE the graveyard + shrine.* After the houses/groves so the scrub fills the gaps THEY leave (not drawn over them); before the funerary + religious features so those sit ON the scrubland, not on bare tan - burial + *kegare* belong on the waste back-slope, and a shrine at the water-mouth sits in its grove/scrub. In the gen this means: farmhouses -> village_grove -> `commons(role="grazing")` margin bands -> `crop_to_content()` -> `shrine_hall` / `cemetery`.
  - *Scrub/reeds are ground-cover UNDER the trodden road, never over it (the GM's rule: "the road should not look overgrown").* A constantly-walked way stays BARE - so `commons` and `marsh` skip any scatter point on ANY corridor tread: lanes, town streets, AND the road (`_corridor_buffers`, clearing each tread's half-width + ~3 px). The skip originally knew only `M['lanes']`, which is how Hoshizora's scrub drew on the Imperial Road bed (GM 2026-07-21) - the corridor set now covers all three. The connector track that leaves the village always crosses the back-slope scrub; without the skip, scrub tufts drawn AFTER the lane sit on top of it and it reads as overgrown. The skip is per-tuft (the corridor must already be recorded, i.e. drawn *before* the ground-cover - which it is: lanes/roads lay their no-build corridor before the houses), so the tread reads clean ON the scrub regardless of z-order, and it generalises to a marsh causeway. No manifest-level check (scatter points are not individually recorded, same as the paddy-skip) - covered by `test_settlement` unit tests (`test_commons_keeps_scrub_off_the_road_bed`).
  - *Scrub/reeds are also kept OFF every building footprint.* `commons` and `marsh` skip any scatter point that falls on a `block_polys` no-build footprint (houses, wellheads, and especially the **shrine hall + its torii arch**) - a commons polygon that OVERLAPS the shrine (Ueda's water-mouth commons contains the shrine) must not dot grass tufts and scraggly pines over the hall and gateway. Same per-tuft mechanism as the lane skip (the structure must be drawn *before* the ground-cover; place `shrine_hall` before `commons`), same no-manifest-check caveat - covered by unit tests (`test_commons_keeps_scrub_off_a_shrine_and_torii`, `test_marsh_keeps_reeds_off_a_building`). Distinct from `shrine_clear_of_grove_trees` / `torii_clear_of_grove_trees`, which gate the dense *grove* clumps (those ARE recorded, so they carry a check); the loose commons scatter is handled purely at draw time.
  - *The URBAN-CLEARANCE HALO: scrub is cleared from the whole BUILT-UP fabric, not just off footprints (GM 2026-07-21, Hoshizora).* The town's gen drew deliberately "generous" scrub polys over the urban core, trusting the per-point skips - and the render showed scrub speckle through the streets, dooryards, and gaps between the shops, merchant houses, laborer housing, and burakumin quarter, crowding right up to the wellheads. *What the reasoning found:* the ground nearest occupied structures is the FIRST stripped bare - dooryards are packed swept earth, the alleys between packed houses are walked bare daily, and a settlement's fuel/fodder-gathering pressure crops brush closest to home first - so scrub survives only past the daily-traffic halo, on the outskirts. *The decision, three mutually-reinforcing layers, one shared constant set (`settlement._urban_keepouts`; real FEET, converted at the map grain):* **(1) engine** - the `commons`/`marsh` scatter skips a **30 ft halo** around every occupied structure (houses, buildings, storehouses, flophouses, byres, farm sheds, religious, manors, ministries, inspection stations; a dooryard's working depth, which also closes the gaps in packed districts whose ~40-48 ft spacing leaves no strip wider than two halos), a **20 ft apron** around every wellhead (the most-trodden, spill-puddled ground in any settlement), and an **8 ft edge** on tended plots (gardens, threshing yards); **(2) check** - `scrub_clear_of_urban_fabric` (town/city ONLY): no occupied structure's or wellhead's CENTER may lie inside a commons cover poly of any role - the recorded poly must itself trace the outskirts, order-blind so a structure drawn AFTER the cover fires too (field `barn`s exempt: a hay barn stands in the grazed ground it serves; at village/hamlet scale the check deliberately does NOT run - the satoyama doctrine interleaves dispersed farmsteads, the water-mouth shrine, and wells WITH the marginal scrub, the farms standing ON the waste, so there only the engine halo applies); **(3) the tm interplay** - `town_margins_clothed` counts the same 30 ft halo around structures as covered "urban floor" (the unwalled-town equivalent of its inside-the-rampart exemption), so the bare trodden ground between districts no longer needs scrub to pass and the two checks cannot fight. Regression fixture: `pool/regressions/scrub_clear_of_urban_fabric_fires_on_hoshizora_town_core.json` (the actual pre-fix manifest, 74 offenders); engine side covered by `test_settlement.py::test_commons_clears_the_urban_halo_around_buildings` + `_wellhead_apron` + `test_marsh_clears_the_urban_halo_and_wellheads`.
  - *Scrub/reeds are kept OFF open WATER too (GM 2026-07: "scrubland rendered over the pond and stream is clearly wrong").* `commons` and `marsh` skip any scatter point on the POND (its ellipse) or on a stream / irrigation channel BED (`_on_watercourse`: within the watercourse's half-width + a hair of `M['streams']` + `M['channels']`). Vegetation never floats on water - the same reasoning as the lane/building skips. This matters most on a hamlet where the scrub matrix fills the whole frame (Moritono's scrub used to cover the tameike + the forest brook + the comb ditches). `marsh` already skipped the open pond for its `pond_fringe` reeds; now both also skip watercourses. Per-tuft draw-time skip (the water must be drawn *before* the ground-cover - which it is: fields + water go down before the hinterland), no manifest check (scatter points aren't recorded) - covered by `test_settlement.py::test_on_watercourse_detects_stream_and_channel_beds` + `test_commons_and_marsh_skip_the_pond_and_watercourses`.
  - *A WELLHEAD is a clean draw-point - no tree canopy over it* (`wells_clear_of_trees`, GM 2026-07). A well lost under the wood reads wrong; the check fires on any well whose drawn head (`vr`) overlaps a **fengshui grove clump** (windbreak / water-mouth / copse), a **per-house grove** rect, a **forest**, or a **coppice-woodland** patch. FIX at placement: wells go down BEFORE the grove, and `village_grove` skips a clump within `well.vr + 0.9*clump` of a well (was `well.r + 0.35*clump` - too tight, so a clump's canopy still overhung the wellhead; Hoshigaoka + Ueda each had wells buried in the windbreak belt). Teeth: `test_checks.py::test_wells_clear_of_trees_fires_on_grove_forest_woodland_grect_but_passes_when_clear`.
  - *The village GRAVEYARD is the shrine's CHURCHYARD (built); the back-slope carries two still-deferred slices.* (a) the **village GRAVEYARD / cemetery (BUILT)** sits in the **shrine's precinct** - its resident Brother of Shinsei performs the funerary rites (see the churchyard rule above, `village_graveyard_by_shrine`), so `s.cemetery(cx, cy, w, h, parish=False, organic=True)` (a village plot ~0.2-0.3 ha, NOT a temple parish ground) goes in the clear ground of the shrine's yard, OFF the hall + torii themselves. **`organic=True` for a village burial ground**: a peasant graveyard was never surveyed into a ruled rectangle - it grew as an unbounded earthen patch, so `organic` draws an IRREGULAR blob (a jittered polygon inscribed in the `w x h` footprint) with the markers filling that blob, instead of the default rounded rectangle (which is right for a walled temple *parish* precinct). The recorded bbox AND the no-build block stay the `w x h` rectangle, so every placement / clearance / set-back check is unaffected - only the DRAWN ground is organic. It still keeps a set-back from OPEN water and the paddy (`funerary_set_back_from_water`) - the shrine sits at the water-mouth but the yard has dry ground off the field; and cremation means the old "graves foul the water source" body-burial worry is weak anyway (Rokugan inters *bones*). **Placement CROP bonus:** moving the graveyard from the far back-slope INTO the shrine precinct means it is no longer the map's outlying westernmost feature, so `crop_to_content` tightens that edge automatically (Ueda's west pulled in ~85px). The high/back slope beyond the grove still carries the two DEFERRED slices: (b) **dry hill-fields + orchards** (the unirrigable "hill crops": sweet potato, tea, mulberry, tung-oil, bamboo, dry grains - the *hatake* / 旱地 margin); (c) **rocky / eroded / poor-soil waste**. So the back-slope zone, in order of workability, is: dry-field + orchard patches -> fuel/fodder scrub commons -> waste. (The `commons` feature above is the scrub-commons slice; the dry-field/waste texture is the remaining deferred slice.)
  - *The village SHRINE sits at the WATER-MOUTH ENTRANCE (built; drives `religious_matches_scale` = village -> a `"shrine"`).* A Chinese village's tutelary / earth-god shrine (土地庙 Tudi Miao) stood "near the entrance, in the most strategic feng-shui location" - which is the **water-mouth** (水口), the feng-shui-critical entry/exit point where the connector track leaves for the road (and where the water-mouth grove already grows). So the village's one Shinto shrine nestles there in its sacred grove (*chinju no mori*), a small `s.shrine_hall(x, y, "", w=30, h=24, kind="shrine", primary=True, torii=[(tx, ty)], graveyard=False)` fronted by a torii facing the village. **SIZE it small** (~30x24 px ~ 270 m², a touch bigger than a plain farmhouse) - a village earth-god shrine is a modest HALL, not a temple; `shrine_hall`'s default 120x82 is a town-monastery / city-temple size and reads as a mansion on a village (pass small `w, h`). **No label** (`label=""`): the vermilion hall + torii glyph reads as a shrine on sight, and the graveyard's marker rows read as a graveyard, so both are left unlabeled per "do not label what is visually evident". `graveyard=False` on the `shrine_hall` call only suppresses its DEFAULT attached parish plot; the village graveyard is placed SEPARATELY (its own `s.cemetery`) in the shrine's yard - the churchyard, since the shrine's monk tends the dead (see the churchyard rule above). This is the CHINA-first answer (earth-god shrine at the entrance); the Japanese surface is the tutelary *chinju* in its grove. A village gets a SHRINE, never a temple (see the village-shrine-not-temple rule + `religious_matches_scale`: hamlet none / village shrine / town monastery / city temples).
- **Farmer wealth + homestead variation - houses come in a modest range of sizes and PROPORTIONS** (drives `farmhouse_sizes_vary`, `farmhouse_aspect_in_range`). Real holdings ranged from the landless *mizunomi* to *honbyakushO* landholders, and no two farmsteads were copy-pasted, so a plain farmhouse varies TWO ways depending on the settlement mode:
  - *DISPERSED (ring villages): a wealth TIER scaling the whole glyph.* A plain farmhouse takes a modest **wealth tier** (deterministic in its position): ~30% poor (0.9x), ~50% typical (1.0x), ~20% well-off (1.12x), recorded as the house's `wealth`. The tier scales the **rendered house** and, with it, the **homestead grove** (a richer farm reads bigger across the board). **Scope limit:** the wealth factor is applied to the rendered glyph ONLY - the footprint is reserved and the yard/garden placed at BASE size, then the house is drawn (and grove sized) at `base x wealth` - so it never perturbs packing/household counts. Here the yard/garden/shed stay uniform (scaling them coupled into placement and dropped houses), so the wealth signal rides the two largest features.
  - *NUCLEATED (clustered villages): the FOOTPRINT itself jitters, LENGTH-dominant (minka bays).* A clustered house varies its base `w,h` directly (position-seeded, wealth stays 1.0): the **length** (E-W long axis) jitters more (~0.85-1.35x), the **depth** (N-S) far less (~0.90-1.10x) - because a *minka* grew by **adding bays along its length** (a *hisashi* aisle, an extra *ma*), staying "longer but not much wider," never ballooning into a square or a 4:1 shed. `farmhouse_aspect_in_range` guards the result (fires above ~2.7:1; a minka norm is ~1.3-2.5:1). And here the **yard and garden vary too** (see their bullets - garden jitters up from its minimum, yard down from its maximum, both gaining irregular-quad shape and the garden an occasional split), because the nucleated bundle reserves each appurtenance's footprint before packing, so varying them within that reservation does not tip a boxed-in farm over.
  - *One check, both encodings.* `farmhouse_sizes_vary` measures the **effective rendered area = w × h × wealth²**, so it catches a regression that flattens houses under EITHER path (uniform footprint + wealth tiers, OR jittered footprint at wealth 1.0). The **headman** is exempt and stays the largest (`headman_is_largest`). All the jitter is a deterministic sin-hash of position (`_hjit`), so it never draws from the placement RNG and never ripples household counts.
- **Farmhouses face SOUTH** (drives `houses_face_south`, and the yard's south-front placement). *Minka* were oriented south for sunlight (warmth and drying); we render the long axis E-W with the entrance on the south (+y) side.
- **Commercial frontage, residential depth, wealth-varied housing** (drives `businesses_front_streets`, `buildings_face_street`, `housing_off_main_street`, `poor_housing_mostly_interior`, `merchant_residences_behind_businesses`, `housing_aligned_behind_storefronts`, `town_merchant_housing_varied` / `town_laborer_housing_varied` / `city_merchant_housing_varied` / `city_laborer_housing_varied` / `city_samurai_housing_varied`, `city_merchant_housing_spread`). In premodern Japanese castle-towns (*jokamachi*) and Chinese cities the valuable street frontage is COMMERCE: shops and workshops line the high street, each facing the road, while dwellings sit behind them or in the block interior reached by alleys. A merchant family lived over/behind its own shop (so merchant residences sit directly behind the storefronts, sharing their orientation). Housing was **stratified by wealth** - a few rich households in large homes or walled courtyard estates, most in modest/uniform dwellings, never one size - and the merchant quarter SPREADS (courtyard compounds) while the laborer warren packs dense and uniform.
- **Communal wells: density, siting, and the samurai exception** (drives `city_well_density_sufficient`, `city_wells_in_block_interiors`, `city_neighborhoods_have_wells`, `wells_among_dwellings`, `settlement_dwellings_watered`, `city_samurai_quarter_has_no_public_wells`). Commoner *machi* shared **public wells in the block interiors** (courtyard wells), roughly **1 per ~10-20 households** - enough that no quarter lacks water, not one on every corner. The samurai/government quarter shows **NO public wells**: noble and official households drew from **private wells inside their own walled compounds** (a status + security distinction), so a communal well there would read wrong.
- **Caste geography & status zoning** (drives `walled_city_has_burakumin_inside`, `city_samurai_quarter_gated` / `city_samurai_ward_sealed` / `city_ward_fence_*`, `city_flophouse_in_humble_quarter`, `city_samurai_estates_outside` / `city_samurai_estates_dispersed` / `city_estates_toward_capital`, and the burakumin-neighborhood siting). Castes lived apart. Burakumin cluster at the margin (a town's edge, or outside a city) - but a WALLED city keeps at least one burakumin neighborhood INSIDE, because it cannot do without their labor during a siege. The samurai/government quarter is sealed off the commoner streets by **kido ward-gates shut at night** (status separation + night security; a small provincial city uses kido, not the continuous walled wards of a great Tang-style capital). The cheap flophouse (*kichin-yado*) belongs in a humble laborer/agrarian quarter, not beside the temples, merchants, or samurai. Wealthy samurai kept walled country **estates outside the walls** and commuted in, for roomier houses than cramped city lots allowed.
- **Funerary geography: pollution, fire, and the two faiths** (drives `cemetery_clear_of_shrine`, `funerary_set_back_from_water`, `cemetery_in_temple_precinct` / `city_temples_have_graveyards`, `cremation_ground_set_back_from_main_road` / `cremation_ground_not_between_temple_and_road` / `city_has_cremation_ground`, `city_has_ossuary`, `walled_graveyards_inside_and_outside` / `walled_exterior_cemetery_larger`). Death-pollution (*kegare*) is a Shinto concern: no grave sits hard against a **shrine**, and burial grounds keep well back from **water** (a flooding/leaching grave fouls the supply). Buddhism manages the dead, so a graveyard is a temple **parish (*danka*) ground** sitting in a temple/monastery precinct. The **crematory (*kasoba*)** sits OUTSIDE the walls (smoke, fire risk, pollution), set back from the main road, and never between a temple and the road (no one should walk past the pyre to reach worship); monks officiate with burakumin assistants (a religious order stands outside the caste system, so handling the dead does not pollute it). A pauper **ossuary** holds the *muenbotoke* (unconnected dead). A centuries-old city splits graveyards inside + outside the walls, the main extramural ground the largest.
- **Religious building by settlement scale** (drives `religious_matches_scale`, the `torii_*` / `city_temple_approach_has_torii` checks, `town_declares_monasteries` / `town_monasteries_dedicated`, `city_temple_neighborhood_has_shrines`). Type and size track scale and the Shinto/Buddhist split: a **hamlet** has no religious building of its own (it shares the village district's), a **village** a Shinto **shrine**, a **town** a Buddhist **monastery** (dedicated to a patron Fortune), a **city** full **temples** (its temple neighborhood dotted with small wayside shrines). A **torii** (Shinto gateway) fronts any shrine/temple a street runs straight up to. (See also the village-SHRINE-not-temple rule in the project memory.)
- **Government & commerce of a seat of administration** (drives `town_has_magistrate_manor`, `town_has_caravan_inn`, `town_has_granary`, `city_has_governor_mansion` / `city_has_six_ministries` / `city_has_ministry_of_rites`, `city_ministries_cluster_at_government` / `city_ministries_front_a_street` / `city_governor_mansion_large` / `city_government_offices_dont_abut`, `city_inspection_station_at_each_gate` / `city_gate_caravan_facilities`, `city_has_gate_market` / `walled_town_has_gate_market`). Following imperial Chinese *yamen* and Japanese *jokamachi* practice, government offices cluster around the **governor's mansion** (the grandest compound) and FRONT an official avenue, never floating mid-block. A provincial city carries the **Six provincial Ministries** (one being Rites, sited in the temple neighborhood). The state taxes trade: **inspection/tariff stations at every gate**, **caravan facilities** (inn + stables + animal pasture) on the trade route, a **tax-rice granary** at a collecting magistracy, and **markets** springing up just outside the gates. A county-seat **town** is run by a magistrate from a walled manor.
- **Paddy plot grain + IRREGULAR PATCHWORK: fields are a fine mosaic of small, odd-shaped bunded plots** (drives `paddy_field`'s recursive `_paddy_plots` subdivision, the `plot` grain, and the `bund` stroke). A premodern wet-rice field was not one green expanse but a mosaic of *many small irregular bunded plots* (each a separately-flooded basin walled by low raised-earth ridges, *aze*, that double as footpaths); a single 1-cho (~1 ha) holding was subdivided into **dozens**, and pre-consolidation paddies were often just a few *se* (~100-300 m^2). Two rules:
  - *Shape - irregular patchwork, NOT a grid.* Pre-modern paddies were fitted to the land and water by piecemeal reclamation and inheritance, so plots are odd-sized and odd-shaped with bunds meeting at **T-junctions**; the tidy rectangular grid is a **modern (Meiji/Showa) land-consolidation (*kochi seiri*) artifact** and reads as anachronistic. So `_paddy_plots` builds the field by **recursively splitting** it - cut each plot's LONG axis with a straight, slightly-angled *aze* at a jittered 0.36-0.64 fraction, down to the target grain (with size variation) - giving the genuine irregular-parcel look. (An earlier jittered *grid* was still too regular; a proper recursive split fixes it.)
  - *Size - to scale.* At village scale (1 px = 2 ft), `plot=34` -> ~0.10 acre/plot (with the split variation ~0.05-0.17 acre), squarely the real pre-modern range (~1-5 *se*). The default `plot=46` (~0.19 acre) is the coarser legibility floor for older/denser maps; villages pass `plot=34`. *Departure:* true plots are finer still, but the bund network + planting-row hatching must stay legible at the 2600 px render. The **bund stroke thins with the grain** (`0.03*plot + 0.35`); per-plot color = staggered flooding/planting, a fraction dry = barley/soy margins.
  - NOTE: the patchwork draws from the shared placement RNG, so changing `plot` shifts every downstream house position - the pool gens carry hand-picked seeds; a grain change means re-finding a clean seed (and placing big fixed buildings BEFORE the packs, which then flow around them).
- **Non-topographic relief: show slope by its CONSEQUENCES, never by shading** (drives the pictorial `hill()` mound, the `irrigation_ditch` + drainage water direction, and the deliberate ABSENCE of hillshade/contours). These maps are deliberately **not topographic** - no contour lines, no spot heights. Relief is shown three ways, which stack for a rice village:
  - *Pictorial hills.* A hill is a shaded **mound** (`hill()`: concentric tiers + radial hachures + a wooded cap) - the old *ezu* / estate-map convention, a picture of high ground, not survey lines.
  - *The farming shows the slope.* Water enters HIGH and leaves LOW; on genuine slopes paddies become **terraces** (*tanada*: long, narrow, CURVED contour-following strips, stacked with a riser between). Terracing is a **STEEP-slope feature** - a **gentle** valley tilt produces NO visible terraces, only a flat patchwork with imperceptible level steps, so we do NOT draw terraces on a gently-tilted village (it would misread as mountainous). Kikuta is a gentle tilt: flat patchwork, slope carried by water alone.
  - *Water direction is the honest gentle-slope cue.* Source high, drainage low. Kikuta: **NW = uphill** (the cold NW winter-monsoon wind pours off the high ground, so windward and uphill coincide) - pond/source at the NW corner, irrigation (*yosui*) distributary ditches (`irrigation_ditch`, drawn OVER the paddy since the deferred water layer sits UNDER the fields) fanning water in from the high NW, and a **drainage (*haisui*) ditch leaving the LOW SE corner** off-map (a hairline `channel(..., {"kind":"field"...}, {"kind":"offmap"})`). Wet rice needs water IN **and** water OUT - irrigation + drainage are the two halves of every paddy system (often separate networks) - so a drainage outlet is a real, expected feature, and it doubles as the downhill cue.
  - *What we REJECTED and why:* directional **shading / cast shadows** to imply the gradient. On a plan map, shaded relief reads as a **light source** ("the sun is in the NW"), not as a rising gradient - it fights the flat-plan convention and misleads. Slope is shown by water + (steep-only) terraces + pictorial hills, never by shading.
- **Marsh - where the paddy STOPS, the low ground is reed WETLAND, not dry plain** (`s.marsh(poly)`, `M['marshes']`; drives `marsh_on_low_ground`; researched + source-verified 2026-07). The empty ground beyond a village's paddy is not "open plain" by default - a wet-rice paddy stops where flooding the land stops being worth it, and *if that land were good paddy it would already be in the district* (the village would be larger). So the ground beyond is UNSUITABLE for paddy, and WHAT it is depends on WHY: **too HIGH to irrigate** -> dry fields (*hatake* / 旱地, the NW margin we already draw); **too STEEP** -> the wooded/scrubby back-slope (grove + graves); **poor/saline soil** -> waste / rough grazing; and - the key one for the LOW, downstream toe - **too LOW / WET / undrained** -> **reed MARSH / wet meadow**. This is grounded in the fact that **wet rice is reclaimed FROM marsh**: the lower-Yangzi / Tai-Lake paddies are embanked **polders (圩田/围田)** diked out into marsh and lake, and where reclamation stops (or the ground is too wet to manage) it *stays* reed wetland (abandoned paddy reverts to marsh). So `s.marsh` draws the low SE/downstream toe as WET and SPARSE: a faint blue-green wet tint (soft translucent patches), scattered reed/sedge tufts, and a few standing-water glints - FEATHERED to nothing at the margin (no hard edge, like the commons), a distinctly wet palette vs the dry tan scrub commons. It SKIPS any point on a paddy, so a generous region abuts the field's low edge (the polder embankment) and only fills the ground beyond, trailing off the map corner. `marsh_on_low_ground` verifies it sits DOWNHILL of the field (higher `fall` per `meta.down_deg`) - marsh is the low valley toe, never the high/dry side. **Do NOT default the beyond-the-paddy ground to dry "plain" or to forest** (valley floor is wet/cultivated, trees are on the hills); it is blank ("edge of our district") or, more accurately for the low end, marsh. **`marsh(poly, role="pond_fringe")`** draws the same reeds as a **water-edge fringe** around a POND's shallow shore (the reservoir sits in the hill catchment - *satoyama*; its shore is reedy): `marsh` skips the open pond water (and the paddy), so a ring polygon rims the shore; `role="pond_fringe"` is EXEMPT from `marsh_on_low_ground` (it is a water margin, wherever the pond is, not the valley toe). The pond's CATCHMENT hill (the cut-over slope that feeds it) is just more `commons` scrub placed behind/around it. So a pond need not float in blank tan: a reed fringe + a little catchment scrub read it as a valley-head reservoir in its watershed. Sources: rice domesticated in "naturally marshy areas" + paddy-as-reclaimed-marsh (AAS "Rice, Technology, and History"); Tai-Lake polders diked from marsh/lake; abandoned paddies revert to wetland.
  - **A marsh is UNBUILDABLE - nothing SACRED or FUNERARY sits on it** (`sacred_and_graves_off_marsh`, GM 2026-07: "no one would ever put a graveyard - or a shrine - in a marsh"). You do not raise a hall or dig graves in a bog. TWO parts: (1) **placement** - `s.marsh(...)` now registers its `toe` footprint as a `block_polys` NO-BUILD keep-out (a `pond_fringe` shore ring is exempt), so collision-placed features (houses, wells, byres) avoid it, and a gen sites its hand-placed shrine/cemetery on DRY ground (the spur / high ground); (2) **the check** - `sacred_and_graves_off_marsh` fires if any `religious` hall or `cemeteries`/`mausoleums`/etc. center or footprint-corner falls inside a `toe` marsh poly. This bit Hikari's split-village rebuild: its SW Bishamon shrine + churchyard graveyard first landed on the broad hinterland toe marsh - moved onto the dry SW below the west block (and the redundant full-width toe dropped via `hinterland(marsh=False)`, since the per-block toe marshes already mark the wet outfalls). Teeth: `test_checks.py::test_sacred_and_graves_off_marsh_fires_and_passes_on_dry_ground`.
  - **DWELLINGS keep OFF the wet low TOE below the drain, too** (`dwellings_above_field_drain`, GM 2026-07: *"would there be farmhouses below the drainage ditch if south of it is marshland?"*). Same reasoning as sacred/graves-off-marsh but for HOUSES: the field drains to its lowest edge (the *akusui* collector ditch), and the ground DOWNSLOPE of that drain - reed marsh, or low reclaimed paddy, or the drainage *tameike* - is the wettest in the valley (it is not *always* marsh, but whatever it is, it is WET), so no farmhouse sits there. **"Below the drain" is not the same as "low ground", and only the check's own geometry decides** (it projects onto `meta.down_deg` and flags a house only within the drain's cross-slope SPAN). The engine models terrain as a TILTED PLANE: with `down_deg=90` height depends only on north-south position, so the wet toe is a horizontal band and the *elevation* boundary is a contour, not the ditch. A collector ditch is NOT a contour - it descends along its length to reach the *tameike* (measured: the drain's height-spread is 269px on Akagahara, 340px on Kikuta, 352px on Hoshigaoka - the "rectangular field = cross-slope drain" story is FALSE, do not repeat it). So on **Akagahara** the ground just south-west of the ditch is NOT marsh: it sits at the same height as rice growing 500px east (at y=1500 there is paddy at x945-1700), and marsh there would put the neighboring rice in a swamp. It is land at paddy elevation the fan does not water - unirrigated scrub, which is exactly why the strewn farmsteads sit on it. **Do not "fix" the gap** between a descending collector and the marsh's straight edge.
  - **Why the marsh abuts the ditch on Kikuta/Hoshigaoka/Ueda but not Akagahara** (GM asked 2026-07; the first TWO answers were wrong, so the verified reasoning is recorded here - and note both wrong answers were geometric theories invented without reading the gens, where the answer was sitting in a comment). **There are two different marsh paths, and that is the whole difference:** Kikuta/Hoshigaoka/Ueda **hand-author** their toe (`s.marsh([...])` with literal coordinates), deliberately drawing a GENEROUS region that OVERLAPS the crop - and since `s.marsh` skips any point on a paddy, the reeds fill up to the field's true low edge whatever its shape, so they abut the ditch. Akagahara/Ikegami/Moritono instead call **`s.hinterland()`**, which COMPUTES the toe from the cultivated extent with only `pad` (90px) of uphill overlap - not enough to reach the fan's diagonal west margin, so a wedge of bare ground shows below the collector's upper reach. **The wedge is nevertheless correct** for the reason above: that ground sits at the same height as rice growing to its east, so reeds there would put the neighboring crop in a swamp. The hand-drawn maps take the opposite liberty - Kikuta's marsh reaches height 1478, above its entire drain (1527..1866), so it paints reeds on ground at rice height; it is invisible only because the reed scatter skips paddies. Neither is a rule to copy blindly: **decide by what the ground IS**, not by which path drew it. FIXED 2026-07 (GM-directed): `hinterland()`'s toe is now a CONTOUR BAND perpendicular to `down_deg` spanning the valley's full width, instead of a bbox-keyed axis-aligned rect - the toe now rotates with the fall like every other feature, and for the axis-aligned callers the inner edge is unchanged (so the wedge is unaffected; it is real, not an artifact). Dwellings ring the DRY margins (the high N head + the W/E flanks) ABOVE the drain. **Scoped to DISPERSED maps** (like the per-house `all_houses_field_adjacent`): each strewn farm must INDIVIDUALLY sit on dry ground, whereas a NUCLEATED cluster is placed as a unit and governed by `cluster_abuts_fields` - and a tight cluster beside a DIAGONAL drain reads as "downslope" of the drain line without being in any wet toe, so scoping to dispersed avoids that false positive (it fired on nucleated **Ueda**, whose W-margin cluster sits SW of an NW-SE drain). A farm off to the SIDE, past the drain's ENDS, is a legit flank homestead and is NOT flagged (only the central toe below the drain is). Needs `meta.down_deg` + a drain ditch; skipped otherwise. Teeth: `test_checks.py::test_dwellings_above_field_drain_fires_on_a_toe_farm`; drove the Akagahara dispersed-hamlet build (its ring placement excludes the S toe below the drain).
  - **Towns and CITIES have NO toe marsh - the drainage-investment gradient (GM asked 2026-07-23; researched + source-verified).** The GM noticed the ground downhill of a drainage ditch is marshy on village/hamlet maps but not on town/city maps and asked whether that split is realistic. It is - and not by accident of code paths. *What the research found:* the real pattern is a **gradient of drainage investment and land value**. VILLAGES had neither the capital nor the incentive to eliminate every wet margin: residual wet ground at the low edge of village land is very well attested (Japanese *yatsuda* valley-bottom paddies were essentially managed marsh; reed beds were harvested village commons under *iriai* tenure - thatch, screens, annual burning; poorly drained *fukada* "deep fields" persisted until Meiji-and-later drainage projects), and crucially it was USED wet ground - reed bed, wet meadow, more paddy at the reclamation frontier - which is exactly what our sparse harvestable-reed rendering reads as. TOWNS/CITIES commanded corvee-scale hydraulic engineering and premium peri-urban land: ditch discharge went into an engineered moat/canal/river network (Suzhou's canal grid; Edo's canals and the immediate infill of the Hibiya inlet after 1590), and the outskirts were intensively worked (suburban vegetable belts fed by urban night soil; Edo-period reclamation waves around Osaka and Edo). Where wetness DID persist near a city it was **bounded and purposed** - a moat, a lotus/fish pond, a flood basin, a deliberate defensive inundation - never a diffuse seepage marsh below a ditch. *The decision:* village/hamlet gens keep the toe marsh (`hinterland()` or hand-placed); town/city gens clothe their margins with near-ring cropland + `role="grazing"` commons and draw NO toe marsh - and this is now the recorded reason that split is CORRECT, not an oversight. Note the split is enforced by which gens call what, not by a scale flag: `marsh()`/`hinterland()` have no scale guard. If a town/city map ever wants a wet accent, the historical form is an engineered feature tied to the moat/canal system (lotus/fish pond, flood basin) or the defensive belt below - never seepage marsh.
  - **Defensive marshland - the ENGINEERED wet belt (`marsh(poly, role="defense")`, `defense_marsh_girds_the_walls`; GM 2026-07-23; researched + source-verified; NO pool map uses it yet - a knob held ready for a future map).** The one historically-grounded way a town or city DOES carry marsh: a deliberately maintained wet belt as fortification. *What the research found:* the Northern Song built an artificial marsh-and-pond belt across the northern Hebei frontier (from 989, He Chengju's lake chain) specifically as anti-cavalry terrain; Japanese *numajiro* "marsh castles" (Bitchu Takamatsu, besieged by water 1582) used surrounding marsh as their primary defense; and flooded paddies around castle towns functioned as a de facto glacis. The constant: peri-urban wetness that survives is PURPOSED - so a defensive belt is military ground, not waste. *Usage rules (all enforced by the check):* hand-author `s.marsh(poly, role="defense")` OUTSIDE the wall circuit, hugging the perimeter - the belt begins at the moat's outer bank / wall foot, within ~60px of the wall or moat line (~180 ft at city scale: moat + patrol berm; a bog detached from the fortification defends nothing), and only on a map that HAS a wall or moat. It is exempt from `marsh_on_low_ground` (it follows the circuit wherever the wall runs, not the valley toe) and from `roads_clear_of_marsh` (an approach road through the belt is a CAUSEWAY - the corridor skip keeps the tread bare - and few, constricted approaches are the belt's military purpose). It stays UNBUILDABLE (`block_polys` keep-out, same as the toe) and stays a bog for `sacred_and_graves_off_marsh`. Tests: `test_settlement.py::test_marsh_defense_role_records_and_blocks_building` + `::test_marsh_rejects_an_unknown_role`; `test_checks.py::test_defense_marsh_girds_the_walls_*` (no-perimeter / inside-the-circuit / detached / hugging-the-moat), `::test_marsh_on_low_ground_ignores_a_defense_belt`, `::test_roads_clear_of_marsh_exempts_a_defense_belt_causeway`.
- **Hinterland - the SCRUB-dominant catena, with a FEW woodland patches (China-first)** (`s.hinterland(down_deg=None)`, GM 2026-07). The marsh + commons catena above was, for the villages, hand-placed per map. `hinterland()` makes it a **standard step**: given the cultivated extent + `down_deg` (defaults to `meta`'s), it drops a reed **MARSH** at the **downhill TOE** (a band just beyond the field's downhill edge - the low, undrained valley toe below the drainage line) and fills the surrounding non-arable margins with the **cut-over SCRUB commons** (`role="grazing"`: coarse grass + a FEW scraggly pines). **CHINA-FIRST is the key rule here (do not re-litigate): the south-China rice hills were stripped for fuel/timber over ~1,000 years, so the DOMINANT cover past the settlement is denuded scrub / rough grazing, NOT forest.** The protected fengshui grove is the green exception, and managed **WOODLAND** (coppice / bamboo / tung-oil / tea-oil "economic forest") is a **FEW discrete PATCHES** the gen adds by hand on the higher / farther ground (`s.commons([...], role="woodland")`), SET BACK from the sun-needing crops by the scrub between. So hinterland lays the scrub + marsh; the woodland patches are per-map. Realism rules baked in: (1) the scrub clothes ALL non-arable open ground, INCLUDING the voids an irregular field leaves inside its own bbox - not just the outer margin strips (see "Interior fill" below); the check `commons_clear_of_paddies` tests the DRAWN OUTCOME (does the patch cover any open ground?), NOT the bbox centroid - the scatter cannot draw on a paddy by construction, so a centroid test was a wrong proxy that failed a legitimate interior fill; (2) the **dwellings stay CLEAR of cover** via a **keep-out** (`avoid=`) - but its SHAPE is form-dependent: a NUCLEATED cluster keeps out its house-bbox (one tight blob), while a DISPERSED settlement keeps out **each homestead individually** (house + yard + garden + grove), because the bbox of ringing farmsteads is the whole map and would forbid all cover (`commons()` and `marsh()` both take `avoid=`, a list of keep-out polygons). `down_deg` orients BOTH the marsh (a CONTOUR BAND perpendicular to the fall, spanning the valley - not an axis-aligned box; see the `dwellings_above_field_drain` note) and which frame side the scrub ring omits.
  - **Interior fill (the fan-void fix, GM 2026-07).** The scrub ring lays strips only OUTSIDE the cultivated bbox, but an irregular field (a comb FAN) does not fill its own bbox - it leaves open VOIDS INSIDE it. Those voids used to render as BARE ground (nothing clothed them: the strips are outside them, the marsh is a contour band below them), which read as conspicuous "empty space" (the only uncovered land on the map). `hinterland()` now also scatters scrub over the WHOLE cultivated bbox; since the scatter already skips every field/lane/watercourse/building/keep-out, it lands ONLY in those voids, clothing them as the rough grazing they are (ground the crop does not use is still grazed). This RETIRED the old per-map hack (Akagahara/Ikegami used to hand-add a `role="grazing"` quad to fill the fan corner). This applies across scales: hamlets call `hinterland()`; the villages that have the water-first hinterland (Hoshigaoka, Ueda) hand-place their scrub + marsh and just add the `role="woodland"` patches on their high ground. (Kikuta / Hikari are the older DISPERSED villages with per-farmstead groves and no surrounding hinterland at all - they would need the full scrub+marsh+patches treatment added if wanted.)
  - **Three DISTINCT `commons` looks so land types read APART at a glance (the GM's rule - grass and woods must NOT look the same).** The bug this fixed: a single grass-plus-scattered-pine glyph made "grazing" and "forest" indistinguishable. Now `s.commons(poly, role=...)` draws three clearly different textures: **`role="woodland"`** = individual, spaced tree CROWNS, an OPEN coppice canopy (gaps show) - clearly TREES, the managed-wood PATCHES, but lighter/more-open than the dense DARK closed-canopy fengshui village **grove** (so those two also stay distinct); **`role="commons"` (default) / `"grazing"`** = the cut-over fuel/fodder SCRUB, grass + a FEW scraggly pines - the DOMINANT denuded-hill cover; **`role="pasture"`** = OPEN grass tufts + brush, NO trees (available for a purely-grazed margin, not used by the scrub-dominant default). So a map reads distinct covers: **marsh** (blue-green reeds, wet) / **scrub** (grass + sparse scraggly pines, dominant) / **woodland** (open green crown clumps, the few patches) / **grove** (dark closed canopy, the fengshui exception). `pasture` and `woodland` are general hinterland land, so - like `grazing` - they are **exempt from `commons_beyond_the_windbreak`** (only the fuel/fodder `commons` proper follows the village->grove->commons toposequence); `commons_clear_of_paddies` still applies to all.
  - **Placement mechanics.** The scrub is placed as picture-frame side-strips of the CULTIVATED bbox (paddy + dry plots) on the three non-toe sides (the toe side is the marsh), each strip lying OUTSIDE the bbox so its centroid clears the paddy. The scatter SKIPS fields/pond/lanes/houses + the hamlet keep-out and BLEEDS off the frame (neither marsh nor commons is a `_CROP_HARD` crop anchor), so it fills only the OPEN ground and the frame still hugs the hard content. **Call it AFTER fields + cluster + pond + dry fields, BEFORE the windbreak grove** (so the dense grove draws over the sparse cover) **and before `crop_to_content`**; the `role="woodland"` patches go AFTER (crowns on top of the scrub). The interior fill (above) now clothes the comb-FAN voids automatically, so a strongly one-sided fan (Ikegami's NE, Akagahara's SW) no longer needs a hand-added scrub quad; the `role="woodland"` patches are still per-map on the high ground.
