The pond-fringe reeds (`marsh role='pond_fringe'`) and the village funerary/back-slope hand-placements still layer on top as before. Tests: `test_settlement.py::test_hinterland_scrub_ring_and_marsh_downhill_each_cardinal` (four cardinal branches), `test_hinterland_honors_hamlet_keepout_and_dry_plot_extent` (keep-out + dry extent + avoid-skip), `test_hinterland_flags_default_downdeg_and_empty_field`, `test_commons_glyph_variants_draw_and_record_each_role` (glyphs + avoid); `test_checks.py::test_commons_beyond_the_windbreak_exempts_general_hinterland_land`.
  - **Woodland patches must not OVERLAP crops nor BLOCK THEIR LIGHT** (`woodland_clear_of_crops`, GM 2026-07 - both the placement and the check enforce it). Two rules: (1) a coppice patch never overlaps a field (a canopy over the crop competes for root/light); (2) the SUN is to the south (maps are north-up), so a tree casts its shadow toward the NORTH - a patch may sit just **north/beside** a crop, but on the crop's **SOUTH (sunny) side** it must stand a canopy's-shadow back or it shades the field. Covers BOTH the paddy and the dry *hatake* plots. The check uses a fixed crown-radius no-overhang `CLEAR` (~14px) everywhere plus a scaled shadow-reach `SHADE` (~14 + 55 ft/px) on any crop's south side (a patch south of a crop with an x-overlapping shadow column). WHY this matters: Ikegami/Hoshigaoka first shipped with coppice patches placed "above the paddy" that actually landed ON the dry-hatake ribbon (the hatake is the N hem, *above* the paddy) - the GM caught trees sitting on and shading the dry crops. So place woodland on the genuinely-open high ground (north of / beside the fields, clear of the dry ribbon), not merely "off the paddy." Helper `poly_gap(a, b)` (0 if two polys overlap/cross, else min distance) backs the check. Teeth: `test_checks.py::test_woodland_clear_of_crops_fires_on_overlap_and_shade_passes_when_set_back_north` + `test_poly_gap_overlap_containment_edgecross_and_separated`.
  - **A woodland patch is a DISTINCT wood from the fengshui GROVE** (`woodland_clear_of_grove`, GM 2026-07). The coppice-commons patches and the protected village grove (`village_groves` - windbreak / water-mouth / copse) must NOT overlap, or the two woods merge into one indistinct green mass. The check fires if any grove clump center falls inside a patch or within the clump's drawn radius of it; place the coppice on its OWN stretch of high ground. This bit Hoshigaoka: an NW pond-catchment patch landed on the west windbreak belt - moved to the N ridge (east of the title bay), well clear. So the map keeps separate woods-textures: the dense dark fengshui grove (near the cluster) vs the lighter open coppice patches (on the far high ground) vs the sparse scrub (dominant). Teeth: `test_checks.py::test_woodland_clear_of_grove_fires_when_on_the_fengshui_grove`.
  - **A settlement can have a real FOREST as its wood (Moritono), and the hinterland adapts** (GM 2026-07). Moritono - the forest-hamlet - was redone to-scale water-first: it keeps its distinctive `forest()` (the Shirin Forest filling the EAST, the high wooded ground + the water SOURCE) and the magistrate's `manor()` at the forest's edge, with water flowing **E->W** (`down_deg=180`) - a source *tameike* fed by a brook out of the forest, the comb paddy draining W to a marsh at the low toe. Three engine tweaks let `hinterland()` serve this: (1) the scrub `ring()` **clamps its bands to the canvas + a small bleed** instead of overshooting to `max(W,H)` - a wide E->W hamlet otherwise scattered a huge off-canvas apron of scrub that blew resvg's node limit; (2) `crop_to_content` now **frames to include a `forest`** (a point-list edge feature, so it sets the frame like `_CROP_HARD`) and **clamps the view to the canvas** so an edge feature that fills to the map edge is not cropped off and does not "stop short" of the frame (the brook likewise runs to the E map edge, `frm={"kind":"offmap"}`, under the forest that is drawn over it); (3) a new **`hinterland(skip_sides=(...))`** param can suppress the scrub band on a named frame flank (e.g. a forest side) - Moritono in the end does NOT skip: the E rough ground (around the manor, between the pond and the forest) reads better carrying scrub, and the scatter already skips the forest/manor/pond blocks, so the wood, the walls, and the water stay clear while the grazing fills the gap.
- **Crop mix + paddy SURFACE: an irrigated valley is rice-dominant, and a wet paddy is a flooded sheet, not ruled rows** (drives the `paddy_field` crop roll and `_paddy_surface` vs `_rows`).
  - *Ratio - ~85% wet rice, dry crops on the MARGINS.* An irrigated valley village exists BECAUSE its land is waterable for rice, so its watered commons run overwhelmingly PADDY (~85%); dry upland crops (barley/*mugi*, soy, vegetables) cluster on the **higher, harder-to-water rim** - so dry/soy probability RISES toward the field edge (`edge_dist`), the well-watered interior all paddy, not scattered evenly. This share is deliberately ABOVE the ~55-60% paddy of Japan's *national* Edo arable - that average is dragged down by whole dry-upland regions (Kanto uplands, mountains) a watered valley common does not represent.
  - *One stage, not a rainbow.* A village transplants TOGETHER - shared irrigation water (released on one schedule) + exchanged labor (*yui*) - so at any moment its paddies are **largely a SINGLE growth stage** (default high-summer green, `PADDY_SHADES`), with only MINOR spread: a few early/late-variety plots, the odd low-lying plot still flooded (`FLOODED_SHADES`, ~6%), a rare early-ripening gold (`RIPE_SHADES`, ~5%). Do NOT scatter every stage evenly - that reads as a crop *mix*, not a rice village. (Corrected error: an earlier 22% flooded / 12% gold looked mixed.)
  - *Nitrogen - the paddy makes its OWN, so soy is food not fertiliser.* A flooded paddy is near self-sustaining for nitrogen: the standing water hosts N-fixing **cyanobacteria + *azolla*** and the **irrigation water carries in silt/nutrients** from upstream - which is why paddies crop continuously for centuries where dry-field monoculture exhausts the soil. Legumes entered as **winter green manure grown IN the drained paddy** (*renge* / Chinese milk vetch, plowed under before spring flooding) + applied night soil / ash / fish-and-oilseed cake - NOT soy on the margins washing in. So the ~6% soy is a **food crop** (dry fields, and characteristically on the paddy bunds - *aze-mame*, "ridge beans"), NOT the paddy's nitrogen supply.
  - *Surface - flooded, not rows.* A wet paddy is a flooded, mottled sheet (irregular hand-transplanted shoots + a water sheen), NOT the crisp parallel rows of **checkrow planting (*seijoue*), a Meiji improvement** - premodern transplanting was irregular, so ruled rows on a paddy are the same era-tell as the consolidation grid. At 2 ft/px a real ~30 cm transplant spacing is sub-pixel anyway, so rows would be invisible; `_paddy_surface` gives rice a sparse shoot-mottle + sheen. *Density (GM 2026-07-23 follow-up):* traditional transplanting set hills (clumps of 2-6 seedlings) at ~20-30 cm centers - ~1 hill per sq ft, ~40-45k per acre - so even at the hamlet scale's 1 ft/px, one-dot-per-plant means one dot per PIXEL: a literally solid fill, not a dense texture. Any drawable mottle is therefore a sample; the GM chose a denser one for the polder-leftover parcels (where rice must read distinctly against fish ponds): `_paddy_surface(pitch=4.5)` draws a JITTERED GRID - centers ~4.5 px apart, alternate rows half-offset, +-1.5 px jitter, ~10% dropout - so shoot spacing lands irregular in the ~3-6 px band with no ruled row or column (a ~1-in-20 sample). The comb maps' original sparse random scatter (pitch=None) is byte-stable and untouched; whether to bring the jittered-grid mottle to ordinary comb paddies is a separate, deliberately-unmade decision. **DRY plots keep `_rows`** - barley/soy/veg WERE ridge-and-furrow row-cultivated, so rows are correct there, and the wet/dry contrast is itself realistic and legible.
    - *WHY rows waited for Meiji when row planting is ancient (GM 2026-07-23 - the idea was never the bottleneck, the economics were).* Dry-crop rows are FREE: the seed goes into a plowed furrow, and the furrow IS the row. Wet rice is TRANSPLANTED into a puddled flooded sheet with no furrows and no guide lines, so rows must be PURCHASED - marked ropes or a rolled gridding frame, plus every planter aligning to them - and the bill lands in the year's tightest labor window (the whole village transplants in days, on a shared water schedule). And for centuries the purchase bought nothing: rows pay when a tool travels BETWEEN them (the ancient dry-field hoe/cultivator), but nothing could travel between rows in a flooded paddy - weeding was by hand and foot either way. What changed in Meiji was the arrival of the between-rows tool for mud: the hand-pushed ROTARY PADDY WEEDER, which only works on plants ruled in both directions - so *seijoue* + marking frame + rotary weeder spread as ONE package, pushed by state extension hard enough that police sometimes stood over farmers to enforce straight lines ("saber farming"), itself evidence the private payoff was marginal before the full package. Traditional transplanting was NOT chaos though: clump spacing was roughly even (a practiced hand keeps density consistent - density drives yield), just never ruled - which is exactly what the sparse unruled shoot-mottle renders.
- **In-field ditch geometry must be VALID, not just drawn** (drives `water_channels_obtuse_turns` + `field_ditches_terminate`; `water_field` records each ditch in `M["field_ditches"]` with its role). Two rules a real ditch obeys: (1) **obtuse turns only** - a canal/ditch never makes an acute (<90 deg) bend; an acute hairpin implies impossible topology (water can't flow round it). Checked at every interior vertex of every watercourse (channels, streams, and the in-field ditches): the incoming and outgoing segments must not fold back (dot >= 0). This is why the MAIN/DRAIN follow the SMOOTHED field boundary (gentle curves) rather than a jagged per-column scan. (2) **every ditch terminates at a ditch that leaves the field** - no channel runs to the middle of a paddy and dead-ends; each LATERAL's two ends sit on the MAIN or DRAIN, which are in turn fed by a pond channel / emptied off-map, so the whole net exits to the pond or the map edge. (3) **the whole net traces to a source AND a sink** (`field_ditches_reach_source_and_sink`): the watercourse graph (channels + streams + ditches, joined crossing-aware) must put every ditch in a component holding a POND-fed segment AND a runoff (off-map/stream) segment - a ditch tied to nothing outside the field, or one that never drains, fails. IMPLEMENTATION TRAP that motivated the termination check: a lateral must END *on* the main/drain, NOT overshoot it - an earlier version stubbed 0.5*plot past them, leaving dead-ends poking at the field edge that read as "ditches that don't drain". Negative fixtures: `pool/regressions/water_ditches_acute_turn_and_dead_end.json`, `.../water_lateral_stubs_overshoot_kikuta.json`.
- **Water-first fields (`water_field`): the irrigation network is the SKELETON; plots, crops, and colors are all DERIVED from it** so the map actually communicates the hydrology (drives `water_field`; the villages use it instead of `paddy_field`). WHY this replaced the layered approach: doing the patchwork, the crop roll, and the ditches as three INDEPENDENT random processes read as random no matter how each was tuned - the eye only sees structure when the pieces descend from ONE model. `water_field` takes a `source` (the pond, high side) and `drain` (low side / a lower field to cascade into) and builds, in the contour/fall-line frame: a SINGLE MAIN canal along the high edge fed from the pond at ONE point, SPARSE lateral ditches (every ~4-6 plot-columns) branching DOWN off it to a DRAIN ditch at the low edge - a connected TREE; it records a feed channel (pond->field) and a drain channel (field->drain). Then: (a) PLOTS are carved to the network (aligned to the laterals) - so they cannot help but follow the water; (b) CROP is a function of water-position - rice through the irrigated body (the paddies BETWEEN laterals are fed plot-to-plot, **田越し tagoshi CASCADE** - so MOST paddies having no direct ditch is CORRECT, not a bug), DRY only ABOVE the main canal / BELOW the drain (gravity cannot flood there) + the field MARGINS - ~85-90% rice. HISTORY: pre-modern used cascade + sparse branching distribution ditches; a ditch beside every paddy (用排水分離) is a MODERN (Meiji 耕地整理) improvement, anachronistic here; (c) COLOR is ~ONE green (`RICE_GREENS`, three near-identical) so a rice field READS as one crop, flooded blue-green only along the head/drain where water pools, gold rare. RNG-isolated (decorative, no placement ripple). Kikuta: pond -> west+central (drain off-map SE) + midnorth (cascades into central). KNOWN residual: the grain holds a uniform ~45deg (the fixed NW-SE fall line) - real valleys let it DRIFT; and the settlement still necklaces the fields + the field OUTLINES are still blobs (the two structural items not yet done).
- **Water-first fields v2 - the WARP-THREAD engine (`waterfields.py`; SUPERSEDES the v1 bullet above).** WHY v2 exists: v1 still started from a field SHAPE (bbox -> blob) and fitted water to its boundary - and boundary-following water is a RING by definition, which is why the GM kept seeing orphan ditches and ring-shaped mains no matter how v1 was tuned. The construction was wrong, not the tuning. v2 inverts it: the water network is grown FIRST and the field is EMERGENT - every plot-column boundary is a thread marched downhill in lockstep fall-steps, clamped so threads can never cross and never pinch below one plot width; the blue delivery ditch is a thread's dug PREFIX (plain bund below); offtakes SPAWN from their parent thread mid-march so they always take off on the parent's real path; the drain is a gently-descending line fitted under the DEEPEST dug ends (shallower columns just cascade further to reach it). Crossings, orphan ditches, and acute turns are impossible BY CONSTRUCTION, not repaired after. Plots are carved between adjacent threads (contour-parallel bund rows; boundary falls back to the parent canal above a takeoff and to the drain below an end, so plots hug the canal and reach the collector without ever spilling past either). The build process is BY-EYE-FIRST: each assembly stage is approved by eye, then the checks are backfilled as ratchets around the accepted look. New water-first maps stage in `pool/wip/` (outside the test glob) during that build, then GRADUATE into `pool/` once complete - the base case **`pool/villages/hoshigaoka`** has graduated (it now runs in `test_villages` and passes the full gate). `waterfields.py` itself stays outside the coverage gate (the emergent geometry is validated by the manifest checks + a regression corpus, not line coverage).
- **Water-first v2 historical grounding (researched + source-verified 2026-07; this is the record - do not re-research).**
   - **Pond**: a valley-head *tameike* behind an earthen dike, sitting ABOVE its fields ("located at a valley head and constructed by dividing off the valley mouth with an earthen dike... at elevations higher than the surface of the paddy fields they serve" - Tabayashi 1986, Geographical Review of Japan 60(1)). ONE outlet: an inclined intake (shahi) feeding a bottom conduit (sokohi) through the dam; the spillway is flood-safety, never distribution (Kagawa pref. tameike docs). Parent/child pond linkage (oyaike/koike, Kagawa; "melon-on-the-vine" in China) is attested flavor for larger systems.
   - **Distribution**: sluice -> head-race -> division point (bunsuiguchi) -> a branching TREE. "Main canals **gradually decrease in size as they are tapped by branch canals**" (Tabayashi) - hence the drawn taper. The smallest ditches "are often considered parts of the paddy fields they serve" - hence ditch-as-plot-boundary. SPARSE is correct: a village digs the minimum network; a ditch beside every paddy (yohaisui bunri) is a Meiji land-readjustment (1899/1905) anachronism.
   - **Layout modes** (terrain-driven; the GM wants all three eventually):
     - **COMB (the default)**: supply canals along the HIGH margins, delivery ditches perpendicular down-slope, one drain along the low line. Grounding: the Edo Kishu-school layout (Minuma-dai 1728: supply on the elevated margins, drainage channel on the lowest line, water reused downstream) AND codified Chinese canal doctrine (mains along contours/ridges on high ground, field channels perpendicular to contours). Chinese *beitang* pond systems - the direct tameike analogue - were THE dominant village-scale mode in rice China (8.3M ponds serving ~39% of irrigated area into the 1950s, ~71% in hilly regions); the GM chose the Chinese default deliberately (Rokugan demographics anchor to Song/Ming China).
     - **FAN (supported option, not default)**: gently-descending canals radiating from a valley-mouth apex - the Dujiangyan / Tedori-alluvial-fan geometry. Correct where the land fans out below the pond.
     - **JORI GRID (future option, recorded on GM request - NOT implemented)**: from the 7th century much of Japan's long-settled PLAINS carried an astronomically-oriented 109 m grid (jori-sei: 1-cho squares in 6x6-ri blocks, cut into ~12 x 109 m tan strips). A plains village in an ancient core province shows semi-regular GRIDDED paddies, not organic patchwork - Rokugan analog: ancient heartland provinces (e.g. Crane/Phoenix cores). The organic warp-thread patchwork is correct for terrain-following villages like Kikuta.
   - **Delivery-ditch SPACING - sparse, min-gapped, real Y-junctions.** The cascade reaches ~4-5 plots laterally, so a ditch every ~6-9 columns waters everything - the SPARSE pre-modern norm (a ditch beside every few paddies drifts toward the Meiji ditch-per-plot grid we avoid). Two placement rules keep it honest: (1) **min-gap** - a delivery ditch that takes off within ~2 plot-columns of another is DROPPED (`min_gap = 2*plot_across`; two ditches watering the same ground twice read as an artifact, not design); (2) **Y-junctions diverge** - a sub-branch takes off HIGH on its parent and heads off STEEPLY (bigger heading, longer run) so the two channels end up >~2 columns apart, a real fork serving a distinct sub-block, NOT two ditches running adjacent for a stretch. Only the INTERIOR (widest) blocks split. `offtakes_a`/`offtakes_b` control the count - keep the net sparse (Hoshigaoka: 3 canal-A offtakes + 1 sub + canal B + 1 canal-B offtake = ~6 ditches, gaps ~4-6 columns). Irregular gaps are CORRECT (terrain, fragmented holdings); evenly-gridded is the anachronism.
   - **Delivery ditches TAPER as they feed the paddies (`delivery_ditches_taper`).** A delivery ditch sheds its water into the paddies ALL ALONG its length, so the flow it carries dwindles - and so must its width, narrowing from full at the canal takeoff to a THREAD at the point where it has delivered the last of its water and stops. This is Tabayashi's "channels gradually decrease in size as they are tapped" rule (already applied to the supply canals) extended to the delivery ditches themselves - which are tapped continuously by the plots they feed. A blunt constant-width ditch that stops dead reads as jarring AND is less accurate. Implementation: `waterfields` tags each delivery ditch with `w` (head) + `w_tail` (thread), the gen draws it as decreasing-width stroked pieces (round caps soften the joins into a smooth narrowing) and records both widths on the field-ditch. `delivery_ditches_taper` gates it: every `role:"branch"` ditch with recorded widths must have `w_tail < ~0.85*w` (older `water_field` maps record no widths and are exempt). NOTE: canal B was reclassified `role:"main"` (it is a SUPPLY canal that narrows past its offtakes, like canal A) so the taper check targets only the true delivery ditches.
   - **Cascade (tagoshi)**: water passes plot-to-plot through bund cuts; MOST paddies touch no ditch; delivery ditches serve block heads and the water walks down the columns. Keep every plot within ~4-5 plots of a ditch laterally, AND cap the vertical tail: a dug ditch extends until no column cascades more than ~8-11 rows past its end to the collector (the engine enforces both). Ditches ENDING MID-FIELD is correct and intentional - the end is the delivery point to the block below, not a bug HEAD PLOTS must ABUT the supply canal (they take water directly from it through bund cuts - the first cascade link); the 'don't plant above the canal' guard is judged by the plot CENTROID, never per-vertex (a per-vertex test silently discards every canal-touching head plot, since their top edge sits ON the canal - this was a real bug, 8/552 plots touching the canal until fixed). (the string-depth bound is judgment, not a sourced number - the only unsourced quantity in this section).
   - **Drain (akusui)**: a dug collector below the DEEPEST delivery ends, exiting downstream; separate supply/drain at SYSTEM level is Edo-attested (Kishu school), per-plot separation is Meiji. Downstream reuse (next village / child ponds) is historical. FLOODED (blue) accents are reserved ENTIRELY for the closing rank that sits ON the collector, so every blue plot ABUTS the drain and reads as pooling-before-outfall, never a stranded mid-field reservoir (blue = flooded rice, not a separate water body); the closing rank's bottom vertices are SNAPPED onto the drain line (sample the drain fall at each vertex's own u, not the row top) so the field edge sits on the ditch. The whole field edge MUST abut the collector: the CLOSING RANK hems every column down to its OWN drain fall (the drain is diagonal, so the triangle between the last uniform row and the drain varies across a sector - filling only the deep side near each ditch leaves a sliver), backed by a final HEM PASS that walks the drain and fills any residual gap up to the existing field.
   - **The drain's two ENDS - water IN and water OUT, and neither dangles.** A drain cannot just STOP in bare ground - conservation of flow. (1) The HEAD (high end) begins AT the field's low SW corner (the westmost delivery-ditch bottom), NOT extended out to the boundary thread and beyond into open land - a collector starts where the first field drains in; the hem pass covers any SW-corner sliver. (An earlier `- uniform(12,26)` stub poked past the field into bare ground and read as "the ditch just stops" - removed.) (2) The OUTFALL (low end) discharges to a runoff SINK: historically paddy drainage is **reused by the next village downstream** or **returns to the valley's natural stream/river** (a standalone "drainage pond" is NOT the typical destination). So the akusui empties into a natural valley **BROOK** (`net["brook"]`, drawn `s.stream(..., frm={"kind":"drain"}, to={"kind":"offmap"})`, meandering, a touch wider than the dug ditch) that carries the water off-map DOWNHILL - **water IN via the pond feeder, water OUT via the brook**. The brook is grown only when the outfall sits INSIDE the frame (else the drain discharges off-map directly). A stream anchored `frm:"drain"` is a first-class case (the `anchored()` helper + `stream_runs_off_edge` accept a brook that starts at a drain outfall).
   - **Water SOURCES come from the map EDGE, not out of nowhere.** The pond does not generate water - its FEEDER must flow IN from off-map. Draw it as a real recorded `s.stream([edge_pt, ..., pond_pt], frm={"kind":"offmap"}, to={"kind":"pond"})` that starts at the map boundary (NOT the old decorative `pond(stream_curve=...)` stub, which floated above the pond and escaped every check). `pond_fed_from_edge` gates it: any stream whose end sits in the pond must have its FAR end at the map edge (a sole-storage / rain-fed pond with no feeder is exempt). So the whole valley reads coherently: feeder in from the edge -> pond -> sluice -> canals -> field -> drain -> brook out to the edge.
   - **The drainage matches the SLOPE, and the FIELD is BOUNDED in the frame (`field_fall`).** The march used to grow the field downhill until the threads left the MAP CORNER (`f_stop = max fall over corners + 300`), so the field filled the frame to the low corner and then SPILLED off the edge - and the drain outfall landed at that clipped far-corner, reading as "the water just stops." `build_comb(..., field_fall=N)` **caps** the downhill march at N fall-units, so the paddy is sized to the **population** (~83 acres for Hoshigaoka) and bounded WITHIN the frame with a low-side margin. Then the outfall lands at the field's true **downhill (max-fall) corner** - verify it: the outfall's fall value must EXCEED the head's (higher fall = lower elevation), so water flows head->outfall consistent with `down_deg` - and the brook discharges from there off the **downhill** map edge (for Hoshigaoka's NW-high/SE-low slant, off the SE/E). Because a capped field also stops spreading cross-slope, **size the FRAME to hold village + field + the low-side margin** (Hoshigaoka went 1980x1320 -> 2420x1560 so the whole 83-acre paddy, its outfall, and the brook all sit in-frame). This resolved the "drainage doesn't go anywhere / runs the wrong way" report: with the field in-frame the drain visibly outfalls at the downhill corner and the brook runs off the SE edge.
   - **A drainage collector runs CROSS-slope, but water never runs UPHILL (`drain_flows_downhill` + `drainage_discharges_downhill`, keyed off `meta.down_deg`).** A subtlety worth stating because it looks wrong at a glance: the drain (akusui) legitimately runs ~PERPENDICULAR to the fall - it is a COLLECTOR crossing the field's low margin to gather runoff from every cascade column; a drain running *downhill* would follow one column and collect nothing. So "the drain runs cross-slope / looks perpendicular to the flow" is CORRECT, not a bug - do NOT add a rule forcing drains to run within X degrees of the slant (it would ban the very collectors that are historically right). On a **diagonal** slope (down_deg=45) this reads oddly: the outfall sits toward the far EAST (downhill on an NW-high map) which is also "up the page" (north), so the collector *looks* like it flows uphill though it does not. Three checks pin the real constraints (all from `meta.down_deg`, so record it on every sloped map): **`drain_runs_cross_slope`** REQUIRES the collector to run roughly perpendicular to the fall (along-fall fraction of its head->outfall vector < 0.65, i.e. >~50 deg off the fall) - a drain running straight downhill collects nothing and fails; **`drain_flows_downhill`** - the outfall (the end discharging to the brook / off-map) must be the LOWER-ground end (never uphill of the head); **`drainage_discharges_downhill`** - the brook must head downhill. Together they encode "a collector crosses the slope AND the water only ever moves downhill." The elevation-monotonic flow (cascade SE -> collector along the contour to the field's low corner -> brook SE off-map) is how real TERRACED / cascade valley fields drain - a contour drain meeting a valley stream at ~90 deg is normal side-drainage, not a bug. If the *perception* still misleads on a diagonal slope, fix READABILITY - NOT the hydrology (slope cues via topo lines look awkward and the channels are too thin for internal flow arrows, so those are out). The readability fix we DID adopt: the **brook CURVES out of the collector's exit heading** toward downhill over its first few steps, so the junction reads as the collector turning down the valley INTO the stream, not a hard ~90 deg corner. **`drainage_junction_smooth`** gates it: for each drain-fed brook, the turn between the drain's ARRIVAL heading (into the shared outfall) and the brook's DEPARTURE heading - each averaged over ~40px so short jittery segments do not fool it, matched to the nearest drain within 40px - must be < 65 deg. Robust by construction: it only fires on an actual drain-fed brook, skips if no drain is within reach, and the 65 deg threshold sits well clear of a real gentle bend (<30 deg) yet catches a right-angle corner (~90 deg).
   - **The connectivity check is CASCADE / ROLE-aware** (`field_ditches_reach_source_and_sink`, made role-aware for the warp-thread model). The old check required each ditch's ONE connected component to hold both a pond source and a runoff sink - correct for the Meiji end-on-drain model, but WRONG for tagoshi: here the delivery ditches END mid-field and the water cascades plot-to-plot to the drain (which sits offset ~32-48px BELOW the ditch ends), so supply and drain are SEPARATE networks bridged by the cascade, not by a ditch. The check now grounds by ROLE: a **supply** ditch (main/branch/lateral) must trace to the pond **source**; the **drain** must trace to a runoff **sink** (off-map / stream / brook). Both water models pass. To satisfy it the gen records the pond->head-race feed (`frm:pond`) and the drain->off-map (or ->brook) discharge as anchored `channels`. Teeth: `test_checks.py::test_field_ditches_reach_source_and_sink_fires_when_ungrounded` (a supply ditch with no source + a drain with no sink both fire).
   - **Plots**: pre-modern 0.02-0.25 acre, irregular; v2 carves ~0.1-0.15 acre, ~9 scattered plots per household (fragmented holdings were normal). STRAIGHT rectangular channels/plots are post-1900 consolidation (the Tedori fan's ditches were only straightened in the early 1900s) - the organic waver is period-correct, do not "clean it up".
   - **Crop + COLOR - the paddy body is UNIFORM; color differences MEAN something or they are not drawn (GM decision).** A rice field is ONE crop at ONE transplant/growth stage, so the irrigated body is a **single uniform green** (`RICE_GREENS` collapsed to one value - the old plot-to-plot shade jitter denoted nothing, so it was removed; the **bund network + footpath dots** carry the structure, not color). The colors that REMAIN are the ones that carry a category: **FLOODED blue-green** = the low plots that sit ON the drain collector and pool water before the outfall (~2%, the closing rank only); **RIPE gold** = early-ripening, when a map opts into it (Hoshigaoka uses none); and the **dry-field (hatake) strip** on the upslope margin above the supply canal, where each plot is a DIFFERENT dry crop (barley / buckwheat / millet / soy) drawn in its own color + furrow direction (a fragmented mosaic of families' strips). So a reader can trust color: uniform green = the one rice crop; a different color = a real difference (draining-low / ripening / a dry crop). *(Kept `RICE_GREENS` as a 3-element list of the SAME value so `R.choice` consumes the RNG stream identically - the field geometry + FLOODED count stay byte-for-byte unchanged; only the body color went uniform.)* NOTE this is a v2 (`waterfields.py`) change; the v1 `water_field` in settlement.py (the not-yet-rebuilt dispersed maps) still carries its near-identical 3-green mottle until those maps are rebuilt.
   - **NOT terraces - bunded paddies on a gentle tilt.** Hoshigaoka is a GENTLE valley slope, and terraces (*tanada* - stacked level benches with risers) are a STEEP-slope feature. So the plot lines are **bunds** (*aze*: low water-retaining earth ridges that double as footpaths - the dotted lines are the walkable bund tops), NOT terrace risers; each plot is a separately-flooded level basin, distinct to the eye but not stepped. Dry plots have NO water-retaining bund, so their boundaries are subtler - marked by a narrow unplowed earthen **balk** (the thin stroke), boundary stones, and the crop + furrow-direction contrast (not string/posts, which are too ephemeral for a field line).
   - **Dry (hatake) plots are RECTANGLES laid out AGAINST THE CANAL they border - NOT the paddy's fall grid (GM, 2026-07).** Dry fields bordering a supply canal are surveyed *to the canal*: one edge runs ALONG the canal, the other PERPENDICULAR to it, extending upslope - clean rectangles. The original code built them in the paddy's contour/fall frame (columns down the fall, rows on the contour), and since the head-race runs ~42deg DIAGONAL to the fall, every plot came out sheared ~43deg to a parallelogram. (A first fix merely softened that lean while STILL orienting to the paddy - the GM correctly rejected it: the plots should be oriented to the CANAL, not the paddy.) FIX in `_dry_fields`: tile the plots by ARC-LENGTH along the canal polyline (`a_pts`), and build each as a rectangle offset PERPENDICULAR to the local canal tangent, upslope. Because the base rides the canal, adjacent plots abut continuously along it - **no shear (skew ~0), no steps** - and only the outer (upslope) edge is ragged. The plots therefore ORIENT WITH THE CANAL (near-horizontal along Hoshigaoka's head-race, near-vertical along Ueda's), which naturally differs from the 45deg paddy grid - that's correct. Keep them on the DRY side: both edges sit UPSLOPE of the canal (a thin `berm`), never dipping across onto the wet paddy. Only `waterfields.py` maps (Hoshigaoka, Ueda); `dry_plot_furrows_vary` + acres unchanged.
   - **Dry plots never lap onto a paddy - on MULTI-FAN maps the hem filters against the fans already recorded (2026-07-23, the Tango fe2-into-fe1 incident).** The berm keeps a hem off its OWN fan's rice, but a multi-fan map places each fan blind to the others, and only hand-tuned per-comb `dry_keepout` circles held their hems apart - hand tuning missed a spot on Tango, and fe2's hem punched two plots (13% and 42% of their area) into fe1's envelope. Dry-crop-on-rice is a contradiction in the water logic (the hem exists BECAUSE its ground is upslope of what the canal commands), so this is now automated: `waterfields.hem_on_paddy` is the ONE shared predicate (quad shrunk 15% toward centroid, so a legitimate berm-kiss or two abutting margins never fire - the same spirit as `no_structure_on_paddy`'s 3px rule), the generators (`draw_comb_field` + the city gens' `comb_field`) drop any hem plot that hits a previously recorded `M["fields"]` paddy outline (the manifest source, per the PLANK BRIDGES same-source doctrine), and `dry_plots_clear_of_paddies` gates it. Negative fixture: `pool/regressions/dry_plots_clear_of_paddies_fires_on_a_hem_quilt_in_the_neighbor_fan.json` (the real pre-fix Tango manifest).
   - **Slope is a KNOB (`down_deg`)**: Kikuta is NW-high (down=SE). Southern Lion lands slope S->N (Spine of the World to the south); Dragon the reverse; Unicorn W->E (Twilight Mountains west). NOTHING may assume SE - the whole frame (threads, carve, canals, drain) rotates with `down_deg`. **Ueda is the first NE-high variant (`down_deg=135`, SW flow), staged in `pool/wip/`** - a NE->SW field grows down-and-left, so it uses a TALLER frame; its population is drawn from `village_population()` (200-500, mode 350) off the seed - SEED=3 -> 300 inhabitants / 60 households, sized down from Hoshigaoka.
   - **Pond sizing (the rule)**: sole-storage tameike run ~2,000-2,500 m3 of storage per irrigated ha (typical depth 2-4 m); a STREAM-FED pond refilling 1-2x a season is comfortable at ~1,200-1,500 m3/ha. Hoshigaoka: 31.8 ha of paddy -> ~1.5 ha pond surface (rx=145, ry=92 px at 1px=2ft) ~ 47,000 m3 at ~3 m ~ 1,470 m3/ha + feeder stream. The first draft's 0.84 ha pond (~790 m3/ha) was honestly undersized - keep pond area proportional to command area.
   - **ONE field vs MANY (design note, GM-directed)**: the number of physical field BLOCKS is TERRAIN-driven - a broad gentle valley holds ONE contiguous expanse (the base case, Hoshigaoka); broken ground gives SEVERAL blocks with non-arable land between (2-3 blocks is historically normal, NOT an anomaly); hamlets are literally farmhouse clusters around scattered arable patches; towns and cities are ringed by many separate field patches. The engine already supports this: each field = one `build_comb` call with its own source/sluice/seed/params (siting the patches apart is the caller's job). Village roster: **Hoshigaoka** and **Kikuta** = the single-field base case (Kikuta's earlier "blighted second field" backstory was dropped in the GM-directed 2026-07 redo - it is now a clean single comb-field with the 7-torii Benten precinct); **Hikari no Sato** = a genuinely SPLIT multi-block village. (A future map wanting a fallow second block can still add one via a second `build_comb` call + `meta.fallow_implies_abandoned`.)
   - **Acreage from population (the sizing rule)**: a person eats ~1 koku/yr; pre-modern yield ~1.3 koku/tan; coarse grain fills part of the diet while ~45% of rice goes to tax -> ~0.8-1.0 tan gross paddy per person -> 350 people ~ 280-350 tan = **69-86 acres** (+ dry margins later). WIP Kikuta lands ~79 acres / ~600 plots.
   - **Dry fields (hatake) + bund beans (azemame) - the non-rice crops (researched + source-verified 2026-07; this is the record).** WHERE dry crops go: wet-rice villages sort by a topographic CATENA - irrigated paddy holds the flat valley bottom / plain; DRY fields (hatake) take the HIGHER, well-drained ground the water cannot command (river terraces, natural levees / micro-highs threading the plain, alluvial-fan edges, lower slopes, AND the slightly-raised ground the homesteads sit on); coppice woodland (satoyama) crowns the hills above. Sources: satoyama land-use literature ("wet-rice in the plains and valley bottoms... satoyama woodlands/grasslands for dry-field crops"; "large middle river terraces... large areas of crop fields and small areas of paddy"); Kanto-plain historical-GIS land-use studies. So dry fields are NOT one neat strip - historically they sit in SEVERAL positions, above all AROUND the houses ("each family has some paddy and some hatake", the household's dry plots near its home). *What v2 currently models, and the deliberate simplification:* only the UPSLOPE FRINGE above the supply canal (the dominant dry position on a water-rich valley floor). The levee and near-homestead hatake are DEFERRED to when farmhouses land - at that layer, scatter some dry plots by the homesteads too, don't leave them all in the top fringe.
     - **How much (the extent is a per-map KNOB, `dry_band` depth, NOT a constant).** Nationally, Edo / early-Meiji cultivated land ran ~55-60% paddy / ~40-45% dry field BY AREA - but that average is dragged UP by hill country with little irrigable land. A PRIME irrigated valley-bottom village (Hoshigaoka: pond-fed, nearly all flat ground commandable) is the opposite extreme: dry land is scarce and squeezed to the margins, so its dry fraction is LOW (Hoshigaoka ~11% of cultivated, a thin fringe). A drier / hill-flanked village runs a DEEP dry zone (toward the national average or past it). Hence `dry_band=(min,max)` upslope depth: default is the THIN fringe (water-rich valley, the base case); WIDEN it for dry/hill villages. Do NOT hardcode a single dry percentage - it swings with water availability, which is exactly the terrain knob.
     - **Crop structure - a fragmented PATCHWORK, not clean ribbons.** Holdings were scattered (each household ~several small non-contiguous plots) and multiple-/inter-cropped, so adjacent small plots carry DIFFERENT crops that cluster rather than forming full-height single-crop stripes. v2 assigns crop PER-PLOT with spatial coherence (usually inherit the neighbor, sometimes switch) so the margin reads as a mosaic. Crops (`DRY_CROPS`): barley/wheat (mugi), millet (awa/hie/kibi), buckwheat (soba), field soybean (daizu) - grain + pulses, the winter/dry-ground staples. These are FIELD-scale hatake, distinct from the dooryard KITCHEN garden (a separate, smaller near-house feature).
     - **Dry-field FURROWS vary PER PLOT (`dry_plot_furrows_vary`) - corrected 2026-07.** Dry crops are ridge-and-furrow row-cultivated (unlike the flooded paddy sheet). The FIRST version ran every plot's furrows ALONG the contour (`theta0`, tiny jitter) on the "ridge-along-contour dams rain / checks runoff" rationale - but that is a **STEEP-slope erosion measure**, and it made every plot's rows identical, so adjacent family strips were indistinguishable (the GM caught this). On a **GENTLE** valley margin contour ridging is not forced; the accurate look is the **patchwork quilt** - a fragmented mosaic where each family strip is plowed to its OWN orientation, so neighboring plots run their rows different ways. So v2 now gives each dry plot a furrow angle spread up to **~1.1 rad (~63 deg) either side of the contour** (never straight down-slope), assigned by dropping each plot's angle into the **widest gap** between the angles of its already-placed neighbors - which GUARANTEES a real separation from every neighbor (and uses exactly ONE `R` draw per plot, as the old contour code did, so the plot GEOMETRY is byte-identical - only the angles changed). `dry_plot_furrows_vary` gates it: no two EDGE-adjacent dry plots (centroids within ~50 px) may run within ~6 deg of the same direction (compared mod pi - a furrow is an undirected line). So a reader can tell adjacent plots apart by row direction as well as by crop color. **PARAMETERIZED both ways** via `build_comb(..., furrow_spread=RAD)` (the half-fan either side of the contour): the default **~1.1 rad** gives the gentle-valley patchwork (Hoshigaoka); a genuinely STEEP / terraced hill village passes a **small** value (e.g. `furrow_spread=0.06`) so the rows converge back onto the contour (ridge-along-contour erosion control). `build_comb` returns `furrows_vary` (True when `furrow_spread >= ~0.3`); the gen records it as `meta(dry_furrows_vary=...)`, and `dry_plot_furrows_vary` only REQUIRES variation when that flag is True - so a declared contour village's aligned rows are correct and do not fire the check. (One `R` draw per plot either way, so `furrow_spread` never perturbs the plot geometry - only the angles.)
     - **Azemame (bund soybeans) - drawn SYMBOLICALLY.** Soybeans grown on the paddy BUND crests (aze-mame, "ridge beans") were universal - a food crop wedged onto otherwise-idle bund tops (NOT the paddy's nitrogen supply; a flooded paddy fixes its own N via cyanobacteria/azolla - see the v1 nitrogen note). At 1px=2ft a single bund-top bean row is genuinely SUB-PIXEL, so it is drawn as a green BEAD line along a fraction (`bean_frac`, ~0.28) of the paddy bunds - an honest legibility SYMBOL, not to literal scale (unlike the dry plots, paddies, and water, whose footprints ARE to scale). GM chose the beaded-bund representation over alternatives.
   - **Free lore hooks from the sources** (for /law, /calendar, village details): drought rotation in fixed village turns; water-heads (mizugashira) elected to run the flow; supply turns timed by BURNING INCENSE STICKS (senkomizu); upstream villages leveraging position in water disputes; a village trading pond-management duty for water rights.
   - Sources: Tabayashi 1986 (jstage grj1984b/60/1), Kagawa pref. tameike structure pages, JSIDRE on Minuma-dai, MAFF agricultural-water history PDF, Shiroyone terraced-paddies (NIES), jori-sei (Japanese Wiki Corpus + Tsukuba field-trace surveys), beitang studies (Nature Comms 2023; Jiang-Huai pond irrigation, PMC6695888), Chinese canal-layout doctrine (灌溉渠道 refs).
- **Irrigation topology: ONE pond outlet that BRANCHES, not independent taps** (informs the `channel()` layout). A *tameike* reservoir has a SINGLE outlet sluice (*hi*) - occasionally two - so all its irrigation leaves by one main channel (*yosui*) that then FORKS downstream to the fields, dividing at junctions; it is NOT several independent pipes drilled into the pond. So route every pond channel from a **single shared outlet point** on the pond's field-facing foot (Kikuta: `OUTLET`), forking out to the commons. Full topology: feeder stream -> pond (storage) -> one outlet -> main channel branching to fields -> in-paddy distributary/drainage ditches (`irrigation_ditch`) -> drainage off the low side.
- **Water-width ladder: ditches, creeks, streams, moats, ponds** (drives `irrigation_channels_hairline`, `watercourses_wider_than_ditches`, `moat_is_heaviest_watercourse`, `moat_dwarfs_ditches`, and the `channel()` / `stream()` / `moat()` / pond-feeder drawn widths). Wet-rice water systems are a strongly **tiered, self-similar hierarchy**: each tier steps up ~2-4x in width because channel width scales with the square-root of the command-area flow it carries. The honest real-world ladder, off the smallest field ditch:

  | Tier | Real width | x field-ditch (true) |
  |---|---|---|
  | Field ditch (waters one paddy) | ~0.3 m | 1x |
  | Distribution lateral | ~1 m | ~3x |
  | Village creek / brook (natural) | ~2 m | ~6x |
  | Main irrigation canal (*yosui*) | ~5 m | ~15x |
  | Town river | ~20 m | ~65x |
  | Provincial-city moat (Himeji-tier) | ~20-35 m | ~70-115x |
  | Grand-city moat (Osaka-tier) | ~75 m | ~250x |
  | *Tameike* pond (areal - draw as polygon) | ~150-200 m across | ~1.5-2x a paddy |

  Anchors: headwater streams cluster near ~0.32 m; Himeji's moats average ~20 m (max 34.5 m); Osaka's outer moat is ~70-90 m; *tameike* reservoirs run ~3-26 ha (~150-600 m across). A field ditch is **~1/300 of the 1-cho (~100 m) paddy it feeds** - at true scale a hairline, essentially invisible.
  - *The stroke convention here (GM ruling 2026-07-21: watercourses are LINEWORK, so this is the sanctioned minimum-visibility floor, never a size license).* The true linear span is ~250:1 (1700:1 with reservoirs) - undrawable on one sheet without the ditch vanishing (a 1 ft ditch at city scale is 0.33 px). So the thin end draws true-width-or-floored: the field ditch parks at the visibility floor (~2.5 px) rather than its true hairline, while honesty anchors on the **larger** features, which draw true (the city moat is `px(66)` = its real ~66 ft, low end of a provincial-seat moat's 66-115 ft). The *ordering* and coarse steps always survive: **channel/ditch ~2.5 px** (hairline floor), **pond-feeder ~5 px** (a lateral), **village-creek stream ~9 px**, **town-river / moat-feeder stream = the moat width** (conservation of flow - a stream supplying a moat carries its full flow), **city moat px(66)** (heaviest watercourse; a provincial city, not the Osaka grand-moat tier). A pond is **areal** - drawn as a polygon at paddy scale, never on the line-width ladder; its *outlet* is a main canal. The checks pin ordering and the key gaps (ditch hairline; creek >=2.5x the ditch; moat the heaviest watercourse; moat >=4x the ditch) rather than exact pixels, so the tiers can never collapse back into one undifferentiated blue line.
- **Farmers, fields, and the economy of the wall** (drives `town_farmers_plurality`, `all_houses_field_adjacent`, `outside_fields_farmhouse_density` / `city_outside_fields_have_farmhouses`, `city_no_inwall_farms`, `wall_hugs_the_town` / `city_no_large_empty_space`). Farmers are the overwhelming majority caste (the plurality), and they build **close to the fields they work** (farmsteads ring the paddies at roughly village density). A city does NOT farm inside its walls - land within is too valuable and the wall too costly to throw around farmland - **unless** an unusual in-wall water source makes a small agricultural district worthwhile (the documented exception). Walls were expensive, so a rampart **hugs** its built-up area: no large empty swath inside, sized to hold the population densely.
- **Fire towers: the fire-defense of a dense, ENCLOSED wooden core** (drives `walled_town_has_fire_tower` / `city_has_fire_towers`, `fire_tower_in_commoner_quarter`). Premodern Japanese cities burned constantly - Edo was the "city of fires," and the Meireki fire of 1657 killed ~100,000. The institution we depict is the **fire-watch tower** (*hinomi-yagura*, 火の見櫓) - a tall braced-timber tower with an alarm bell (*hanshō*, 半鐘) the watchman struck in a cadence that told the city how *near* a fire was. **Why walled towns and cities but NOT open towns:** the tower belongs to the dense, contiguous, *enclosed* wooden core. Two reinforcing reasons. (1) **Density** - fire is catastrophic only where roofs touch (the packed intramural *machiya*/tenement warren); an open road-town strung along the highway, fields running off every edge, has natural breaks (the road, the field-gaps, the surrounding open ground), so a standing tower does not earn its cost. (2) **The wall traps the fire** - the rampart that keeps raiders out also keeps a blaze *in*, so the watch over the rooftops is most needed exactly where there is a wall. **The unwalled county seat is NOT an exception - it is the confirming case (GM question 2026-07-24, researched; this reverted the 2026-07 audit's brief widening of the town check to unwalled seats).** The real-world analog of an unwalled tax-administration seat is the *jin'ya*/*daikansho* town, and its fire posture was defensive, not observational: a *hanshō* bell on a ladder, rooftop platform, or simple frame at the office or watch post (the local temple bell doubling as alarm elsewhere), night patrols with clappers, stored water (Takayama Jin'ya, the surviving Hida daikansho, preserves its fire-prevention water system), and above all the plastered fireproof *kura* protecting the tax rice and ledgers - losing the tax rice was the career-ending disaster, so the storehouse was fireproofed rather than the horizon watched. The freestanding countryside fire tower everyone pictures is a *Meiji-and-later* institution (early Meiji rural fire watch was still a wooden ladder and a bell; the standalone towers spread end-of-Meiji through Showa with the volunteer *shōbōdan*), so an Edo-flavored unwalled town with a central tower is roughly a century anachronistic. China agrees at both ends: the Song built staffed *wanghuolou* fire towers in hyper-dense Kaifeng and Hangzhou, while ordinary market towns had nothing of the kind (and Chinese county seats were walled anyway). On our maps the unwalled seat's fire story is carried by the kura on the yamen, headman, and merchant houses; a small *hanshō* bell-frame at the magistrate's compound would be the one defensible extra glyph, and plain omission is equally accurate. **L7R/Sengoku adaptation:** the famous chartered townsman brigades (the *machi-bikeshi* "iroha" companies) are a peace-era Tokugawa institution; in a Sengoku-flavored Rokugan the fire-watch is the **magistrate's** (yoriki/doshin/ashigaru manning the tower), not self-governing townsman companies. The tower is drawn as an OPEN braced frame with a red bell (distinct from the solid guard towers ON the rampart - a fire tower is a *civilian interior* structure watching the rooftops, not the wall); it is an overlap-checked struct, so it must stand clear of the wall, roads, buildings, and open water (the pond - Tango's west tower once landed on the pond rim, which is what closed that gap with `no_structure_on_pond`), and `fire_tower_in_commoner_quarter` keeps it in the commoner warren, never the samurai ward. It also keeps **>= 5px of daylight** around its frame (`fire_tower_standoff`): the blanket overlap check only catches true intersection, so a tower butted flush against a house passed it while reading as a collision - the glyph's roof cap overhangs the recorded frame by ~2px a side, and an open braced tower's footing and ladder sit clear of the neighboring eaves (it stands on a seam, not in a party-wall row). The one neighbor neither pass can see is a **wellhead** (`fire_tower_clear_of_wells`): wells are overlap-EXEMPT (a wellhead's nominal footprint may kiss a dense-city building, because houses ring a tenement court closely), so a tower dropped onto a well trips no generic check - but the tower's footing would stand in the well court blocking the quarter's shared draw-point (the idobata), so the tower keeps the same >= 5px of daylight from every well's clearance disc (this caught Tango's west tower standing squarely on a well; the bad manifest is pinned in `pool/regressions/`). **When a settlement rates MORE than one tower, they DISPERSE ACROSS THE QUARTERS** (`fire_towers_dispersed` + `fire_tower_amid_its_district`): historically the fire-watch was parcelled out per neighborhood - each Edo machi block-group kept its own tower, and the shogunate's official watch stations were likewise distributed one to a district - because a tower's useful watch is its visual neighborhood of rooftops. Two rules enforce it. (1) Pairwise floor: two towers inside one watch radius of each other duplicate coverage, so every pair stands at least one watch radius (the same ~230px the commoner-quarter check uses for "near its rooftops") apart. (2) District centering: a comfortable pairwise gap is NOT enough - Tango's original pair stood 341px apart yet both NW of center, leaving the NE laborer warren (the city's biggest rooftop mass) unwatched. So every commoner dwelling (inside the walls, when walled - the extramural gate-market rows are not what the towers exist for) is assigned to its NEAREST tower; that partition is the de facto watch districting the drawn towers imply, and each tower must stand near its district's center of mass (offset <= max(0.9 x district RMS radius, one watch radius)) - AMID the blocks it watches, not at their far edge with a whole unwatched quadrant in its ledger. In practice: put one tower amid each major commoner quarter, sized to the tower count (Tango: NE laborers, west servant/burakumin, south-central merchants -> 3 towers). Place it LAST, after the packs and `farmsteads()`, on a cleared seam the finished map already has. *(Deliberate divergence from literal scale, per the legibility license: a single tower stands in for a quarter's whole fire-watch; real cities had many.)*
- **Why we DON'T depict firebreaks (a deliberate omission, so it is not re-added).** The other historical urban fire-defense was the **firebreak** (*hiyokechi*, 火除地 / *hirokoji*, 広小路) - a cleared strip of open ground a blaze could not jump, which, too valuable to leave idle, filled with removable stalls and theater stages and so doubled as the market/amusement ground (Edo's *hirokoji* became its entertainment districts). We tried two depictions and dropped both. A **drawn** plaza (a shaded clearing placed before the dwelling packs) forces the packer to flow houses around an *invented* clearing, which reads as a designed plaza rather than the *absence* of building a firebreak actually is. A **label-only** marking (a run-aligned annotation dropped onto a gap the finished map happens to leave) is just naming existing dead space - it adds a word, not a feature, and getting the gaps and label orientations to read well takes real effort for no real payoff. So a firebreak is **not a feature of these maps**: the towers carry the fire-defense story on their own. If a future map genuinely needs a firebreak (a wide ceremonial fire-plaza that is itself the subject), build it as a real, deliberately-placed feature with its own glyph - do not resurrect the marked-gap approach.

- **City commoner housing is CONTIGUOUS - the row-packing doctrine** (drives `s.rowpack(...)`, `city_row_housing_touches`, `city_row_housing_gap`; GM-confirmed 2026-07 in the 1px=3ft scale pass).
  - *What the history says.* Urban commoners in both reference cultures did not build detached-with-yard. Machiya street frontage was continuous - party walls or touching eaves, because street frontage was taxed and precious; back-lot *nagaya* were literally ONE ROOF over a row of family units; Chinese county-seat courtyard housing shared rammed-earth or brick party walls in continuous street walls. Detached-with-yard was a samurai (and rural) form.
  - *What we had.* The measured pre-doctrine Tango: 553 row-class dwellings with a median nearest-neighbor gap of 12px (~31 ft at the old grain) and ZERO touching pairs - a suburb, not a city quarter. Pinned as `pool/regressions/city_row_housing_fires_on_scattered_housing.json`.
  - *The drawn fabric.* Rows of touching units (hairline 0.4px seam - drawn as distinct outlines that merge into a terrace strip, which is also what it WAS: independent structures grown together); a ~3-6 ft eave/drainage gap between back-to-back rows (rain dripped between roofs; gutter and night-soil access); an idobata court every couple of rows (the communal well courtyard a tenement block's life turned around); access by roji drawn at the linework floor, not by ambient spacing. Obstacles break a terrace naturally - the occasional gap is historically honest (post-conflagration cities kept a few firebreak gaps), it just isn't the default texture.
  - *Knock-ons.* This packing is WHY the fire furniture exists (packed wooden terraces burned - the fire-watch towers and block wells stop being decorative); and the samurai quarter's detached-compound scatter (`pack`, not `rowpack`) is the deliberate visual contrast - the one district that historically DID build detached.
- **In-wall VEGETABLE tracts (`kind="vegetable"`) vs paddy** (drives the `kind != "paddy"` exemptions in `fields_show_water_source`, `field_ringed`, `city_interior_fields_farmhouse_density`).
  - *Why they exist.* At the declared 3 ft/px city scale, ~600 contiguous households genuinely occupy a small fraction of a ~0.9 mi walled ring - the real walled seat filled that surplus with intensively-worked garden ground (well- and night-soil-fed vegetable plots), which is also Tango's canon (average population, above-average space per capita BECAUSE of the agricultural district).
  - *Why the exemptions.* A paddy must show its water (channel/pond/stream) and its farmstead ring; a city garden tract is watered from wells and worked by the residents of the surrounding quarters - there is no separate farm household to draw and no surface channel to demand. East of the Imperial road a channel CANNOT reach anyway (it would have to cross the road).
  - *Bed SIZE - intensive, the SMALLEST dry parcels* (`vegetable_beds_are_intensive`, veg-bed area <= 0.15 real acres; fixture `vegetable_beds_intensive_fires_on_tangos_fat_veg_slabs`). A kitchen/vegetable garden is hand-worked ground; its beds are ~55 ft, distinctly smaller than a grain-field hem strip (~90-100 ft) - the veg tract should be the map's smallest dry parcels, not its largest. Tile the tract width into `round(width / 55 ft)` EVEN columns with only small jitter; a single uniform cut leaves one fat ~150 ft column (0.24-acre slabs, the biggest dry parcels on Tango - backwards for a garden; GM 2026-07-22). The check keys on the VEG_CROPS labels (daikon/greens/onions/beans), distinct from grain DRY_CROPS (barley/millet/buckwheat/soy), so it fires only where a veg tract exists and never touches village grain hems.
- **The scale ladder itself** (drives `meta(ftpx=...)`, `s.px()/s.lw()`, the 4px linework floor, and the ftpx-aware check thresholds - `on_a_street` 85 real ft, the city theater's 185 real ft).
  - Hamlet/town 1 ft/px, village 2, provincial city 3 (GM decision, 2026-07): round numbers a human can read distances from. The 2026-07 audit that drove it: dwellings were already mutually consistent in real feet across every map (the bscale grain WAS the scale change), but town fields ran 4-8x under real area, and fixed-pixel constants (roads, streets, inn, stables, kura) silently changed real size between tiers. Constants are now declared in real feet and converted per map; thin linework floors at 4px (a 5 ft roji at 3 ft/px would be an invisible 1.7px) - true-width-or-floored, never inflated past the floor.
- **Label doctrine: empty ground wins** (drives the ministry auto label side, the deferred road label, `s._label_hits`; GM, 2026-07).
  - *The rule.* If a label can sit somewhere that still clearly labels its subject but covers NOTHING else, it must; when every candidate covers something, take the least. The Ministry of War label sitting over samurai houses when the ground below the office was bare, and the "Imperial Road" label over a farmstead's trees when the road's other flank was empty, were the motivating defects.
  - *Generator, not checker, does the work* (keeps the render cheap - the GM's explicit constraint): `s._label_hits(x, y, text, size)` counts already-placed footprints (placed rects + homestead grove rects) under a candidate label box - a few thousand AABB compares. Ministries score above vs below and take the clearer (`label_below=None` = auto; pass True/False to force). The ROAD label is **deferred to `finish()`** - at road-draw time the map is still empty, so scoring would see nothing - where the given anchor competes against its mirror across the roadline.
  - *Checks stay narrow.* No new label-vs-everything gate (expensive, brittle across nine maps); the existing `labels_clear_of_other_buildings`/`no_label_overlaps` remain the backstop.
- **Gate furniture never overlaps; watch-towers keep off the graves** (`city_gate_guard_inspection_separate`, `fire_tower_clear_of_graveyards`; GM, 2026-07). The guard house and inspection station walk fixed arcs (80/144) along the wall from the gate - on a small tight ring (Tango's 480x450 shrink) the arcs converge and the annex was drawn through its guard house, so the generator now walks the inspection OUTWARD (+22px steps) until the two rects clear, and the check pins it. A fire-watch tower's braced footing gets the same 5px daylight from a graveyard as from wells and buildings. Both captured red-first in `pool/regressions/gate_furniture_overlap_and_tower_on_graveyard_tango.json` per the regression-corpus discipline.
- **The kido aligns with its fence; the guard box stands ward-side** (`kido_aligned_with_ward_fence`; GM 2026-07-24). The kido is a gap IN the ward fence: its roofed bar spans that gap, so its long axis follows the LOCAL FENCE TANGENT - a 30-degree fence run gets a 30-degree gate, never an axis-aligned stamp (same doctrine as `city_wall_towers_aligned` for rampart towers). Both cities carried the live defect: Nagahara's SW ring-road kido sat axis-aligned on its ~159-degree fence run (and its top-fence kido was a full 90 degrees off - a wrong legacy orientation flag the tangent rule now makes impossible), Tango's S ring-road kido likewise on the ~44-degree SW jog; both pre-fix manifests are frozen in `pool/regressions/`. `s.ward` computes each gate's rotation from the boundary polyline at its seat and hangs the guard box on the WARD-INTERIOR flank - the gate watch belongs to the ward it seals, so the neighborhood guard house rotates and sides with its fence too (the old fixed-per-orientation flanks put Nagahara's E-fence boxes on the street side). Gates in `s.ward(...)` are plain (x, y) seats now (a legacy horizontal flag is accepted and ignored); `s.kido(rot=, guard_side=)` covers hand placement. The check pins the recorded `rot` (legacy manifests fall back to the horizontal flag) to the nearest fence segment's tangent within 7 degrees mod 180; a kido more than ~16px from every fence is exempt (nothing to align to). Kido reservations (`KIDO_SPOTS` block_polys) should be SYMMETRIC squares - which flank the box lands on now depends on fence direction, so one-sided rects tuned to the legacy flanks no longer bound the glyph.

- **River cities (`s.river`, the `Nagahara` worked example)** - drives `s.river`, `s.moat(river=...)`, `s.canal`/`s.dock`/`s.jetty`/`s.water_gate`, the multi-road `M["roads"]`, and the river-aware check variants (`moat_is_heaviest_watercourse`, `city_moat_surrounds_wall` / `city_moat_joins_river`, `city_roads_run_offmap`, `city_canal_reaches_dock`, `city_wharf_jetties_on_bank`).
  - *The history (China first, Japan agreeing).* MOST provincial cities sit on a river (Tango, on an off-map stream, is the exception). The trunk river NEVER runs *through* the walls - the one great city that let a river in, Kaifeng, silted its bed meters above the plain and was devastated by floods seven times (the 1642 flood alone killed ~300k of 378k). Instead the city stands ON one bank: the RIVER is the water defense on its flank, and the dug MOAT covers the three landward faces, tapping the river upstream and returning downstream so the current flushes it (Xiangyang's Han-river face + dug moat; Pingyao on the Fen; Okayama's Asahi "natural moat"; Shanghai's county-seat moat "connected to the Huangpu"). The ONE way water legitimately enters the walls is a **water gate** (*shuimen*) admitting a navigable CANAL, not the river - Suzhou's Pan Gate, a paired land-and-water gate with a sluiced arch, feeding the city's inner canals + a dock basin. The riverfront **wharf** (jetties, warehouses, market) sits OUTSIDE the water-side gate, the river-city version of the gate market (*guan-xiang*).
  - *One mouth on the river, not two (GM 2026-07-23, spotting Nagahara's water-gate corner).* Pre-fix, the cargo canal opened its OWN river tap 36 real ft beside the moat's downstream junction and rode collinearly inside the moat arm's stroke for the whole bank crossing - rendering as a smeared doubled channel with a sliver fork at the mouth. The historically grounded fix MERGES the mouths rather than separating them: in the Suzhou pattern the city's canals communicate with the MOAT, and the moat's downstream river junction is the single navigation entrance (separating them would also be geometrically worse here - a distinct mouth further downstream would have to cross the moat's southern arc at grade). So Nagahara's canal now ends ON the moat's outfall-arm corner (`CANAL[0] = MOAT[1]`, self-correcting if the wall resizes) and boats run river -> moat arm -> water gate -> dock basin. `city_canal_reaches_dock` accepts the moat handoff, and `city_canal_shares_moat_mouth` pins the defect (a canal end tapping the river directly must not sit inside the moat's stroke corridor - a mouth on open bank away from the moat stays legitimate, real cities had water gates on the river face itself); the pre-fix manifest is frozen in `pool/regressions/city_canal_shares_moat_mouth_fires_on_nagaharas_second_river_mouth.json`.
  - *One finding assessed and ACCEPTED as-drawn (2026-07-23 hydrology review - recorded so nobody "fixes" it later).* The two junctions sit only ~800 real ft apart along the river, so the driving head is tiny and the far side of the ring would circulate sluggishly - true of every real example of the pattern (river moats were kept full by the river's stage more than they were flushed; partial stagnation is period-accurate, not a defect). (A second finding from the same review - the blunt perpendicular T-junctions, initially accepted as attested - was SUPERSEDED the next day when the GM pushed on it: see the junction-angles bullet below.)
  - *Junction angles follow the current (GM 2026-07-24, overturning the initial accepted-as-drawn call).* The GM noticed both junction arms were EXACTLY parallel square tees and asked whether they shouldn't angle with the flow. They were parallel only because `s.moat(river=)` projected each open end onto the river by perpendicular foot - an algorithm artifact, never a decision. The research verdict split by end, and the ASYMMETRY is itself the historical signature:
    - **Outlet: the GM's intuition is standard practice.** Junction angle is a primary control on confluence flow - small downstream-pointing angles merge smoothly, large angles drive the entering jet across the receiving channel (stagnation, separation, far-bank attack). Natural tributaries curve to join pointing downstream; engineered drainage returns do the same. The outlet now sweeps ~22 degrees off square, downstream.
    - **Inlet: gentler-in-theory, but sediment pushes back.** A flow-aligned intake is the smooth-entry answer, BUT an offtake swallows a disproportionate share of the river's bedload - the more smoothly aligned, the more it drinks, and a silted moat is a maintenance failure. Classical headworks therefore kept the offtake itself near-square (control by sluice, siting on the outer bank, skimming the cleaner upper water); a moat also wants STAGE (staying full), not throughput, so it never needs a momentum-capturing aligned mouth. The inlet now tilts only ~10 degrees upstream-of-square.
    - *Engine + convention.* `s.moat(..., river_inlet_tilt=10.0, river_outlet_tilt=22.0)` - each junction foot shifts along the river by arm-length x tan(tilt), so the arm sits exactly its tilt off square; the upstream end is the inlet. **`river` pts run UPSTREAM-FIRST** (source before mouth) - both the tilt and the check key on this order; Nagahara's Hayakawa (N -> S) complies.
    - *The check.* `city_moat_junction_angles`: the outlet's junction must sit >= 12px downstream of its arm's square foot, the inlet's <= 4px (square or upstream-tilted, never swept downstream). Pre-tilt Nagahara frozen in `pool/regressions/city_moat_junction_angles_fires_on_nagaharas_square_tees.json`.
  - *The engine.* `s.river(pts, width=)` draws the trunk waterway (a wide off-map-to-off-map stream) and records `M["river"]` so the checks know which stream legitimately outweighs the dug moat. `s.moat(ring, gap=, river=<pts>, river_cut=, river_inlet_tilt=, river_outlet_tilt=)` builds an OPEN moat arc: the vertices within `river_cut` of the river are dropped and both open ends extend onto the river, which closes the water ring itself - the junction feet tilted with the current (see the junction-angles bullet). `s.canal(pts, width=)` is a mid-tier watercourse (heavier than a hairline ditch, lighter than the moat); `s.water_gate(x, y, rot=)` is the grated shuimen arch (the wall is drawn with a matching gap via `city_wall(water_gates=[...])`, and mural towers skip its vertex); `s.dock(cx, cy, w, h)` is the in-city basin at the canal head; `s.jetty(x, y, rot=, length=)` is a planked finger from the bank. `s.road` now appends to `M["roads"]` (a city with no Imperial spine has several roads, none the "Imperial road").
  - *The checks.* A river city declares `meta(imperial_road=False)` (its Imperial highway passes off-map) - then `city_imperial_road_through` is waived and `city_roads_run_offmap` instead requires the road net to leave the map in >= 2 directions. `city_moat_surrounds_wall` gains a river-arc variant (every wall vertex behind the moat OR the river) plus `city_moat_joins_river` (both open moat ends meet the river) and `city_moat_junction_angles` (outlet swept downstream, inlet square-to-upstream - the junction-angles bullet). `moat_is_heaviest_watercourse` excludes the river's own record from the comparison (the river IS the heavier defense). `no_structure_on_canal` keeps buildings out of the canal water; `city_canal_reaches_dock` requires the canal to connect the dock to the water - the river directly OR a handoff onto the moat (like a street reaching the road, no visible gap); `city_canal_shares_moat_mouth` forbids a canal opening its own river mouth inside the moat's junction corridor (the shared-mouth doctrine above); `city_wharf_jetties_on_bank` requires each jetty's root at the near bank and its tip in the near-half water (a finger, not a bar spanning the river). The **water set-back** and `city_moat_fed_offmap` work unchanged (the river is a wide stream). **THE DEAD CROSS THE RIVER**: the moat's water set-back leaves no dry landward fringe, so the funerary complex sits on the far bank, downstream, clear of the bridge road.
  - *Gateposts are tangent-aware* (`city_wall`): each gate's two posts stand to either side of the opening ORIENTED to the wall's LOCAL tangent - so an E/W gate's posts stand N and S of it (the old code hard-coded N/S vertical posts, which floated parallel to an E/W wall). Recorded as `gate_structs` kind `"gatepost"`.
  - *Samurai estates face OTOSAN UCHI* (`city_estates_toward_capital`, replacing the hard-coded `city_estates_in_southeast`): a samurai builds his country seat on the capital-facing side, so the direction is per-city - `meta(capital_dir=<cardinal>)` (Tango SE, Nagahara NE). And `city_estates_clear_of_roads` keeps an estate off the highway out of the city.
  - *City temples honor the clan's TWO PATRON FORTUNES* (`city_temples_dedicated`, extending the town `CLAN_FORTUNES` rule to cities - l7r.md: Hantei X codified that every city holds a temple to each of its clan's patron fortunes). Declare `meta(clan="Crab")`; the labeled (major) temples must be the two patrons (+ optionally Bishamon, the warrior fortune of any samurai quarter), the rest small wayside shrines. Nagahara (Crab) = Bishamon + Ebisu; a great Temple of Suitengu (the river fortune, thematic but not a patron) is demoted to a small shrine. Tango declares `meta(clan="Crane")` = Benten + Daikoku.
  - *Funerary grounds stay off the fields* (`funerary_clear_of_fields`, `structures_clear_of_dry_plots` extended to funerary): a burial / cremation ground sits BESIDE the farmland, never on a paddy body or its ditches.
  - *Render / layout order (GM doctrine).* Lay the immovable infrastructure FIRST so everything flows around it and overlaps become impossible: **river -> walls (+ moat) -> roads/streets -> ring road -> guard towers -> gates + gate furniture -> canal / water gate / dock -> civic compounds (temples, ministries, yamen, theater, flophouses - each reserving a no-build block) -> the dense packs -> farmsteads / wells / fire towers LAST**. A feature placed after the packs (a fire tower, a hand-placed merchant house) must reserve its ground up front (`block_polys`) or it lands on a packed dwelling.
  - *Nagahara is DONE (v2, population 3,000).* The river-hemmed ring is sized `RX,RY = 494,460` (no agricultural district) and passes the full validator with zero mechanical failures, 560 dwellings in band. Getting there is what drove the **wall-sizing process** below - the first-cut wall (430x400) was too small for 3,000, and rather than grind placements by trial and error we MEASURED and resized the wall once.

### Sizing the wall to the population (`city_capacity`, `--capacity`, `--capacity-map`)

The hard question every city raises is *"do the walls enclose too much empty space or too little for the target population?"* Guessing a wall size and then grinding placements to fit is backwards. Instead **measure** with the space-budget analysis in `check_village.py`:

- **`city_capacity(M)`** grid-samples the walled interior and classes every cell as **dwelling / civic-overhead / water / trunk-road / residential-street / field / OPEN**. From that it reports: `target_dwellings` (population / 5), `placed` (dwellings actually drawn), **`inherent_capacity`** (dwellings the residential-capable ground CAN hold at the canonical well-packed density `RHO_CANONICAL = 0.00149` dwellings/px^2 - Tango's 561 placed over its ~378k px^2 of non-reserve residential ground; feature 009 recalibrated this from the original 0.00127, which had divided by ground that still contained the agri reserve's ~72k of non-field slack and so under-read what packed urban ground delivers), the area budget, `open_frac`, and a **`suggested_wall_scale`** (>1 enlarge, <1 shrink) that resizes the ring ONCE to the right size. Reserve ground of ANY kind (agri included) is deducted from the residential-capable ground - it is committed to non-housing and must never dilute the density norm.
- **The verdict** is one of four, and each says exactly what to do next:
  - **`too_small`** - even well-packed the wall cannot hold the target (`inherent_cap < 0.9*T`). ENLARGE the wall (multiply all coordinates by `suggested_wall_scale` about the wall center).
  - **`too_big`** - far more room than the target needs (`inherent_cap > 1.4*T`). SHRINK it.
  - **`underpacked`** - the WALL is right but the placement is too sparse (`placed < (1 - population_tol)*T`, i.e. below the *same* line the `population_consistent_with_housing` check fails at, default 7%). Do NOT resize - add dwellings / densify the quarters.
  - **`about_right`** - wall sized and packed to target. Done.
- **`city_wall_sized_to_population`** is the gate check that fails on `too_small` / `too_big`, so a mis-sized wall is a first-class, automated finding rather than something discovered by hand late.
- **`--capacity-map` prints an ASCII map of the interior classification** (each cell 40px), so the report shows WHERE the open ground is, not just how much. Read it to decide *where* to add a quarter (or that the open ground is all civic precinct / setback and the answer is denser packing, not a new quarter).

**The Nagahara worked example (the wall-sizing process in action, 2026-07):** first cut at 430x400 declared 3,000 -> `enlarge` (inherent 446 < 600), suggested scale x1.13. Scaled the whole generator x1.15 about the center -> `densify` (wall now right, placement sparse). The `--capacity-map` showed the open ground was the civic-committed west half (temple neighborhood NW, sealed government ward SW). The first pass densified the east; feature 006 later showed the honest fix was to fill the empty NW itself (the monzen pocket) so the quarter is not merely under-built - see the per-quarter doctrine below. End state: `sized_and_packed`.

**Why `RHO_CANONICAL` is honest but the aggregate model over-read:** the density is calibrated on Tango (a fully-packed city), so `inherent_capacity` is what a *well-packed* quarter delivers. The ORIGINAL `city_capacity` was a global aggregate (total residential-capable area x that density vs total placed), which is blind to DISTRIBUTION - a dense east plus an empty west averages to "fine." It passed a visibly-broken Nagahara (dense east, empty NW temple quarter, ~35 commoner dwellings spilled outside the walls). Feature 006 (below) replaced the aggregate with a per-quarter model.

### Quarters and per-quarter density (feature 006)

Density is a SPATIAL property, so it is judged PER QUARTER, not city-wide. The measured proof: Tango (good) and the broken Nagahara had the SAME in-wall dwelling count (521 vs 525) and the SAME median block density (4.6/10k px^2) - an aggregate or anonymous-grid check literally cannot tell them apart. The only difference was WHERE the empty ground sat. So the generator DECLARES its intent and the check reads it.

- **The generator declares quarters.** `s.quarter(poly, zone, kind=None)` records a region + `zone` in {`residential`, `civic`, `mixed`, `reserve`} into `M["quarters"]`; a `reserve` also carries a `kind` (`drill_ground` / `garden` / `agricultural_district`). A `drill_ground` (bare earth + dashed muster perimeter) and a `garden` (green sward) are DRAWN so their open ground reads as deliberate, not accidental; an **`agricultural_district` draws NOTHING of its own** (GM 2026-07-22) - its combs, in-wall farmhouses, and label already carry the visual, and the faint dashed boundary it used to add read as a stray dotted line cutting through the farmhouses and across the Imperial road above the burakumin neighborhood. The quarter is still recorded in `M["quarters"]` regardless, so per-quarter density judging is unaffected. A walled city tiles its interior with wedges split at the crossroads (road x=CX, axis y=CY); Tango and Nagahara both do this.
- **The checks (all walled-city-scoped):**
  - `city_quarters_declared` / `city_quarters_tile_interior` - a walled city MUST declare quarters that cover the interior (>=85%) without overlap (<=5%) or spill outside the wall (<=3%).
  - `city_residential_quarters_dense_enough` - every `residential`/`mixed` quarter's average density (over NON-civic ground: quarter area minus the civic compound footprints inside it, so a government ward's yamen does not read as under-built) must sit in `[QUARTER_DENSITY_FLOOR, QUARTER_DENSITY_CEIL]` = `[0.30, 2.30]/1000px^2`. A `residential` quarter additionally must hold no DEAD ZONE - `largest_empty_gap` (the biggest empty pocket radius, counting wells and civic compounds as served) must stay under `DEAD_ZONE_MAX` = 150px, so a half-dense/half-empty quarter fails even with an in-band average.
